[DA 7]

<gai>Ma gheibh sinn ùine ri fhaicinn,
Bheir sinn fùcadh mu seach air a’ chlò.<eng>

If we get time to see it,
We will waulk the cloth in turn.

DONALD MACLEOD
<gai>Orain Nuadh Ghaeleach, p. 229 (1811)


’S cha b’e am biadh dhaibh breacag thana,
Ach bacastair a’ bruich an arain,
’S fuaim na beòire dol ’sa channa
Fuaim an fhìona dol ’sa ghlainidh,
’S uisge beatha na treas tarraing,
’S branndaidh chruaidh a nuas a Gallaibh.<eng>

And their food would not be a thin scone,
But bread baked by bakers,
The sound of beer being poured into tankards,
The sound of wine being poured into glasses,
And thrice distilled whisky,
And strong brandy from abroad.

Entertainment in great Highland houses, as described in old waulking songs.

Latha bha ’n Ridire ag òl

(a) Anna Raghnaill Eachainn a’ seinn

Latha bha ’n Ridire ag òl,
Hó ró, hùg a hug a,
’San taigh-òsd’, e fhéin ’s a bhean,
Hug a bhi a, seinn dug ho ro,
’San taigh-òsd’, e fhéin ’s a bhean,
Hó ró, etc.
Ridire gun gheàrr e mheur,
Hug a bhi a, etc.
Gus na rànaig e ’n cnàimh glas;

Dh’fhalbh an fhuil ’na struth gu làr						5
Dh’fhalbh an anail ’na ceò as—
Fios air a chàirdean gu léir!
Fios air a chinne gu luath!
Gus an corp a chur gu cill,
Gus a chur fo’n ùir gu grad!							10

Latha chaidh Fionn dha’n bheinn t-seilg,
Thachair ris colann gun cheann—
Dh’éirich Conan, dh’éirich Conn,
Dh’éirich friogh is fraoch air Bran,
Thug iad gu bodhradh mo chinn.						15

’S ann agam fhìn a bha ’n long
B’fheàrr a chuireadh tonn ’na déidh,
’S ann agam fhìn a bha ’n cù
B’fheàrr a chuir a shùil ri seilg,
’S ann agam fhìn a bha ’n t-each						20
B’fheàrr a mharcraicheadh le sréin,
’S ann agam fhìn a bha bhean
B’fheàrr a leanadh ri fear fhéin.

XC

Shuidh mi air cnocan an tiùrr

Bean Nìll a’ seinn


E ho o hao rì o ho,
E ho o hao rì o ho.
Shuidh mi air cnocan an tiùrr,
E ho o hao ri rì a hóg u,
E ho o hao ri iù ra bhó,
Shuidh mi air cnocan an tiùrr,
E ho o hao rì o ho,
Thog mi teine, rinn mi smùid,
E ho o hao ri rì a hóg u,
E ho o hao ri iù ra bhó,
Thog mi teine, rinn mi smùid,
E ho o hao rì o ho,
Thug mi seall’ air taobh mo chùil,
E ho o hao ri rì, etc.
Thug mi seall’ air taobh mo chùil,
E ho o hao rì, etc.

[TD 46]

Chunna mi tighinn an triùir,							55
Fear is gille, gunna ’s cù,
As deoghaidh sin, luchd nan cùl,
Luchd nan leadan ’s nan cùl fionn,
Dh’òladh an deoch, cha b’e ’m bùrn,
Ach fìon dearg a thig o’n bhùth.						60
E ho o hao rì o ho,
E ho o hao rì o ho.

Shuidh mi air cnoc àrd na buaileadh,
Thug mi seall’ air taobh mo ghuailleadh,
Chunna mi tighinn na h-uaislean,
Coltas Raghnaill òig is Ruairi,
’S Alasdair buidhe na gruaigeadh;						65
Nam bu chuimhneach leat an uair sin
Nuair a bha mi ’s tu ’n Gleann Cuaicheadh,
Rìgh! cha bhagradh tu mo bhualadh,
Cha bhagradh, ’s gum b’fhada bhuat e.
E ho o hao rì o ho.
E ho o hao rì o ho.

Fhir a shiùbhlas ro’ na gleannan,						70
Cha chuir mise trom ort eallach,
Ach a ghiùlain mo chuid bheannachd
Dh’fhios a’ bhuill am bheil mo leannan,
’S innis dha gu bheil mi fallain,
Mar a chuir mi ’n geamhradh fairis,						75
’S gun do thiarainn mi bho’n earrach,
Ràna mi samhradh a’ bhainne,
Foghmhar chur sguab air na bannan.

E ho o hao rì o ho.

XCIII

Thug mi ’n oidhche, ge b’fhad’ i

(a) Ruairi Iain Bhàin a’ seinn

Thug mi ’n oidhche, ge b’fhad’ i,						120
Ó, hu a leò ho ro ho,
Chaidh an cadal a dhìth orm,
Hò ro hill a rinn à,
Hò ro hù ri rinn à,
Ho ró hu a leò ro ho.
Chaidh an cadal a dhìth orm,
Ó, hu a leò ho ro ho,
’S mi ri amharc nam bràithrean,
Hò ro hill, etc.
’S mi ri coimhead nam bràithrean,
Ó, hu a leò, etc.

[TD 53]

[Beurla]

[TD 54]

Bh’anns a’ phàirc an taobh shìos dhiom;
Gum bu sin na fir fhurail,
Fhuair an urram ’s a b’fhiach e,						125
’S math ur gnothach ’sa Ghearmailt,
Gur neo-chearbach ur gnìomh ann;
Thig an claidheamh siar faraibh,
Nial fala gu’n dias air;
Thig an clogada cruadhach							130
Air gruaig nan clann snìomhainn.

Ach, òganaich laghaich,
Sguir dha m’ thadhal ’s mi suarach,
’S mi gun dugadh ort comhairl’,
Nan gabhadh tu bhuam i—							135
Gun bhith trom air a’ chadal,
Mun déid t’aigne air bruaillein,
Na bi ursainn gu h-ursainn
Le gugach na truaighe.


(b) An t-òran mar a tha e aig Nan Eachainn Fhionnlaigh, air a recordadh le Dòmhnall Mac a’ Phearsain

ISE:
‘Ach, òganaich laghaich,							140
Ó hó, hill eó ho ró,
Bhios a’ tadhal na buaileadh,
Hò ro hill a rinn à,
Hò ro hù ri linn à,
Ho ró hù ill eó ho ró.
Cha mhath liom do shùgradh,
’S cha mhùth’ ’s mi ’n gruaim riut,
Ach thu bhith as mo dheoghaidh,
Gu cladhach na h-uaghach.’							145

ESAN:
‘Éisd, a bhean, tha thu gòrach,
Tha thu ’n còmhnaidh ’ga luadh rium;
Ged a bhiodh tu bhuam torrach,
B’e siod solar a b’uaisle;

XCVIII

Lìon mulad, lìon mulad

Lìon mulad, lìon mulad,
Lìon mulad ’nad dhéidh mi;							280
Cha dìrich mi ’m bruthach,
Cha siubhail mi eutrom;

Cha lot mise feur leat;
Chiad leannan a bh’agam,							285
Tha e bagradh mo thréigsinn,

Cum gu dlùth agad fhéin i,							290

’S don’ an t-earras le chéil’ iad!

’S a dh’fhuaighleadh do léine,						300
’S nì ’n todhar ’sa chladach,
’S nì ’n fheannag ma’s fheudar.

XCIX

Gura mis’ tha gu tinn

(a) Ealasaid Iain Dhunnchaidh a’ seinn

Ó ho hì hiù ra bhó,

Ó ho hì, hiù ra bhó,

Cha n-e goirtead mo chinn,
Ó ho hì, etc.
Ach na chuir mi ’n a’ chill							305
’S ann dhiu Iain is Niall

CI

Mhurchaidh bhig, a chinn a’ chonais

Ann an là’ill Pàdraig earraich.

Ann an là’ill, etc.
’S ann air Coinneach tha ghruag dhuilleach,

Mu’n fhiùran fhoghainneach àlainn,

CIII

Ciad soiridh bhuam dhachaigh

Màiri a’ Ghobha a’ seinn


Ciad soiridh bhuam dhachaigh
thar monadh is mòinteach,
Chalainn éile, hùg i, hùg i, hoireann o ho,

far ’n goir na h-eòin bhòidheach,						490

CVI

Thogainn fonn gun bhith trom

Ceit nighean Dhunnchaidh a’ seinn


Thogainn fonn gun bhith trom
Air an nighinn duinn a bha ’n Cataibh.

Latha dhomh ’s mi falbh an fhraoich,
Fhuair mi ’n fhaodail bha taitneach.						670

Fhuair mi ’n òg-ghruagach dhonn
Air an tom ’s i buain dearcag.

Lùib mi i ’nam bhreacan fhéin,
Liom a b’éibhinn mar thachair.

[TD 109]

[Beurla]

[TD 110]

Biodh sneachd ann ’s gaoth a tuath,						675
Phaisginn suas i ’nam achlais.

Bha thu foghainneach air blàr,
Fiamh a’ ghàir’ ort ri t’fhaicinn.

Beul gun sgòd nach labhradh foill,
Gruaidh mar ros as glan laiseadh.						680

Sùil mheallach fo’n mhalaidh chaoil,
Gruaidh nach caochail am maise.

’S nam biodh fios agam a raoir
Gur h-e ’n fhoill bha ’nad bheachdsa,

Bhithinn còmhla riut ’sa choill,						685
Bheirinn roinn dhut dha m’ leabaidh.

Bhithinn sìnte ri do thaobh,
Chuirinn aotrom do chadal.

Ach nam bithinn-s’ air an fhéill,
’S e do bhréid thigeadh dhachaigh.						690

Riobain ùr tana dlùth,
Ged bhiodh crùn anns an t-slait dhi.

Théid mi sìos, théid mu suas,
Bheir mi ruaig do Loch Abair.

Bheir mi liom air mo chuairt							695
Tochradh gruagaich a Cataibh.

Chuirinn taod ri mart bradach.

’S ged ’iodh Rothaich air a shàil,
Bheirinn tàin a chrodh breac liom.						700

’S nam bithinn ’nam ghruagaich òig,
Dheanainn pòsadh gun fhadal.

[Beurla]

’S liom an t-each a bh’air an t-sliabh						705

CX

Dh’fhalbh mo rùn ’s dh’fhàg e ’n caladh

(a) Fanaidh nic Dhunnchaidh Ruaidh a’ seinn

Dh’fhalbh mo rùn ’s dh’fhàg e ’n caladh,
Dh’fhalbh mo rùn, o hì ù,
’S thog e shiùil ris na crannaibh,
Dh’fhalbh mo rùn ’s dh’fhàg e ’n caladh.

(1)

(2)
Dh’fhalbh mo leannan bhuam a Lìte
Mo thoil-inntinn aoibhinn fallain …


(b) Mar a tha e aig an Urramach Uilleam Mac Mhathain

Dh’fhalbh mo rùn bho na chala;
Dh’fhalbh mo rùn, hó i ù,
’S thog e siùil ris na crannaibh;
Dh’fhalbh mo rùn bho na chala.

(1)
Dh’fhalbh mo leannan o chionn bliadhna
Air bàta dubh dìonach daraich.

(2)
Dh’fhalbh mo leannan uam a dh’Éirinn,					845
’S as a dhéidh cha bhi mi fallain.

(3)

’S rinn an seòladair mo mhealladh.

(4)

’S cha d’fheòraich thu mi gu d’ bhanais.					850

(5)
Chuala mi gun d’fhuair thu céile
Thig fo bhréid a thìr na gainimh.

(6)
Ach ma fhuair, gu meal thu fhéin i,
’S gun thu dhol an éiginn mara.

CXXV

(4)
Cha déid mi do shaor nan cuman,
Cha taobh mi truimeir na clàrsaich.

(5)
Cha déid mi do shaor na locrach,						1515
Gur neo-shocair nì e m’fhàgail.

(6)
Cha déid mi a ghille na fidhle,
Feumaidh e ’n teud bhinn a chàradh.

(7)
Cha déid mi idir dha’n phìobaire,
Bidh e cur a bhrìgh ’sa mhàla.						1520

(8)
Cha déid mi thàilleir nan cleasan,
’S bochd an teisteach liom an t-snàthad.

(9)
Cha déid mi idir dha’n ghriasaich’,
Bidh na briagan a’ comh-fhàs ris.

(10)
Cha déid mi idir dha’n t-soighdeir,						1525
Bidh e dol le mhusgaid ’s gach àite.

(11)
Cha déid mi mhuinnleir an t-sadaich,
Cha lig e liom cadal sàmhach.

(12)
Cha déid mi do dh’fhear am bliadhna
Bhios ’gam iarraidh comh’ ri mhàthair.					1530

(13)
’S mór gum b’annsa liom ri m’ ghualainn
Tuathanach, ’s e ’s buaine làithean.

[TD 193]

[Beurla]

[TD 194]

CXXVI

Chaidh mo lothag air chall

(a) Anna Aonghais Chaluim a’ seinn

Chaidh mo lothag air chall,
Ó hao, gur h-oil leam,
Chaidh mo lothag air chall.

(1)
Chaidh mo lothag dha’n mhuileann,
’S i gun duine ’na ceann.

(2)
Chaidh mo lothag dha’n fhéith						1535
’S mór am beud dhi dhol ann.

(3)
Chaidh mo lothag dha’n chladach,
’S cha robh tac’ aice ann.

(4)
’S tu gun treobhadh an t-iomair’
As do dheoghaidh le crann.							1540

(5)
’S tu gun treobhadh an tiadhan,
Ged ’s e riasg a bhiodh ann.

(6)
Dh’fhalbh mo lothag dhonn ghaolach
Do’n aonach ud thall.

(7)
Chaidh mo lothag dha’n fhireach						1545
’S i fo shileadh nan allt.

(8)
Dh’fhalbh mo lothag a dh’Éirinn
Le each réidh nan rosg mall.

[TD 195]

[Beurla]

[TD 196]

(b) Màiri Eóghainn Mhóir a’ seinn

Chaidh mo lothag air chall,
Ó ho hao, gur h-oil liom,
Chaidh mo lothag air chall.

(1)
Chaidh mo lothag dha’n fhéithe,
Gura beud dhi dhol ann.							1550

(2)
Chaidh mo lothag ’n a’ mhuilinn,
’S i gun duine ’na ceann.

(3)
Chaidh mo lothag dhonn ghaolach
Bhuam dha’n aonach ud thall.

(4)
Chaidh mo lothag dha’n fhireach,						1555
’S i fo shileadh nan allt.

(5)
’S i gun deanadh a fodradh
Air dosraich nan allt.

(6)
’S i gun treobhadh am fiadhaire,
Ge bè riasg a bhiodh ann.							1560

(7)
Chaidh mo lothag a dh’Éirinn,
Le fear réidh nan rosg mall.

(8)
’S i gun icheadh a biadh ann,
Nuair bhiodh i cliathadh nam beann.

(9)
Tha i ullamh ealamh biadhta,							1565
Tha i ciallach ri clann.

[TD 198]

CXXVII

Cha n-eil mi gun mhulad orm

Anna Nic Iain a’ seinn


Air fà lì leó,
Hó ro ghealladh éileadh,
Air fà lì leó.

Cha n-eil mi gun mhulad orm,
Air fà, etc.

Ch-uile h-aon a’ pòsadh,
’S tha mise gun phòsadh fhathast;
Seann-duine cha ghabh mi idir,						1570
Bidh e fada ’g éirigh,
Fada dol ’na éideadh,
Fada cur a churraic air,
Cha déid e mach gun am bata,
Cha dig e steach gun an ad air,						1575
Saoilidh e gur caoirich creagan,
Saoilidh e gur geòidh na cearcan,
Saoilidh e gur grian a’ ghealach,
Saoilidh e gur siucair sneachda,
Saoilidh e gur làir an searrach,						1580
Saoilidh e gur luch an lacha!
B’annsa balach sgiobalta
A thogadh mi ’s a leagadh mi,
’S a bheireadh mi a féithe.

[TD 199]

[Beurla]

[TD 200]

CXXVIII

Cà na dh’fhàg thu m’fhichead gini?

Bean Phluim a’ seinn


Hao ri rì, hó hill eó
Hóg i hó, mo ghaol a’ chruinneag,
Hao ri rì, hó hill eó.

Thànaig fear an aodainn bhàin rium,						1585
‘Cà na dh’fhàg thu m’fhichead gini?’

‘Chuir mi not dheth anns a’ bhàta
’Gam thoirt sàbhailt’ ro’ Chaol Muile;

‘Cheannaich mi each, cha b’e ’n diù e,
Dha ’n t-seann-trùpa bh’aig Rìgh Uilleam.					1590

‘Cóig puinnd Shasannach air dìollaid,
Bha i bliadhn’ na shuidh’ aig uinneig.

‘’S dh’fhàg mi ’n t-each ag iche tràthach
As a’ stàbla ’n Cille Cuimein.

‘Cheannaich mi dhut gùn dha’n t-sìoda,					1595
’S prìneachan gus a chumail.

‘Cheannaich mi dhut brògan àrda,
Gus do shàbhaladh bho chunnart.

‘’S cheannaich mi bat’, spuir, is brògan—
Cha robh gròt aig Dòmhnall tuilleadh!’					1600

[TD 201]

[Beurla]

[TD 202]

CXXIX

Có nì mire rium?

Màiri Eóghainn Mhóir a’ seinn


Ó hoireann ó,có nì mire rium?
Hù hoireann ó, có nì mire rium?
Ó hoireann ó, có nì mire rium?

Có nì sùgradh, có nì cinnealtas,
Có nì è, mur dian na gillean à?
Latha dhomh ’s mi falbh nam fireachan
Thachair orm a’ ghruagach chinnealta,
Sgian bheag ’na làimh ’s i buain biolaire					1605
’S i buain eòintein an cois na linneadh ud;
Theann mi null is fhuair mi bruidhinn dhi,
’S dh’fhoighneachd mi gu dé bu chinneadh dhi?
’S thuirt i riumsa ‘tha bann-Siosalach’.
Thuirt mi rith’ gum b’e mo chinneadh e,					1610
Thuirt i rium ‘cha bhi mo thurus riut,
Gura Leathanach mo roghainn-sa,
Gun dian Dòmhnallach mo choinneachadh.’

[TD 203]

[Beurla]

[TD 204]

CXXX

Nighean donn bheadarrach, ho ró

Catriana nighean ’Ill’ Easbuig a’ seinn


Nighean donn bhàn, é ho ró,
Nighean donn bheadarrach, ho rò,
Nighean donn bhàn, é ho ró.

’S gu bheil Calum ’n seo gun ghràdh,
Nighean donn bheadarrach, ho ró,
Nighean donn bhàn, é ho ró.

’S math as aithnte dhomh do ghràdh,						1615
Nighean donn bheadarrach, ho rò,
Nighean donn bhàn, é ho ró.

’S gura Màiri thug dhut gràdh,
Nighean donn bheadarrach, ho ró,
Nighean donn bhàn, é ho ró.

Gur h-i ’n nurs a bhios iad ’g ràdh,
Nighean donn bheadarrach, ho ró,
Nighean donn bhàn, é ho ró.

(agus mar sin air adhart, ag ainmneachadh an fheadhainn a bhiodh an làthair, air son spòrs agus dibhearsain a dheanamh).

Hug hòireann o, ’s e m’fhuath ì.

’S e — —— nì do ruaimleachadh,

’S e — —— a laigheas leat,

<eng>APPENDIX

This Appendix contains three little-known songs in 8² metre that were recovered too late for inclusion in Vol. II; and the texts, with translations, of Barra versions of numbers XXXII and XXIII of Vol. I, these differing considerably from the Uist versions printed there, and being of some historical and literary interest.<gai>

[TD 210]

CXXXII

Mhic ’ic Ailein seasgair sìobhalt

Bean Mhurchaidh a’ Bhealaich a’ seinn


Hò ró ho hù linn ó,
Hao ri linn ’s a bhó ro ho,
Hò ró ho hù linn ó.

Mhic ’ic Ailein seasgair siobhalt’,
’S a Mhic Leòid nan sròl ’s nam pìoban,
Na creid bean-shiubhail na mì-rùin—						1630
Tha nìghneagan òg a’s tìr-sa
Gun bheud, gun mhasladh, gum mhì-chliù;
Tha Màiri gu foinnidh fìorghlan,
Tha geall beat aig Cairistian’ oirr’,
’S math thig bas geal ’s sìod’ is lìon oirr’					1635
Brògan bhuinn àrd bhanndail sìod’ oirr’.
Thànaig do Chlann Nìll na sgriachadh
Bheireadh cluas a mach ’san Ìochdar,
Ghluaiseadh bodha an Cille Brìghde,
’S am Baghasdal an taobh shìos dheth.					1640
Froiseadh nam froineacha crìona
Air a’ bheul nach dubhairt an fhìrinn,
Froiseadh mar an fheamain chìrein
Ri muir reothairt na h-an-sìde.
’S fhada mi ’m chadal ’s mi dìomhain,					1645
Nach do dhùisg mi garg gun sìothchain,
Nach do sgiol mi cinn gun chìrean,
Gun sgian gun siosar gun innean,
Ach le éisginn mo bheòil innse.
Mhic ’ic Ailein seasgair sìobhalt,						1650
’S a Mhic Leòid nan sròl ’s nam pìoban,
Car’ thu mhac Tighearna an fhìona,
Do Mhac Nìll nan sròl ’s nam pìoban.

[TD 211]

[Beurla]

[TD 212]

CXXXIII

Òganaich dhuinn a rinn m’ fhàgail

Faclan aig Ceit Ruairi Iain Bhàin; Bean an Dotair A. Mac ’ill’ Eathain a’ seinn


Hi ri a bho, hiù ra bho ho,
Hi ri a bho ró hao ag, o
Hi rì a bho, hiù ra bho ho.

Òganaich dhuinn a rinn m’fhàgail,
Chum thu air deireadh air chàch mi,						1655
Gun fhios do athair no mhàthair,
Gun fhios do phiuthar no bhràthair,
Fios aig Lachlann, fios aig Teàrlach,
Fios aig Iain Donn as a’ ghàrradh.

Òganaich o thìr a’ mhurain,							1660
Tha mi air mo lionn-dubh umad—
Cha n-ann le fuath, ach le furan.

’S aithne dhomh fhìn dé chum bhuam thu—
Mìod mo ghaoil ort, ’s lughad m’fhuath ort,
’S tainead mo chruidh air a’ bhuailidh;					1665
Ged is ise ’s truime bhuaile,
’N saoil am mise ’s miosa duaiseadh?
’S ann tha ise shìol na tuatha,
’S ann the mise shìol nan uaislean.

A bhean ud thall aig a’ chuibhill,						1670
’S aithne dhomh mar tha do chridhe,
Tha lionn-dubh ’gad chur an umhail,
Caimbeulach òg or ’o shiubhal;
Cha n-fhaigh thu baisteadh an Uibhist,
Gun dig thu nall air an rubha,							1675
Gun ruig thu fear a’ chùil bhuidhe.

[TD 213]

[Beurla]

CXXXV

Thug an latha gu dìle

(a) Nan Eachainn Fhionnlaigh a’ seinn

Hoireann ó, hó gù,
Hi rì ri, em bó chalainn,
Hoireann ó, hó gù.

Thug an latha gu dìle,
’S gu sìor-uisge meallach;
Thug am bàta na caoil oirr’,							1690
Bha mo ghaol oirr’ a dh’fhearaibh,
Bha mo ghaol air a h-ùrlar,
Lùb ùr a’ chùil chlannaich;
Bu leat urram ’ga stiùradh
Ann an sùghannan mara,							1695
’S tu mo chnothan ’s mo chaorain,
’S tu mo thomanan meala,
Ach ma lig sibh gu tìr i,
Gum bi mìostadh mu’n chala,
Gum bi fuil nan trì rìghrean,							1700
Fuil Mhac Rìll is Mhic ’ic Ailein,
. . . . . .
’S Tighearn’ Òg Loch nan Eala.


(b) Bho’n LS aig Dòmhnall Mac an t-Saoir

Ó ho ró, hó gù,
Hao ri iù, ho ro ghealladh,
Ó ho ró, hó gù.

Tha mi dubhach, trom, deurach,
’S trom mo cheum ’s gun mhi fallain;					1705
Thug am baile gu chéile
Eadar ghréidheir is sgalag,
Eadar bhanchaig is bhuachaill’,
’S luchd ualach an fhearainn.

[TD 217]

[Beurla]

[TD 218]

Thug an latha gu dìle,								1710
’S gu sìor-uisge meallach
. . . . . .
Ann an aodann nam fearaibh.
Chì mi long as a’ chaolas
Air a taobh am bàrr mara,							1715
Cha n-eil oirre ach Goill bheaga,
’S iad a streap ris na crannaibh;
Mura lig sibh gu tìr i,
Gum bi dìobhail mu’n charraig,
Gum bi fuil an Iarl’ òig ann,							1720
Fuil Mhic Leòid ’s Mhic Nìll Bharraidh.

Mura can mi ach fìrinn,
’S ann tha ’n aoibh air mo leannan,
Gruaidh dhearg as glan deàrrsadh
Ann an sgàthan na glaine;							1725
Thig am boineid le fàbhar
Air fear àrd a’ chùil chlannaich,
Thig an triùbhsair air fhiaradh
Air sliasaid a’ ghallain,
’S tu mo chnothan is m’ùbhlan,						1730
Mo chruit-chiùil ’s mo chruinn mheala.

[TD 220]

(XXXII)

Có sheinneadh an fhìdeag airgid?

(a) Anna Raghnaill Eachainn, am Barraidh, a’ seinn

(A)
Có sheinneadh an fhìdeag airgid?
Hì rì ill iù ill eò ro,
Có theireadh nach seinninn fhìn i?
Hó ro hu ò, hù ill eò ro,
Có theireadh nach seinninn fhin i?
Hì rì ill iù ill eò ro,
Có theireadh nach seinneadh, sheinneadh,
Hò ro hu ò, hù ill eò ro,
Có theireadh nach seinneadh, sheinneadh,
Hì rì ill iù ill eò ro,
Sheinneadh i Mac Leòid ’s Mac Coinnich!					1735
Hò ro hu ò, etc.
Mac mo Rìgh a’ tighinn a dh’Alba
Air luing mhóir nan trì chruinn airgid,
Cha bhuill ghaoisne, cha bhuill chainbe,
Streanganan dha’n t-sìoda Fhrangach,
’S ulagan òir air gach ceann dhiu.						1740

(B)
Nuair thig mac mo Rìgh dha’n bhaile,
Nìtear le Clann Dòmhnall banais!

(C)
Gura h-e mo chion ’s mo rùn thu,
Fìdhleireachd bu ragha ciùil dhut,
’S clàrsach bhinn ’s a cruinn ’gan rùsgadh,					1745
Ruighle mu seach air an ùrlar.

(D)
Gura h-e mo chion ’s mo roghainn
Tormod Òg nan gormshuil loinneil;
Ge bè thrialladh gu d’ thaigh thalla,
Gheibheadh e fialachd ann gun ghainne,					1750

[TD 221]

[Beurla]

[TD 222]

Cha b’fhiach leat bhith ’g òl an leanna—
Uisge beatha na treas-tarraing,
Branndaidh chruaidh a nuas o Ghallaibh!

(E)
Gura h-e mo chion ’s mo ghràdh thu,
Giamanach gunna nan càrnan,						1755
Giamanach na h-eala bàineadh,
Nì thu mac an fhéidh a thàladh.

(F)
Clogada cruadhach mu d’ ghruaig chamlaich,
Clogada cruadhach ’s suaicheantas dearg ris;
’S b’fheàrr liom na na bhuailt’ an airgiod					1760
Bhith far do ghluais iad dha d’ mharbhadh,
Mo sgian chlì bhith ’n cridh’ mo dheàrnadh.

(G)
Falbhaidh mise, fàgaidh mi ’m baile,
Ruigidh mi tìrmor na Hearadh,
Far na dh’àraicheadh mi ’m leanabh,						1765
Far an robh mi òg ’nam chaileig,
Cha n-ann ri fuine nam fallaid,
Ach ri ualach a’ chruidh-bhainne.
Hò ro hù a, hù ill eò ro.


(b) Mar a bha e aig Anna Aonghais Chaluim

(A)
Có sheinneadh an fhìdeag airgid?
Hì ri il iu il eo,
Mac mo Rìgh a’ tighinn do dh’Alba						1770
Hò rò hu o hù ill o,
Air luing mhóir air bhàrr na fairge,
Air luing a’ mharaiche dh’fhalbh e
Air luing a’ mharaiche ghreannmhoir.
Bha stiùir òir is dà chrann airgid,
’S tobar fìona shìos ’na garbhlach,						1775
Cuplaichean dha’n t-sìoda Fhrangach,
Ugalan òir air gach ceann dhiu.
Có theireadh nach seinneadh, sheinneadh,
Có sheinneadh ach Eóghainn Camshron!
Có theireadh nach seinneadh, sheinneadh!					1780
Sheinneadh Mac Leòid is Mac Coinnich!

[TD 223]

[Beurla]

[TD 224]

(B)
Nuair a thig mo Rìgh gu f(h)earann,
Crùnar am Prionnsa le caithream
’San Taigh Bhàn an tàmh na gaisgich;
Nìtear le Clann Dòmhnall banais,						1785
Bacasdair gu deanamh arain,
’S grùdairean gu ligeil leanna,
Feòl mhuc ’ga bruich, ’s muc ’ga gearradh
Le àrd-bhean-fheadhma Ghlinne Garadh.


(c) Mar a bha e aig Iain MacFhionghuin, Baile na Creige

(A)
Có sheinneas an fhìdeag airgid?						1790
Hò ro hù ò, hò ill eò,
Mac mo Rìgh-sa dol gu fairge
Hì rì ill iù leò,
Deanamh deas gu tighinn a dh’Alba
Air luing mhóir nan trì chrann airgid,
Streanganan dha’n t-sìoda Fhrangach,
’S ulagan òir air gach ceann dhiu.						1795

(B)
Nuair thig mac mo Rìgh gu fhearann,
’S a chur fàilt’ air Mac ’ic Ailein,
’S air ceann-fheadhna Ghlinne Garann,
’S air Mac Dhòmhnaill mhóir o’n Cheapaich,
’S air Mac Dhòmhnaill Duibh Loch Abair.					1800

(C)
Fear taighe nach fhaighte brùideil,
’S nach bi luchd-tathaich dheth diùmbach.

(D)
’S cha b’e am biadh breacag thana,
Ach bacastair a’ bruich an arain,
’S fuaim na beòire dol ’sa channa,						1805
Fuaim an fhìona dol ’sa ghlainidh,
’S uisge beatha na treas tarraing,
’S branndaidh chruaidh a nuas a Gallaibh.

(H)
’S chuireadh iad na Goill gu’n dùbhshlan,
’S chuireadh iad Rìgh Seòras dhachaigh					1810
Do Hanóbhar a null fairis,

[TD 225]

[Beurla]

[TD 226]

’S có sheinneas an fhìdeag airgid?
Có theireadh nach seinninn, sheinninn,
Có theireadh nach seinninn fhín i?

(F)
Mo cheist am ministeir ainmeil,						1815
Nam bithinn an àite buille d’ mharbhadh,
’S clogada cruadhach air ghruaig chamlaich.
’S clogada cruadhach ’s suaicheantas dearg air.


(d) Ceathramhnan nach robh aig Dòmhnall Mac Carmaig, anns an bhearsain a fhuair Mgr. Ailein bho Bhean Dhunnchaidh an Éirisgeidh

(I)
Dhomhsa b’aithne beus do bhaile,

Canon MacLean	MS. collection of the words of 44 songs, mostly waulking songs and a few in more than one version. These were taken down mostly from Mrs. Mary MacCuish (Màiri nighean Alasdair), a few from Mrs. Angus MacIntyre (mother of the late Donald MacIntyre, Dòmhnall Ruadh, the bard), Mrs. Donald Steele, and Mrs. Fanny MacIsaac. Manuscript containing 17 waulking songs, and two or three other songs, taken down by the late Donald MacLachlan, Connel, in Oban in the 1890s, probably from Colin Campbell, piper, an old soldier who was a native of South Uist. The texts of the waulking songs are good, and clearly related to other South Uist versions. Donald MacLachlan, whose family came from Conaglen in Ardgour, died in 1921 aged about 76.

W.S.B.			Waulking Songs from Barra. Scottish Tradition 3, recorded and documented by the School of Scottish Studies, University of Edinburgh (London, 1972). LP disc containing seven waulking songs, and some piping, plus illustrated texts with translations.

Z.C.P.			Zeitschrift für celtische Philologie. 1897–.

The above manuscript sources naturally overlap. They contain in all approximately 220 different waulking songs, some in more than one version; some are only fragments. It is to be feared that the airs of about 12 or 15 per cent of them are now totally lost.


LXXXIX

<gai>Latha bha ’n Ridire ’g òl<eng>

(‘One day when the Knight was drinking’)

Recorded from Mrs. MacDougall (‘Anna Raghnaill Eachainn’), Castlebay, Barra, on disc XXIV in March 1938; published in G.F.B., with disc, in 1950. The second version was recorded from Miss Mary

[TD 236]

Morrison, Earsary (‘Màiri Eóghainn Mhóir’) with chorus, at Castlebay on 29/12/49, wire recording no. 109.

Other versions; a recording of Miss Mary Morrison made in 1966 by the School of Scottish Studies has been published, with text, translation, and musical transcription in Scottish Tradition, 3, ‘Waulking Songs from Barra’, in 1972. It is therefore possible to hear both versions of this song on records. A version of this song was also recorded from Nan MacKinnon by Francis Collinson and James Ross in 1956.

Text (12 lines) and harmonized musical transcription (from Frances Tolmie), Gesto Coll., Appendix, p. 15 (1895).

Musical transcription and translation of text, F. T. no. 91, p. 253, where the tune given is the same as that used in Skye for Am Bròn Binn. In Barra these were sung to different tunes (cf. vol. ii, no. XLI).

A version of the tune, with refrain syllables and one line of text, was printed by Mrs. Kennedy-Fraser in More Songs of the Hebrides (1929), p. viii.

Fragments of text occur in songs printed by K. C. C. in Orain Luaidh Màiri Nighean Alasdair, pp. 46 and 114.

MS. versions: the only one known to me is that in the Morison MS. taken down in Mull about a hundred years ago. I have to thank Dr. Alan Bruford for drawing my attention to this version, which is the longest (45 lines).

As sung by Miss Mary Morrison with a very competent chorus in December 1949, the song was perfectly performed as a waulking song, the soloist beginning with the short refrain, which was repeated by the chorus, and repeating every line, with alternating refrain sung by the chorus. At the end of each section, where the repetition of the last line was followed by the short refrain sung by the chorus, the soloist repeated the short refrain to indicate change of subject, and the chorus repeated it, and at the end of the song this process took place again. By this method the soloist had complete control of the choice of song, and of the ending and beginning of each section of the song and of the song itself (see vol. i. 215–17).

Like many of the older waulking songs, this one has the dream-like quality of including a number of different sections on different subjects, in differing order in different versions, and with some metrical variation. Taking the episodes in the order in which they occur in the Morison MS. version, these are:

A. The Knight cuts his finger (while carving, F. T.) in an inn, where he has gone with his wife, and dies (apparently from shock—normally one would expect blood poisoning) quite suddenly.

B. His relations are sent for at once to bury him.

C. His wife says she will not leave his graveside until she dies herself, to be buried beside him.

D. A man (‘the skipper’, M.), asks her to marry him. She refuses. (This episode only in the Morison MS.)

[TD 237]

E. A man tells her that she is foolish—many a young wife lost her husband before now (and got over it).

F. ‘I had the best wife, horse, ship, dog, etc.’ (In M., ‘If I were king, I would have …’).

G. Fionn, Conan, Conn, and Fionn’s dog Bran, encounter a headless monster while returning from the hunt.

H. Sorrow is expressed for good Ruairi (M.? Guaire) with whom the authoress has travelled far and wide before they were separated.

It is evident, first, that the first five episodes here make a coherent story, and that the last three are probably extraneous; secondly, that if the song was originally a ballad of quatrains with internal rhyme and with end-rhyme in even lines, a number of lines have been lost.

In fact, episode F occurs in versions of the ballad Am Bròn Binn taken down by Alexander Carmichael in North Uist about a hundred years ago, see T.G.S.I. ix. 72–3, and C.G. v. 96. Episode G may well be connected with the ballad ‘Collun (sic) gun Cheann’, of which a fragment (6 quatrains) was printed by J. F. Campbell in L.F., 212 (1872). This was taken down for J. F. C. in Lewis in 1866; he remarked he had no other fragment of it.

Episode H is reminiscent of passages in the waulking song Seathan (vol. ii, no. XLVI), though the Morison MS. reading of ‘Guaire’ (a Fingalian name) for ‘Ruairi’ of other versions suggests that ‘Ruairi’ may be a corruption.

These episodes are represented in the other versions in the following order:

Mrs. MacDougall: A, B, G, F (Miss Annie Johnston said that it should have been A, B, F, G).

Miss Mary Morrison: H, A, B, C, E, G.

Gesto Coll.: A, B, C.

K. C. C.: A (p. 46, mixed with a fragment of Duan Fraoich); B (p. 114), mixed with a fragment of no. XVI in vol. i of this series.

1, 33. <gai>Dha’n tigh mhór,<eng> Gesto Coll. <gai>A fhein sa te mhoir a bhean,<eng> M.

3, 24. <gai>Snagadaireachd ghearr è mhéur,<eng> Gesto Coll., translated ‘When he was carving, he cut his finger’ in F. T. <gai>Snagaireach gun ghear e mhiar,<eng> M. <gai>Spadaireachd gun ghèarr e a mhiar,<eng> K. C. C. 46. Cf. Dieckhoff, <gai>‘Snagaireachd,<eng> chipping with a knife’.

4, 35. This line is not in M., which has instead <gai>’S nuair chunnaic e dearg a bhas<eng> = ‘when he saw his palm was red’.

5. Gesto Coll. <gai>An t-eagal a ghabh è mu mhéur,<eng> translated ‘From the fear he felt about his finger’ in F. T.

12. The monster is said in M. to have had <gai>Da shuil dhiag air a taobh cli / Da shuil dhiag air a taobh deas,<eng> ‘twelve eyes on her left side, and twelve eyes on her right side’. Nothing more is said about her in M., but the fragment of the ballad Collun gun Cheann printed in L. F. suggests that

[TD 238]

a version of the motif ‘Man disenchants loathsome woman by embracing her’, well known in Gaelic folklore, may be involved. See item D 732 in Tom Peate Cross, Motif-Index of Early Irish Literature; J. F. Campbell, West Highland Tales, iii. 421, ‘Nighean Rìgh fo Thuinn’.

13, 50. Conan was a well-known member of the Fiann, notorious as a trouble-maker. There were various members of the Fiann called Conn, one of whom was Conan’s son.

14, 51. Bran was Fionn mac Cumhail’s dog; see Stories from South Uist, pp. 8–12; L.F. 148–50.

21, 48. <gai>Marcraich<eng> is the form used in Uist and Barra for literary <gai>marcaich.<eng>

24, 25. M. <gai>Mulad tha lionndubh tha gruaim orm / Mulad mor an deaghainn Ruairi (? Guaire);<eng> K. C. C. 114, <gai>Mulad, tha liunn dubh, tha gruaim orm, / Fadachd mhór an deaghaidh Ruairidh<eng> (in a completely different song); W.S.B.: <gai>Mulad is lionn-dubh tha gruaim / Mulad mór a deoghaidh Ruairi<eng> (recording of Mary Morrison made by the School of Scottish Studies in 1966). The original wire recording of 29/11/49 might be <gai>air deagh-Ruairi,<eng> but this would not make sense.

This section comes last in the Morison MS. version, and probably did not belong to the song originally. Ll. 27–31 here recall ll. 233–6 in the song on <gai>Seathan<eng> in vol. ii, see also C.G. v. 70, last 16 lines on page, where the line <gai>Bha mi ’n I nan cailleacha dubha leat<eng> corresponds to the line <gai>Bha mi ’n I nan calachean ruadha<eng> of the Morison MS. version of the present song.

29. Glasgow is not usually referred to in such flattering terms in Gaelic verse; a more frequent appellation is <gai>Glaschu nam bùth<eng> = Glasgow of the shops.

30. <gai>Suas<eng> means ‘southwards’ in Uist and Barra, as well as ‘up’. The Ross of Mull must be meant here. In M. the places visited are Inverness (twice), and ‘Iona of the russet nuns’.

34. In recording this song in 1949, Miss Mary Morrison sang <gai>Gheàrr a’ bhann-ridir’ a meur,<eng> ‘the knight’s lady cut her finger’, but in 1966 according to the transcription in W.S.B. she sang <gai>Gheàrr a’ Ridire mheur,<eng> which must be the correct reading, as it fits the sense of the song and is supported by all the other versions.

37. In 1949 Miss Morrison sang <gai>a cinneadh<eng> ‘her kin’, but <gai>a chàirdean<eng> ‘his relations’ in the next line.

41. The metre shows that the middle vowel of <gai>dealchadh<eng> is a svarabhakti vowel that has presumably arisen since the original middle vowel was dropped through syncope. The same thing can happen in similar words, e.g. Orain Ghàidhlig le Seonaidh Caimbeul, p. 73, where <gai>failcheadh<eng> (from <gai>falaich,<eng> hide) rhymes with <gai>margadh.<eng>

42, 43, 45, 46. These lines are not in M.

[TD 239]

XC

<gai>Shuidh mi air cnocan an tiùrr<eng>

‘I sat on a mound at high-water mark’

Recorded from Mrs. Neil Campbell (‘Bean Nìll’) at Frobost, South Uist, on tape on 3/6/58. Not widely known, tune probably unpublished.

MS. version: Morison MS. (15 lines). This begins <gai>Dh’eirich mi moch maduinn chiuin / E ho hao ri ri o hu / Shuidh mi air cnocan a’ chiuil / E ho hao ri ri huiribh i / E ho hao ri ri o hu.<eng>

Printed version: K. C. C. 111 (26 lines).

The K. C. C. version has 15 lines that correspond to lines in Mrs. Neil Campbell’s version, and 8 that correspond to lines in the Morison MS. version. Six lines correspond fairly closely in all three versions. The last 9 lines in Mrs. Neil Campbell’s version do not occur in either K. C. C. or the Morison MS., but they are found in other songs, see below.

53. This was the usual way of signalling for a ferry in the old days. The Morison MS. has <gai>Rinn mi gurraban is tùirs’,<eng> ‘I lamented and sorrowed’. See Gairm 81.95.

56. In the Morison MS. this line is followed by

<gai>Mac an Albanaich air thus
Mac an Eirionnaich mo run<eng>

‘the son of the Scot first, the son of the Irishman my beloved’. K. C. C. has the same lines, but the positions are reversed.

70–8. Cf. ll. 425–35, 598–604, and 844–55 in vol. ii. This is a passage that turns up repeatedly in old waulking songs. It cannot be said to belong to any particular song more than another.


XCI

<gai>Dh’éirich mi gu moch Di-luain<eng>

‘On Monday early I arose’

Recorded from Miss Mary Gillies (‘Màiri Mhìcheil Nìll’), with chorus, at Castlebay, Barra, on 5/4/51, wire recording no. 735. Apparently unpublished.

81–4. Cf. ll. 877–9 of no. XXIV of vol. i.

89–100. Cf. ll. 866–76 in no. XXIV of vol. i.

93. Cf. (perhaps) Kuno Meyer, Mitteilungen aus Irischen Handschriften, ‘Eine mystiche Auslegung der Tonleiter’ (‘A mystical interpretation of

[TD 240]

the scale’), an extract from T. C. D. MS., H. 3.18. ‘An cūiced ponc. .i. sōl 7 inann 7 grian 7 inann īsidhe 7 fìrendacht’ ‘the fifth note’ i.e. sol and it equals ‘sun’ and that equals ‘righteousness’ (ZCP. 5. 497).

This may be coincidental, but the full scale of the bagpipe has nine notes, and it is difficult to think of another meaning for the allusion to the ‘fifth note’.


XCII

<gai>Mìle marbhaisg air a’ ghaol<eng>

(‘A thousand curses may there be’)

Recorded from Miss Annie Johnston (‘Anna Aonghuis Chaluim’), at Castlebay, Barra, in March 1938 (disc no. XXXIII; 16 lines), and from Miss Mary Gillies (‘Màiri Mhìcheil Nìll’), with chorus, by the Barra Folklore Committee on 9/3/50 (wire no. 304; printed here). Also from Miss Elizabeth Sinclair (‘Ealasaid Iain Dhunnchaidh’) on Vatersay by Mr. Donald MacPherson in 1953, on tape (21 lines).

MS. versions exist in the notebooks of Calum and Annie Johnston; also in K. 204, from Marion MacLennan (20 lines).

Versions of the text have been printed in the Inverness Collection of Gaelic Songs (1821, p. 215, 14 lines; in the McD. 266, 19 lines; and in K. C. C. 19, 29 lines). This is therefore one of the first traditional waulking song texts to have appeared in print. There has been some confusion between the text of this song and that of no. XXXIII of vol. i, which begins <gai>Cha déid mise, cha déid mi, / Cha déid mi do Cheann Loch Fraon;<eng> but the refrain syllables and the tunes are quite distinct. Versions of the texts of both songs can be seen side by side in K. C. C.

102. Inv. Coll. <gai>’S ann a rinn a mise chlaoi;<eng> McD., <gai>Asam fhin a thug e chlaoidh;<eng> K. C. C. <gai>Os a h-è rinn mise chlaoidh.<eng> For ll. 102–3, here, Annie Johnston had

<gai>Gura bràthair dha’n aog,
Tholl e mo chridh’ air gach thaobh<eng>

‘It (love) is the brother to death, it has pierced my heart on both sides.’

Elizabeth Sinclair had

<gai>’S mise cheannaich e gu daor,
Chuir e saighead ann am thaobh<eng>

I paid for it dearly, it put an arrow in my side’.

106–10. Miss Elizabeth Sinclair had the following interesting version of this passage:

<gai>’S truagh nach mise bh’air an stiùir
’N amm bhith togail rith’ an t-siùil,
Spionnadh unnam b’urrainn trìùir,

[TD 241]

Spionnadh ceathrar bhith ’nam chùl,
Spionnadh ochdar o ’s e ’s mùth’;
Chuirinn na gillean air chùl,
’S chuirinn an sgiobair air stiùir,
Rachainn fhìn ’s mo ghaol fo rùm.<eng>

‘A pity I was not at the helm, at the time when the sail was hoisted, with the strength of three men in me, the strength of four in my back, better still the strength of eight; I would get rid of the lads, I would set the skipper to steer, and my love and I would go below deck.’

111. Inv. Coll. <gai>Cha teid mi gu Mac Iain Mhaoil, / Chan ann air a tha mo ghaol,<eng> ‘I will not go to the son of bald John, I do not love him’.

For ll. 111–18, here. cf. ll. 1164–70 in no. XXXIII in vol. i.

113. Miss Annie Johnston and Miss Elizabeth Sinclair both began the song with this line, in effect reversing the order of the sections. Miss Sinclair ended her version with lines corresponding to 101–4 here, two of which have been quoted in the note on l. 102.


XCIII

<gai>Thug mi ’n oidhche, ge b’fhad’ i<eng>

(‘I spent the night, though it was long’)

First version recorded from Roderick MacKinnon (‘Ruairi Iain Bhàin’) at Northbay, Barra, in March 1938, disc no. XIII. Second version recorded from Nan MacKinnon (‘Nan Eachainn Fhionnlaigh’) (no relation) by Donald MacPherson on Vatersay in July 1951 for the Folklore Institute of Scotland. Both of these are printed here.

Seven lines and refrain were recorded from Mrs. David Patterson (‘Ceit nighean Ailein, ’ic Chaluim’) née MacNeil, at Benacadie, Cape Breton, on 4/10/37. Her version, like Nan MacKinnon’s, began with what is l. 132 here. The late Monsignor P. J. Nicholson, President of St. Francis Xavier University, Antigonish N.S., a Gaelic speaker who was brought up amongst people of Barra and South Uist descent in the neighbourhood, told me he had never heard the song begun as Roderick MacKinnon began it.

MS. versions: Capt. D. J. MacKinnon (son of Ruairi Iain Bhàin), MSS., 22 lines; Barra MS., 23 lines; A. J. (2), 18 lines.

The recording of Roderick MacKinnon’s version, together with text and translation, was published in G.F.B. 28 in 1950. This, as far as we know, is the only time that the text of this little-known song has been printed.

Art version of the air: Mrs. Kennedy-Fraser, From the Hebrides, p. 104, ‘The Silent Crane’. ‘Air from Mrs. MacKinnon. N. Bay, Barra. English words by Kenneth MacLeod.’ The English words have nothing to do

[TD 242]

with the real song, but the Gaelic refrain syllables were used. ‘Mrs. MacKinnon’ was presumably Roderick MacKinnon’s sister, known in Gaelic as ‘Bean Shomhairle Bhig’.

The song was probably originally composed in couplets to a different air.

122. <gai>Ionndrainn<eng> ‘missing’ in Nan MacKinnon’s version (l. 158) makes better sense than <gai>amharc<eng> or <gai>coimhead.<eng> The poetess could hardly have been watching the brothers in the field in the darkness of night.

123. The MS. versions follow this line with <gai>Gum bu sin na fir allail,<eng> ‘Those were the famous men’. This was not sung by Ruairi Iain Bhàin; if it is part of the song, the metre and rhyme suggest that a following line has been lost.

124. <gai>Furail,<eng> ‘hospitable’, so Dieckhoff. Not in other dictionaries.

125. This may imply that the brothers were amongst the Scots mercenaries in the army of King Gustavus Adolphus, which distinguished themselves at the battle of Leipzig (1631). Some 6,000 Scots had been raised for the Swedish service by the Marquis of Hamilton.

128. D. J. McKinnon, <gai>Beidh an claibhidh sios math ruidh,<eng> Barra MS., <gai>Thig an claidheamh sior-mar-ur<eng> A. J. (2) <gai>Thig an claidheamh sior ma-ribh. Siar<eng> ‘behind’ makes the best sense; Ruairi Iain Bhàin apparently sang sìos.

129. D. J. McKinnon, <gai>Nial falaish gu cul dith<eng> Barra MS., <gai>Neall faileis gu chùl dheth<eng> A. J. (2), <gai>’S neul falais gu chùl dheth.<eng> Ruairi Iain Bhàin sang <gai>Nial falluis gu chùl air.<eng>

The line is obviously corrupt; it does not make sense, nor does cùl or chùl fit the rhyme, which requires long i or ia. A couplet on p. 299 of the Gillies Collection suggests what the reading should be:

<gai>Agus claidheamh geur tharad, o,
Neul fala o dhéis air.<eng>

‘You wearing a sharp sword, o, the hue of blood off its blade-point.’

Cf. McD. 50, <gai>Luchd nan sgiath ’s nan lann tana, / Neul na fala gun déis orr’,<eng> also the Turner Collection, p. 39, <gai>’S neul fal’ air roinn am picean,<eng> ‘The hue of blood on the points of their pikes’. It therefore seems justifiable to amend the line here to <gai>Nial fala gu’n dias air,<eng> which makes sense and rhyme. Cf. also McD. 215, <gai>Bu tu sealgair na h-eala, / Bhiodh neul fal’ air a taobh<eng> ‘You were the hunter of the swan, there would be a hue of blood on her side’.

140–63. Nan MacKinnon’s version of this part of the song makes sense, and explains what is happening. The shorter versions, with many lines lost, are very puzzling.

144–5. The song must have been composed originally in a dialect in which <gai>deaghaidh<eng> (pronounced deoghaidh in Barra) and <gai>cladhach<eng> rhymed.

[TD 243]

151. <gai>Bucach,<eng> ‘a lump of a lad’.

163. This line lacks internal rhyme; l. 127 preserves it.


XCIV

<gai>Tha mo ghaol air Catriana<eng>

(‘My love is for Catriana’)

The preservation of this song is due to Annie and Calum Johnston. On 25/4/72 Calum Johnston sang and recorded ll. 176–81. The song was new to us, but later the complete text was found in the MS. notebook where Annie Johnston had written down the words of seven waulking songs for Mrs. Kennedy-Fraser in 1908.

The song is apparently unpublished. A version of the words of ll. 182–7 occurs as the words of song no. 62 in F. T. 220, to a different tune and refrain. The same Gaelic words, with one or two minor alterations, are attached to ‘The Land of Little People’ in Mrs. Kennedy-Fraser’s From the Hebrides, p. 126, but the refrain syllables are different. The song is headed ‘Dream-Sail-Hoisting. Air Eilean Mhara nach Traigh. Gaelic and Air from N. Bay, Barra. English verses from Robert Louis Stevenson’s “THE LITTLE LAND”’. But the air is different from that sung by Calum Johnston. On the other hand, Annie Johnston’s notebook contains a version of the air taken down in tonic sol-fa under the title ‘Rowing Song’ and with the note ‘Words and Air from Mrs. Cameron’ written below. The list of Mrs. Kennedy-Fraser’s recordings shows that the song was also sung for her by Mrs. MacKinnon (‘Bean Shomhairle Bhig’) in 1927.

168–9. The order of these lines is reversed in the MS., but the sense suggests they should be as here.

175. Dwelly gives as one meaning of <gai>uinneag<eng> ‘a recess in the wall of a kitchen used as a repository for miscellaneous articles’ (Sutherland).

183. In Miss Johnston’s MS. there is an X after this line, referring to the following added after the end of the song:

<gai>O Cholla gu Rum
O Chanaidh (?) do’n Dùn
O Uibhist a bhrùc
Gu Hirt nan eun fionn.<eng>

‘From Coll to Rum, from Canna to Dunvegan, from Uist of the sea wrack, to St. Kilda of the white birds’ (i.e. fulmars).

189. MS. first on linn, on deleted and gu substituted.

191. i.e. the MacDonalds of Sleat, cf. B.Gh. 4600–1.

[TD 244]

XCV

<gai>’S mi ri dìreadh ’san dùbhghlaic<eng>

(‘I am climbing up the dark glen’)

Recorded from Mrs. MacDougall (‘Anna Raghnaill Eachainn’), Castlebay, Barra, on disc no. XXII in March 1938.

Another version of the text was taken down by Fr. Allan McDonald, MS. collection of waulking songs, fol. 73. This is also printed here.

Printed version: K. C. C. 72.

Fr. Allan McDonald composed a Christmas hymn to the air. This was printed in the Mercat Cross of December 1953, and again in Bàrdachd Mhgr Ailein, edited by J. L. Campbell, 1965, p. 98. The air printed there is the basic air of the present waulking song, transcribed by Francis Collinson.

194. <gai>dùbhghlaic,<eng> ‘dark little glen’. The u is lengthened through stress falling on the first syllable of this compound word. <gai>Glaic,<eng> gen. for nom. <gai>glac,<eng> is used in Barra and Uist.

201–2, 216–18. Cf. the very similar passage in no. XXX, vol. i, ll. 1064–9, and the note there on it. The persons mentioned were tacksmen (large farmers related to clan chiefs), in this case MacLeans or MacDonalds.

203. This line recalls a personality frequently mentioned in old Gaelic stories, <gai>Bean chaol a’ chòt’ uaine,<eng> ‘the slender woman of the green coat’, a supernatural helper. Green was the colour associated with the fairies.

215. ‘Veer sunwise’, i.e. from west to north.

221–3. Cf. the very similar ll. 432–4 in no. XII, vol. i.

224–9. Another stock passage, cf. ll. 417–22 in no. XII, vol. i.

226, 228. The word <gai>circeag,<eng> diminutive of <gai>cearc,<eng> ‘hen’, is not in Scottish Gaelic dictionaries, but cf. Irish circín. It means ‘hen grouse’ in these songs.


XCVI

<gai>Phiuthar chridhe, chomainn chléibh’<eng>

(‘Beloved sister, bosom friend’)

Air recorded from Mrs. Neil Campbell (‘Bean Nìll’) at Frobost, South Uist, on 3/6/58.

Mrs. Campbell could only remember two lines of the song. A text (18 lines) was printed in K. C. C., 93. No other version is known to us.

The song is sung in the very unusual form of a divided line of seven syllables, divided into 4 + 3¹.

[TD 245]

So far as the story behind the song is concerned, a sister who is alone in her house along with an infant child, asks her sister to come in and visit her; her mother is tending cattle, her father is hunting deer, her brother is fishing in the loch, and her lover (who appears to have taken up with another woman) is at the cattle-sale.


XCVII

<gai>Trom òr o chalainn, ’s mise nach eil fallain

(‘Trom or o chalainn,<eng> I am unhappy’)

Recorded from Miss Mary Morrison, with chorus, at Castlebay, Barra, on 29/12/49, wire recording no. 110.

Unpublished. In MS., an alternative text is preserved in Miss Annie Johnston’s first notebook (1908). The variant part of this is printed here.

239. <gai>sgire Caluim<eng> is possibly a kenning for some district in the isles.

248. The Revd. William Matheson informs me that <gai>eàrradh<eng> is the groove inside a barrel at top and bottom into which the circular lid and bottom are fitted, and then locked into place by the hoop nearest top and bottom, called <gai>an cearcall-eàrraidh.<eng> The spigot has to be inserted at the <gai>eàrradh<eng> to ensure that the barrel or cask can be completely emptied.

251, 275. A person who would fit this patronymic is Iain, son and heir of Sir Ruairi Mór MacLeod of Dunvegan. Iain succeeded his father as (fourteenth) chief of the MacLeods, and died in 1649.

255. <gai>Nar mheala mi ’m bréid,<eng> ‘May I never live to wear the kertch’. Survival of a present subjunctive form. Cf. Alexander MacDonald, MS. LXIII, p. 143:

<gai>Na no mhela mi mo bhrecan
Muile mhaon mar sin smo phersa,
Mar heil mi toilich an iobra ncertuair
Ann abhir an riogh sa chertis<eng>

‘May I never live to wear my plaid, (or to enjoy) all my goods and my person, unless I am willing to offer them immediately in the cause of the king and of justice.’

Cf. also An Duanaire, 52, <gai>Nar a mheal mi am muineal;<eng> Sàr-Obair, 303, <gai>Nar a mheal mi mo shlainte.<eng> See J. Fraser, S.G.S. v. 168, on this survival.

The <gai>bréid<eng> or kertch was worn by women after marriage in the old Highlands.

257. <gai>Beannag.<eng> Cf. Alexander Carmichael, C.G. v. 12. ‘A small shawl [formerly] worn by women in Uist, sometimes on the shoulders and sometimes on the head … In the early half of the nineteenth century

[TD 246]

all young women in Uist put up the <gai>beannag<eng> on the morning of their wedding. This was of white linen formed into thin broad bands or hems from the forehead to the crown of the head. This was the badge of marriage.’ Ll. 256 and 257 therefore are the equivalent of saying ‘May I never be married’.

270–1. i.e. collecting seaweed used for manure, used on the <gai>feannagan<eng> or lazy-beds, the digging of which was described by Martin Martin, A Description of the Western Islands of Scotland, 1716 edn., p. 3. Cf. Fr. Allan McDonald, ‘the special work in making lazy beds called <gai>taomadh<eng> is the filling of the hollow between the row of sods on the outer edge of each lazy bed with soil taken from the drain or trench at each side of each bed—a drain formed by the removal of the sods already mentioned’ (G.W.S.U. 236).


XCVIII

<gai>Lìon mulad, lìon mulad<eng>

(‘Sorrow, sorrow has filled me’)

J. L. C. recorded this song from Mrs. Neil McInnis (‘Anna nighean Nìll’), Glace Bay, Cape Breton, on 12/10/37. He did not hear it again until Miss Kathy Torrey sent a recording she had made from Mrs. MacPhee (‘Maighread Shomhairle Bhig’) at Stonybridge, South Uist, in July 1975. Mrs. MacPhee is a native of Barra, being a niece of the late Roderick MacKinnon (‘Ruairi Iain Bhàin’), who is often mentioned in these volumes.

Mrs. McInnis’s version contains 22 lines, and is printed here, with an opening couplet from Mrs. MacPhee’s version; Mrs. MacPhee remembered 6 lines, of which the next 4 correspond to Mrs. McInnis’s version.

Miss Torrey has kindly allowed my collaborator’s transcription of the tune from her recording to be used in this book.

The metrical structure of the song suggests that it was probably originally composed in couplets, possibly to a different air. The song appears to be unpublished.

282. Mrs MacPhee used the form <gai>aotrom,<eng> which does not rhyme, suggesting that the song was originally made in a dialect where <gai>eutrom<eng> was the normal pronunciation. Mrs. McInnis, whose father’s people were of South Uist origin and whose mother’s were from Barra, had eutrom.

283. Mrs. McInnis had <gai>Nàile! goirtead mo choise.<eng>

295. Cf. the proverb <gai>Uaisle gun chuid, maragan gun gheir,<eng> ‘Gentry without goods, sausages without fat’.

301. See note on l. 271.

[TD 247]

XCIX

<gai>Gura mise tha gu tinn<eng>

(‘It is I who am ill’)

Recorded from Miss Elizabeth Sinclair (‘Ealasaid Iain Dhunnchaidh’) on Vatersay around 1951 by Donald MacPherson for the Folklore Institute of Scotland; tape recording made on a Wirek machine.

Apparently unpublished. There is a version in the O. 19, which is printed here, along with Miss Sinclair’s. Two songs in K. C. C. beginning with the same first line, pp. 57 and 58, are not connected.

312. The line following this one was inadvertently omitted in transcribing. It is <gai>Cha do dh’fhoighneachd, cha b’fhiach,<eng> ‘He didn’t ask (the price), he didn’t need to’.

319. Cf. ll. 492 and 544 in vol. i.

323. The transcription of this line is not certain.

330. Cf. Dinneen, <gai>scuabach<eng> ‘in sweeping masses (of the hair)’ (amongst other meanings); R.I.A. Contrib., scúap, sense (e), referring to human hair.


C

<gai>Tha mulad, tha mulad<eng>

(‘I am sad, I am sad’)

Recorded from Mrs. Neil Campbell (‘Bean Nìll’) at Frobost, South Uist, by Fr. John MacLean in the winter of 1958–9.

Version of text (23 lines) in K. C. C. 116–17. Otherwise apparently unpublished.

368, 341. Cf. ll. 256, 257.

378. <gai>Coir’ a’ Cheathaich,<eng> the Misty Corrie, in the Forest of Mamlorn, north of Crianlarich, the subject of a famous poem by Duncan Ban MacIntyre. This is evidence of a mainland origin for this song.


CI

<gai>Mhurchaidh bhig, a chinn a’ chonais<eng>

(‘Little Murdo, contentious fellow’)

Recorded from Miss Penny Morrison, with chorus, at the genuine waulking held at Gerinish, South Uist, on 29/3/51, wire recording no. 643.

[TD 248]

Previously recorded from Miss Morrison at a private céilidh at Gerinish on 2/11/50, wire recording no. 501. The text printed here is as sung on 29/3/51. Also recorded from Mrs. Neil Campbell (‘Bean Nìll’) at Frobost, South Uist, with Mrs. Patrick MacPhee joining in the refrain, on tape on 22/5/65; also printed here.

MS. version: D. McI. 106; printed here.

Printed versions; K. C. C. 95, 35 lines, of which eleven on the formulaic motif ‘if I were dividing the land you would get …’ seem quite out of place. C.G. iv. 350, three quatrains of the form ABCD + DEFG + GHIJ, with translation, in the section of the book headed ‘Miscellaneous’, source not stated. The first 6 lines correspond very closely to ll. 401–5, 415–17, and 451–5 here. This indicates that at least part of the present song was not originally a waulking song.

As we now have it, this strange song, sung by Miss Penny Morrison to an air in a pentachordal scale, consists of a number of unrelated passages, the order of which differs in the different versions. As they occur in Miss Morrison’s version, they are:

A. A short passage relating to an unsatisfactory person called Murchadh Beag mac Mhurchaidh ’ic Ruairi, not yet identified.

B. A passage about a person called Coinneach, who apparently has a well of wine in his hair.

C. About persons who would aid Diarmaid Donn in fighting. Possibly Ossianic.

D. On the sight of a beloved hunter.

E. A cryptic passage on an expedition to gather nuts, possibly referring to the same person.

F. On difficulties involved in illicit love-making. Of these Mrs. Neil Campbell has D, F, C; D. McI. has A, B, D, C; K. C. C. has D, A, C, plus the unrelated passage described; and C.G. has D.

391. For the ‘well of wine’, cf. l. 1224 in vol. i, and l. 166, here.

393, 399. These lines were not in the version recorded on 2/11/50.

396, 431, 462. Diarmaid Donn was a synonym for Diarmaid Ó Duibhne, the well-known Fingalian hero who eloped with Fionn’s wife Grainne, see L.F. 153–6; Stories from South Uist, p. 29, etc.

397, 398. The version recorded on 2/11/50 has leat for leis.

398, 433, 464. Bran = Bran mac Febail? Brian probably = Brian Boroimhe, victor of the battle of Clontarf, whose name occurs in some Scottish Gaelic folktales.

399. The allusion is not clear. Dublin is seldom mentioned in Scottish Gaelic traditional songs, though Galway often is. Perhaps the reference is to one of the Norse kings of Dublin.

400. In the version recorded on 2/11/50, this line was followed by <gai>Le each crùidheach cròinfhionn soilleir,<eng> ‘with his bright well-shod white-haired horse’.

[TD 249]

406. Cf. l. 1227 in vol. i.

412, 421. <gai>Is minig<eng> is used in Uist with the sense of <gai>Is mairg,<eng> ‘Pity he who’, not with its usual meaning of ‘often’, which is the one that fits the sense here, suggesting that the song was originally made in a district where <gai>minig<eng> usually had that meaning.

417. The singer here had <gai>Marcraich’ sìdhneadh nan each seang,<eng> a line which does not make sense, and which was probably due to the confusion of two such lines as <gai>Marcraich’ nan steud crùidheach seang, / Sealgair sìdhneadh nan damh donn,<eng> ‘Rider of slender well-shod steeds, Hunter of venison of the red deer’. Cf. l. 905 in vol. i.

438–40. These lines are out of place here. They occur more fittingly in ll. 1264–6 of no. XXXVI in vol. i.

461. ‘It was the custom with the bullies… to demand a cess from the country, to which every gentleman and commoner was to contribute, unless the Laird could find a person who was able to defy them with the sword.’ W. C. MacKenzie, The Western Isles, p. 130. See Stories from South Uist, p. 92. See also T.G.S.I. xxxix-xl. 163–5.


CII

<gai>Mhic Iarla nam Bratach Bàna<eng>

(‘Son of the Earl of White Banners’)

Recorded from Roderick MacKinnon (‘Ruairi Iain Bhàin’) at Northbay, Barra, on disc no. III in March 1938. There was only room for the first 11 lines on the disc.

MS. versions: one in one of Capt. D. J. MacKinnon’s notebooks, obviously from his father, but complete. Also in A. J. (2), p. 54, a very similar version.

Art version: Mrs. Kennedy-Fraser printed Miss Annie Johnston’s version of the air in Songs of the Hebrides, vol. iii, p. xv, calling it a ‘slow rowing song from Barra’. In From the Hebrides she utilized the air for an art song which she called ‘Iona Boat Song’ Air noted from Annie Johnson (sic), Barra and arr. by M. Kennedy-Fraser. Words, Gaelic and English, by Kenneth Macleod, with ancient refrain’ (p. 69).

The editors have not ever heard Miss Johnston sing this song, and do not know of any other version of it. Roderick MacKinnon certainly did not sing it at the tempo of a ‘slow rowing song’. His version was published, along with a reproduction of the disc recording, in Gaelic Folksongs from the Isle of Barra in 1950, from which the song has acquired considerable popularity. The text printed here conforms to the recording, and is completed from the version in Captain MacKinnon’s notebook.

474. A. J. <gai>sithinn.<eng> The word sounds like si on the record, and Capt. MacKinnon writes shi. Metrically it must be only one syllable.

[TD 250]

476. The rhyming of <gai>stàtail / airgid / bàineadh<eng> and later of <gai>sàile / airgid / Gailmhinn / Spàine<eng> is an unusual phenomenon. Commonly words containing a svarabhakti vowel require similar words for rhyme, but as John MacDonald, M.A., has pointed out, sometimes words containing a long vowel or diphthong can be used instead. Instances in the poems of Rob Donn and Lain Lom are referred to (S.G.S. ii. 73). In the Fernaig MS., folbh rhymes with òl and with thòs (M. MacFarlane, ed., pp. 263, 273).

481, 482. Cf. the version of An Fhìdeag Airgid printed by Ella Carmichael in the Celtic Review, i. 147, <gai>Air luing riomhaich nam ball airgid, / Le stiuir oir is da chrann airgid, / ’S cupuill oirr’ de shiod na Gailbhein.<eng> See also ll. 1737–40, here.

Silk from Galway (imported from Spain) is a cheville in popular seventeenth-century Scottish Gaelic verse, cf. Mary MacLeod on her chief’s arrows, ‘fledged from the plumage of the eagle and the silk of Galway’ <gai>(Is o shìoda na Gailbhinn),<eng> Gaelic Songs of Mary MacLeod, ed. J. C. Watson, p. 40.

485–6. Dunvegan and Duntuilm, frequently mentioned in old waulking songs, were the seats of the MacLeods of Harris and the MacDonalds of Sleat, in Skye. ‘Of no great height’; this implies that the son of the Earl of White Banners, whoever he was, lived in a grander castle than Dunvegan.


CIII

<gai>Ciad soiridh bhuam dhachaigh<eng>

(‘A hundred greetings homewards’)

Recorded from Mrs. Archie Munro (‘Màiri a’ Ghobha’) at Lochboisdale, South Uist, on 3/12/65 on tape.

We have not yet found any MS. or printed version of this song, or anyone else who knows it.


CIV

<gai>A Mhic Iain ’ic Sheumais<eng>

(‘O, son of Iain son of James’)

Recorded from Roderick MacKinnon (‘Ruairi Iain Bhàin’) at Northbay, Isle of Barra, in March 1938, on disc no. XV (4 couplets); from Calum Johnston in Edinburgh in November 1948 on disc no. LXXX (7 couplets); and from Mrs. Kate MacCormick (‘Bean Phàdraig’), Hacklett, Benbecula, on 21/11/49, wire no. 27. Previously I had recorded a version of the air from Mrs. MacLean, née Nicholson, at Beaver Cove, Cape Breton, on 1/10/37 on ediphone cylinder no. 104.

[TD 251]

MS. version: Carmichael Papers, printed here.

Printed versions: text alone, Òranaiche, p. 131 (24 couplets); K. C. C. 2 (17 couplets).

Text (10 couplets) and tune, communicated by Frances Tolmie, Gesto Collection, Appendices 58–9 (1895). Text (11 couplets, not 10 as enumerated, and a different version), translation, and air as sung by Harriet M’Vicar in North Uist in 1870, F. T., no. 93.

Text (27 couplets, of which 24 from the Revd. John N. MacDonald’s notebook in the National Library of Scotland), translation, and tune (this being Séamus Ennis’s transcription of the ediphone recording of Mrs. MacLean’s version referred to above), Donald A. Fergusson, Beyond the Hebrides, pp. 61–3.

A version of the air was printed in Donald MacDonald’s Collection of Ancient Martial Music of Caledonia, published in 1822, p. 117, in an Appendix of eleven ‘Airs Composed in the Islands of Uist and Skye’. The tempo is marked ‘very slow’. Mrs. Kennedy-Fraser printed another version of the air, with 2 couplets and the refrain, in S.H. iii. xvi; this was probably taken down from Calum Johnston. It is wrongly titled ‘Là Blàr na Léine’, but this was a different battle, fought between the MacDonalds of Clanranald and the Frasers at Loch Lochy in 1544.

Another version of the song has been published in Australia by Clement Hosking in his Fine Songs for Singing; this was taken down from the late Mrs. Susan Ferguson, Clachan, North Uist. (I am obliged for the information about this and about the version in Donald MacDonald’s Collection, to the Revd. William Matheson.)

F. Collinson has printed a transcription of Calum Johnston’s version of the air in The Traditional and National Music of Scotland, p. 92.

Five couplets and the refrain, sung by Ruairi Iain Bhàin’s daughter Ceit (Mrs. Buchanan), are on the long-playing disc of Scottish Gaelic Folk Songs published by the School of Scottish Studies in 1960.

This song differs from nearly all other waulking songs in that the story behind its composition is well known and attested. The subject is the wounding of Dòmhnall mac Iain ’ic Sheumais who led the MacDonalds in Uist in the successful affray against a raiding party of MacLeods at Carinish in North Uist in 1601. Accounts of the battle are to be found in Sir Robert Gordon’s Genealogical History of the Earldom of Sutherland, p. 244; the anonymous History of the Feuds and Conflicts among the Clans in the Northern Parts of Scotland and in the Western Isles (1780), p. 68; An Gàidheal, v. 309 (October 1876), ‘Blar Chairinnis’ le ‘Iain’; A. MacDonald and A. MacDonald, The Clan Donald, iii. 40–5 (1904); F. T. 256–7 (1911); Erskine Beveridge, North Uist, pp. 57–9. But by far the fullest account is that given by the Revd. John MacDonald (1830–68), a native of Benbecula and minister of Harris from 1855 until his death, to Captain F. W. L. Thomas, R.N., and quoted at length in a paper on ‘Three Churches in North Uist, Benbecula, and Grimsay, said to have been built in the Fourteenth Century’, read by Captain Thomas to the Society of Antiquaries of Scotland on 4/12/1871, and printed in vol. v of Archaeologia Scotica, pp. 231–7, after the quotation of the account by

[TD 252]

Sir Robert Gordon (1580–1656) who was living at the time of the event.

The Revd. John MacDonald must have been an active collector of Gaelic oral tradition. He contributed seventeen poems to K., of which seven were of his own composition; of these seventeen, nine appear in McD. Of the battle of Carinish, the Revd. John MacDonald told Captain Thomas that he ‘had not lost an opportunity of getting information regarding it for the last twenty years. I gathered it from the most trustworthy people in Uist; with some of these I have walked over the ground and had the plan of battle pointed out and explained to me. One of them gave me the account as he had it from his grandfather, who was a native of Carinish … he was accounted the best sennachie of his day. In fact, while I may say I have heard the account fifty or a hundred times, I have never heard it with any material difference.’ (1)

Briefly stated, the story is as follows: around 1601 hostilities had broken out between the MacLeods of Dunvegan (or Harris) and the MacDonalds of Sleat in consequence of Donald Gorm MacDonald of Sleat (who is frequently mentioned in old waulking songs) having repudiated his wife, who was the sister of Sir Rory MacLeod of Dunvegan. A raid by the MacLeods on MacDonald lands in Skye was followed by a retaliatory raid by the MacDonalds on the MacLeod land of Harris; whereon the MacLeods, forty men under the command of Dòmhnall Glas, a cousin of Sir Rory, raided North Uist, which then belonged to the MacDonalds of Sleat, and captured the goods and cattle which the MacDonalds had put into the sanctuary of the old church building called Teampull na Trianaid, and installed themselves there for the night.

Word of this raid quickly got to Donald MacDonald, ‘Dòmhnall mac Iain ’ic Sheumais’, then tenant of the island of Eriskay, who was grandson of James MacDonald of Castle Camus and second cousin of his chief Donald Gorm of Sleat. Donald travelled rapidly north with twelve retainers, picking up three more recruits on the way, and arrived at Carinish in North Uist after crossing both the south and north fords, while the forty MacLeod raiders were breakfasting within Teampull na Trianaid on a cattle beast they had captured and slaughtered. The MacDonalds were outnumbered by more than two to one, but they had the advantage in leadership, bowmanship, and surprise. In the words of the Revd. John MacDonald:

‘The M‘Leods had hitherto encountered no opposition, and had no idea that danger could be so near. Reasons were too obvious to the enemy why he should not fall upon them in the “temple”, and being well acquainted with every inch of the ground, he made the following disposition of his few men. Dividing them into three parties, he concealed the first, consisting of seven, behind the rising ground that faces the present mission-church, and to the south of the brook (now a drain) called “Feithe na fala”, i.e., the Ditch of Blood; the next division, four


(1) Erskine Beveridge attributed these words to Captain Thomas; but it is quite clear that they are part of the Revd. John MacDonald’s account.

[TD 253]

in number, he stationed behind a knoll about half way between the position of the first division and the “temple”; and the last, consisting of the remaining four, were appointed to give the alarm to the M‘Leods that “M‘Iain had arrived”. The men had been duly harangued, and each had received his instructions in the most definite terms; their leader then took an elevated position on the height a little to the north of where the preaching-house now stands, from whence he had soon the satisfaction of seeing his orders carried out to the very letter. The alarm given, out rushed the M‘Leods all in confusion, and before they were aware of what like the danger really was, four of their number were brought down by the cool aim of their enemies. The latter, after carrying out their orders so far, fell back with all speed upon the second party, and with them waited the approach of the foe. These, fancying they had only a handful to deal with, rushed on in the same confusion till they were checked by a second shower of arrows, which made eight of them reel back and bite the dust. The M‘Donalds now precipitated themselves upon the main body, and waited as before until the enemy was within range, when all suddenly springing up and letting fly a third discharge of arrows with the same well-regulated aim, and with the same galling effect, rushed across the hollow through which the road now passes, and took their position for the brunt of the day, a little below the place where their leader stood.

‘The upshot of these tactics was that the fight was reduced to one on level terms, in which the MacDonalds were victorious. Only five or six of the MacLeods escaped; Dòmhnall Glas was killed on a strand of Baleshare Island, which has ever since been called after him; his skull, distinguished by a sword gash, remained in a window of Teampull na Trianaid for many years. Towards the end of the fight Dòmhnall mac Iain ’ic Sheumais getting to close quarters with the enemy, had “received an arrow <gai>ann am beul a’ chuarain”,<eng> i.e. at the mouth of his <gai>cuaran<eng> or shoe, which threw him on his length in “Féithe na fala” . . . .

‘He was “conveyed to a house in Carinish with the arrow sticking in his flesh. He was very weak; and it was from the Old Castle of Borve, Benbecula, then inhabited, that a cake of barley-bread was procured to strengthen him. Anything else was out of the question, so wretched was the state to which “creachs”, and broils, and depredations had reduced the islanders. The arrow was extracted, but not without great difficulty and pain. The song sang by “Nic Coshem”, M‘Iain’s foster mother, to drown his cries, is now for the most part lost. She sang it extempore, at the head of a band of young women arranged around a “waulking” board. Knowing how passionately fond he was of a good song, she set up this demonstration to divert his attention while undergoing the operation. There was no chloroform in those days. The song commenced—

<gai>“Mhic Iain, a laoidh mo chéilleadh
Gur moch a chuala tu’n éibhe,” &c.<eng>

i.e. “Vich Iain, O darling of my soul, early didst thou hear my cry.”’

[TD 254]

This song is printed in MacD., 31. The circumstances of its composition are of interest as evidence of the extempore composition of waulking songs at the waulking hoard.

Erskine Beveridge says that it is understood that Dòmhnall mac Iain ’ic Sheumais later settled at Carinish. ‘His descendants continued to occupy Eriskay … for several generations, and it was in the house of this Donald’s great-great-grandson—Angus MacDonald, tacksman of Eriskay in 1745—that Prince Charles Edward spent his first night on Scottish soil after disembarking from the Doutelle.’

Dòmhnall mac Iain ’ic Sheumais was not long afterwards involved in the apprehension of the abominable Uisdean mac ’Ill’ Easbuig Cléirich, the murderer of the four MacVicar brothers in North Uist, and the instigator of the disastrous feud between the MacDonalds and the MacLeans of Duart which did so much damage to the Isles in the last quarter of the sixteenth century (see no. LXII in vol. ii; The Clan Donald, pp. 46–8). He was still alive in 1656, when the Synod of Argyll meeting at Iona on 28 May recorded a complaint from the Presbytery of Skye that the Vincentians Frs. Francis White and Dermid Dugan, and Frs. John MacDonald and James Cumine ‘had for the most part perverted’ Uist, Barra, Moidart, Knoydart, and Arisaig ‘and the chief men amongst them, as Captains Clanrannald elder and yonger, McNeill Barra and McIen vic Shemis and Glengaress whole familie, if not himself’ (though in fact the first two chiefs mentioned were converts of the earlier Irish Franciscan mission).

McD. contains, on pp. 32–4, three songs the composition of which is ascribed to Dòmhnall mac Iain ’ic Sheumais. Two are rowing songs, and one is a quern song. It is probable that the papers of the Revd. John N. MacDonald were the source, though this is not stated: but on p. x, in the list of Contents, it is revealed that the version of A Mhic Iain ’ic Sheumais, Tha do sgeul air m’aire printed in the Òranaiche came from this source. Of the Revd. J. N. MacDonald, the Revd. Angus MacDonald wrote in his Preface to K.

‘In the summer of 1852, John Norman Macdonald, then a Divinity student, afterwards Minister of Harris, collected a number of songs in Benbecula and entered them in a notebook. This MS was afterwards borrowed by John MacRury, now Minister of Snizort, who unwarrantably copied some of them and sent them to be printed in the Transactions of the Gaelic Society of Inverness. There is another small MS volume by the same collector of an earlier date. Both these were drawn upon for this volume, as well as several songs of the collector’s own composition. He collected much more Gaelic poetry which unfortunately is now lost, or at least cannot be traced.’

The poems, seven in number, including two waulking songs, were read to the Society on 27/2/1889 and printed in T.G.S.I. xv. 140–51. In fairness to the Revd. John MacRury it must be said that his acknowledgement (in Gaelic) is quite explicit: translated, it is ‘I found the following

[TD 255]

songs written in a little book left by the late Revd. John Norman MacDonald, who was minister of Harris. He wrote them down in Uist twenty-seven years ago.’ Since the notebook has been lost, we must be glad that the Revd. MacRury did publish them in this way.

With some diffidence I have ventured to print a composite text of the song, rather than relegate couplets from various sources to the notes. As numbered here, they occur in the various sources in the following order:

Òranaiche version, taken down by the Revd. J. N. MacDonald in the 1850s, probably in Benbecula: 1–7, 10, 22–7, 12, 13, 18–21, 31–3.

MS. version in Carmichael papers, almost certainly communicated before 1900: 1, 27, 3–6, 8–10, 7, 11–14, 18, 20–4.

Gesto Coll.: 1–7, 10, 22, 23.

Tolmie Collection: 1, 22, 23, 26–9, 14, 18–20.

K. C. Craig: 1, 27, 22, 23, 2, 3, 7, 5, 4, 6, 10, 18, 20, 21, 12, 16, 17.

Mrs. Kate MacCormick: 1, 22, 23, 3–6, 18, 21.

Calum Johnston: 1–7.

Roderick MacKinnon 1, 3, 5, 6.

498. <gai>salach,<eng> A. C., K. McC.; <gai>ballach,<eng> Òr., G., C. J.; <gai>na ballan,<eng> K. C. C.

501. <gai>do chuirp,<eng> Òr., A. C., G., C. J.; <gai>do chuim,<eng> K. C. C., K. McC. (latter also in l. 439).

506. <gai>na spreod / corp<eng> A. C.; <gai>na spreòd / Romh chorp<eng> K. C. C.; <gai>’n a spreol / An corp<eng> Òr., G., C. J. (the word spreol is not in Scottish Gaelic dictionaries).

511. <gai>Cha do ghabh thu ’m bristeadh,<eng> Òr.; <gai>Ged a ghabh thu bristeadh,<eng> A. C.; <gai>’S cha do ghabh thu ’m bristeadh,<eng> G.; <gai>Cuma nach do ghabh thu am bristeadh,<eng> K. C. C.

513–14. This couplet may be formulaic; it occurs twice in no. XX in vol. i, ll. 723–4 and 733–4.

515–16. Carmichael MS. <gai>An eilean Beag iosal / eadar Nial s Siol Ailean;<eng> K. C. C. <gai>A chuir iad ann an criochaibh / Eadar Niall is Sìol Ailein; Òranaiche Ann an eilein ìosal / Eadar Niall is Ailein.<eng> The Òranaiche version is adopted here, because it is known that Dòmhnall mac Iain ’ic Sheumais was living on Eriskay, which is between Barra, belonging to the MacNeils, and South Uist, belonging to the MacDonalds of Clanranald, the patronymic of whose chief was Mac ’ic Ailein.

518. <gai>gun bharrach<eng> Òr; <gai>ach barrach<eng> A. C.

520. <gai>Ach cluain am bi gaineamh<eng> F. T.

527. <gai>curaidh<eng> A. C., K. C. C., K. McC.; <gai>curach<eng> Òr., F. T.

528. <gai>thar chuan<eng> A. C.; <gai>air chuan<eng> Òr., F. T., K. C. C., K. McC.

532. <gai>No brath<eng> A. C., K. C. C.; <gai>Air mac<eng> Òr.

[TD 256]

538. <gai>Dh’fhas na speuran salach<eng> A. C.; <gai>Dh’fhàs na reultan salach<eng> K. C. C.; <gai>’S bha na reulta galach<eng> Òr., G.; <gai>’S bha na reultan ’gam falach<eng> F. T.; <gai>’S bha na reultan salach<eng> K., Mrs. McCormick.

543. <gai>Mu Mhac<eng> Òr.; <gai>’S è Mac<eng> F. T.

545. <gai>feileadh<eng> A. C.; <gai>féile<eng> Òr.; <gai>féileachd<eng> K. C. C.

546. <gai>eubhal<eng> A. C.; <gai>éibhil<eng> Òr. K. C. C.; <gai>éibhleag<eng> F. T.

549. According to Clan Donald, Dòmhnall mac Iain ’ic Sheumais called his sword ‘Cuig Mharg’ ‘because five merks was the price he paid for it’ (iii. 501); according to Alexander Carmichael, he called it ‘An Lannaire Riabhach’ or ‘An Ranaire Riabhach’ ‘the brindled peregrine’ or ‘the brindled roarer’ (C.G. ii. 317).

551. <gai>Rìgh Leòdhais<eng> = MacLeod of Lewis. Dòmhnall’s paternal great grandmother was a MacLeod of Lewis (Clan Donald, iii. 469).

553. His paternal grandmother was a MacLeod of Harris (Clan Donald, iii. 499). He was at war with the present chief, Sir Rory MacLeod.

556. <gai>Sìol Ailein mac Ruairidh<eng> = the MacDonalds of Clanranald. Dòmhnall mac Iain ’ic Sheumais’s mother belonged to this branch of the clan (Clan Donald, iii. 500).

The refrain printed here is that of the version of the song in the Carmichael Papers. Other versions are printed in the musical section. It will be noticed that there are small variations in the meaningless syllables.

The song was sung as set out in the version contributed to the Òranaiche by the Revd. J. N. MacDonald; the first couplet was sung once, followed by the short refrain, and each succeeding couplet was sung twice, followed the first time by the long refrain, the song ending with a repetition of the first couplet sung once only and followed by the long refrain, which ended the song.


CV

<gai>Ach a Mhaighread nan cuiread<eng>

(‘But, o tricky Margaret’)

Recorded from Roderick MacKinnon (‘Ruairi Iain Bhàin’) at Northbay, Barra, in March 1938, 8 couplets (disc III); from Mrs. A. J. MacLellan, Hacklett, Benbecula, on 21/11/49, wire recording no. 34, 14 couplets and again on 31/10/50; and from Mrs. Kate Nicholson, Iochdar, South Uist, when sung at a genuine waulking at Gerinish on 29/3/51, wire recording no. 640, 23 couplets, printed here.

MS. versions: D. C. M. 77, taken down before 1868, 14 couplets. Published by him in his Duanaire in 1868 with the omission of 2 couplets

[TD 257]

and the addition of 4 from an unknown source; his original transcription was published by J. L. Campbell in S.G.S. xi. 184 (1968).

Fr. Allan McDonald, fol. 119, 3 couplets of which the first does not appear to belong to the song. K. MS., p. 18, sixteen couplets, ‘taken down from the recitation of Mary MacDonald, Baleloch, North Uist, an old woman about 80’, and printed in McD. 240, with no reference to its source.

Canon MacLean, i. 28, 32 couplets from Màiri nighean Alasdair, Snaoiseabhal, South Uist. Printed here.

Other printed versions: the poem Fleasgach a’ Bhadain, printed by D. C. MacPherson on pp. 132–3 of his Duanaire contains 5 couplets that are usually associated with this song (D. C. M. 55–6), although the metre in this case is 6² + 6², not 6² + 5¹. The couplets correspond to lines, 565–6, 569–70, 561–2, and 567–8 here, in that order.

D. C. MacPherson ascribed the 16 couplets which he published in the Duanaire, only two of which might relate to the second part of the song, to the composition of Mary MacLeod, Màiri nighean Alasdair Ruaidh, the seventeenth-century Skye poetess (see vol. i, no. XVII). The late Professor J. C. Watson accepted this ascription without question, and included the Duanaire version of the song in his edition of the Gaelic Songs of Mary MacLeod, published in 1934, and reprinted by the Scottish Gaelic Texts Society in 1965, without comment, although meanwhile the text of the version (34 couplets) taken down by K. C. Craig had been published (p. 117, 1949). The fact that a version of the song had also been printed in McD. in 1911, was also overlooked in the reprint, and the ‘Notes on Mary MacLeod’ by the Revd. William Matheson, published in T.G.S.I. xli. 11–25 (1951) was not mentioned. See also J. L. Campbell, ‘Notes on the Poems ascribed to Mary MacLeod in D. C. MacPherson’s Duanaire’, S.G.S. xi. 171–91.

It may be pointed out that D. C.
