MacPherson collected most of the material in his Duanaire in the Braes of Lochaber, a long way from Mary MacLeod’s country, the Isle of Skye; also that it may well be significant that this song is not to be found in either of two collections strongly associated with Skye, that of Frances Tolmie or the Gesto Collection.

Printed version of the air: Mrs. Kennedy-Fraser, Songs of the Hebrides, vol. ii, p. xvii, gives a not very similar version of the air with refrain syllables and couplet beginning Ach a Mhairead nan cuiread taken down from Kirsty MacKinnon on Eigg, and headed ‘A Mary MacLeod Waulking Song’.

The version of this song taken down from Màiri nighean Alasdair by Canon MacLean in the 1930s and later—with two more couplets—by K. C. Craig, is incomparably the best. It appears to be composed of two songs; the first 20 couplets in K. C. C. are a spirited rebuttal by the anonymous poetess of the scandal one ‘tricky Margaret’ has been spreading about her; the last 14 are on the theme of unrequited love of the poetess for Ruairi Òg MacNeil of Barra. Nowhere is there any reference to a MacLeod. Some of the couplets in the latter part are formulaic. Probably traditional singers take it for granted that the same person made both parts of the song.

[TD 258]

The attractive air to which the song is sung in Barra and Uist does not seem to have been published hitherto.

570. Following this couplet, Mrs. Angus John MacLellan has <gai>Cha tog fidheall no clàrsach no pìob àrd air deagh-ghleus,<eng> ‘Neither fiddle nor harp will lift it, nor the tall well-tuned pipes’.

579–627. Canon MacLean, <gai>Ann an làth’rach an Tighearna;<eng> T underlined as if to question; K. C. C., <gai>Ann an làrach an Tighearna.<eng> Cf. Dinneen, láithreach, ‘presence’. The presence of their laird or chief appears to be indicated, in his judicial capacity.

591. Cf. the first couplet of no. CVI. In K. this couplet’s equivalent is followed by <gai>Le ghunn’ air a ghuallain Dol gu buaille (sic) Mhic Neill,<eng> ‘With his gun on his shouldier. Going to the cattlefold of MacNeil’ (of Barra, presumably).

601–45. Many chiefs of the MacNeils of Barra were called Ruairi, ‘Roderick’. If a historical person is involved, the Roderick MacNeil who succeeded his father Neil, son of Neil, before 1675 is a possibility. See Tales from Barra, p. 34.

632. Canon MacLean, <gai>’s dhad athair-se ’n éisg;<eng> K. C. C., <gai>’s dha t’athair sa, éisg.<eng>

641. Canon MacLean, <gai>’S tìr mór a cuir smala, mnathan geala ’cuir ghleus.<eng> K. C. C. <gai>Teine mór a’ cur smala ’s mnathan geala cur gleus.<eng> Mrs. A. J. MacLellan, <gai>Teine mór a’ cur smal dheth ’s mnathan geal’ a’ cur gréis. A’ cur gréis<eng> ‘embroidering’ seems to make better sense than <gai>a’ cur gleus<eng> ‘putting in order’.

643. Mrs. A. J. MacLellan <gai>’S iad ag òl an fhìon dhathte ’s a’ cur seachad d’a réir.<eng> (These lines in her second recording.)

657–60. Cf. the first 2 couplets of no. CVIII.

665–8. Cf. the fifth and sixth couplets of no. CVIII. Ruairi Iain Bhàin followed these couplets in his version by the seventh and eighth couplets of CVIII. The whole passage is clearly formulaic.


CVI

<gai>Thogainn fonn gun bhith trom<eng>

(‘I would sing a cheerful song’)

Recorded from Miss Kate MacDonald at Peninerine, South Uist, on 30/10/50, wire no. 466; also from Miss Penny Morrison, Iochdar, at Bornish on 1/5/58 on tape, 6 couplets. Miss Kate MacDonald’s text, a good one, is printed here.

[TD 259]

This is a good example of a song, belonging originally to the mainland (in this case Lochaber) tradition, being preserved in South Uist. Traditionally the composition of the song has frequently been attributed to Dòmhnall Donn mac Fear Bhoth-hunndainn, a member of a family connected with the MacDonalds of Keppoch, a well-known reiver of the seventeenth century, who is supposed to have been at feud with his own chief, to have killed the son of Iain Lom the famous royalist Gaelic poet, and to have been executed at Inverness by the Laird of Grant in 1692.

The traditional account of the circumstances of the composition of the present song is given in Gaelic by Hector MacDougall in an appendix to his edition of A. MacLean Sinclair’s Clàrsach na Coille (1928). Translated this reads:

‘It was when he (i.e. Dòmhnall Donn) lifted a spoil in Caithness that he made the song <gai>Thogainn fonn gun bhith trom,<eng> and what happened was that he lifted the “brown-haired girl” along with the spoil (of cattle). He had a companion with him called Lachuinn, and it seems that Lachuinn fell for the girl just as much as Dòmhnall Donn did. Lachuinn wanted to have the girl for himself, and he agreed that Dòmhnall Donn should have the entire spoil, if he got the girl. Dòmhnall Donn agreed to this; but the girl got an opportunity to escape from the inn where they were stopping for the night on the way home, for they were tired and sleepy. He (Lachuinn) lost both ways; he lost the girl and he lost the cattle.’

Hector MacDougall quotes 16 lines of the song as he had heard it, and implies that there were others not fitting for print.

MacLean Sinclair, who took down a 44-line version from John MacDonald ‘An Tàillear Abrach’ at Antigonish, Nova Scotia, in 1877 (Clarsach na Coille, 2nd edn., p. 223) accepted Dòmhnall Donn’s authorship of the song, and stated in The Gaelic Bards from 1411 to 1715 that Dòmhnall Donn had ‘a son by a girl in Sutherlandshire ’an nighean donn a bha ’n Catabh’ (p. 116).

The ascription of the song to Dòmhnall Donn was questioned by Colin Chisholm as long ago as 19/12/1877 in a paper read to the Gaelic Society of Inverness, where 16 lines of the song are given (T.G.S.I. vii. 56). He said ‘I may remark that the exploits of Dòmhnall Donn, as related in Lochaber, coincide with those of Alasdair Sgoileir, as related in the West of Rossshire’.

Dòmhnall Donn’s authorship was reasserted by Alexander Campbell in a paper on ‘Traditions of Lochaber’, read to the same society on 12/12/1905, where 4 lines are quoted; and in Story and Song from Loch Ness-side (1914) Alexander MacDonald, who gives a version of 28 lines and refrain (p. 84), refers to the ascription to ‘Alasdair Sgoilear’, but says that ‘the verses have always been locally sung as the composition of Donald Donn’.

However, Somhairle MacGill-Eain, M.A., in a paper on ‘Dòmhnall Donn of Bohuntin’ read to the Gaelic Society of Inverness on 9/4/54, where this and others of the songs connected with Dòmhnall Donn are

[TD 260]

critically discussed, remarks that ‘the weight of detailed tradition about “An nighean Donn a bha ’n Cataibh” inclines to Alasdair Sgoilear rather than to Domhnall Donn’.

The Revd. W. Matheson has pointed out to me that this Alasdair Sgoilear is not to be connected with the ‘Sgoilear donn na Beurla’ ‘the brown-haired scholar of English’ mentioned in vol. ii, ll. 1413 and 1677, and in this volume, l. 1165, as the title of ‘Sgoilear’ was bestowed on Alasdair in derision. But 1. 903 in this volume might refer to him.

K. C. Craig has a version of 46 lines from Màiri nighean Alasdair (p. 63). But the fact that a version of this song was printed (p. 118), as long ago as 1786 in the Gillies Collection has been overlooked. This is headed simply ‘North Highland Airs, No. 40’, referring to Patrick MacDonald’s Collection of Vocal Highland Airs, published in about 1780, where the air is titled <gai>‘Tha fonn gun bhi trom<eng>—I am disposed to mirth.’ But this is a different song, as the Revd. William Matheson has pointed out to me; the ascription of the tune by Gillies to the present song is an error. In the Gillies Collection it has 5 quatrains with a 4-line refrain, all of which correspond closely to similar verses and refrains in the MacLean Sinclair and A. MacDonald versions.

It is to be noted that in all the mainland and Nova Scotian versions the song is set out in quatrains with a 4-line refrain of which the first 2 lines correspond to the 2-line refrain here, and the third and fourth are versions of the last couplet but two in K. C. C., e.g. Colin Chisholm, <gai>Gruagach òg a’ chùil duinn, Dh’éireadh fonn orm ri t’fhaicinn.<eng> This also suggests very strongly that the song was not originally sung as a waulking song, and that the air to which it is sung in South Uist is not the original air.

Apart from this, there are 20 lines in the Nova Scotian version which are not found in the South Uist versions under consideration, though 12 of them are found in the Lochness-side version, 10 in the Gillies version, and 2 in Colin Chisholm’s version. The last two have 4 lines in common not found in the other versions. Altogether there are 74 different lines in the six versions under consideration, though ll. 677–8, and 701–4 here do not seem to be relevant.

670. K. C. C., <gai>aodail;<eng> A. M. S., <gai>’n fhaodail,<eng> so C. C.; Penny Morrison <gai>aoibh gheal,<eng> cf. C. G. iii. 124, <gai>A rioghainn aoibh-ghil nam flathas,<eng> also v. 138, <gai>’S i ’n ao-gheal donn.<eng> K. C. C. also has <gai>aodmhail<eng> in another song, p. 87, <gai>’S e an aodmhail i—tha rud aice.<eng>

679. K. C. C. has <gai>Beul gun sgòd labhradh foill.<eng>

683–4. He did not know she meant to try to escape.

694. K. C. C. <gai>Loch Carthann;<eng> A. M. S., McD., <gai>Lochabar.<eng>

699. K. C. C. <gai>air a’ chàrn;<eng> A. M. S., McD., <gai>air a’ chàl.<eng>

706. Penny Morrison has the additional couplet, of which a version is also found in K. C. C., <gai>Liom a’ chuibheall, liom an càrd, Liom a’ bhrà, liom a’ bhratach,<eng> ‘Mine the spinning-wheel, mine the wool-card, mine the quern, mine the banner’, referring to the reiver’s booty. For <gai>bratach<eng> K. C. C. has <gai>craiceann,<eng> ‘hide’.

[TD 261]

CVII

<gai>Dh’fhalbh mo rùn bho chionn seachdain<eng>

(‘My love a week ago departed’)

Barra version recorded by Miss Kathy Torrey from Mrs. Archie MacPhee (‘Maighread Shomhairle Bhig’), at Stoneybridge, South Uist, in August 1975, and kindly put at our disposal for publication. Cape Breton version (of Barra provenance) recorded on Ediphone from Mrs. Neil McInnis (‘Anna nighean Nìll’), Glace Bay, on 8/10/37.

This is another song with a semi-literary background. A version of 30 couplets was printed in E. (1776), p. 351. Another version, closely connected with the preceding, but having only 19 different couplets, was printed in M.T., viii. 104 (1899). This version is the same as that found in K., on p. 122, and in McD., on p. 196, except that the last omits, no doubt by mistake, a couplet occurring in the K.—M.T. version.

None of these versions is provided with a refrain. The E. version is headed ‘Irram a rinn te ga leannan ’n deidh, dho bean eille a posa’ (sic), i.e. ‘a rowing (or labour) song which a woman made to her lover after he had married another woman’. K. MS. gives the song a similar title. All the 19 couplets in this version correspond to couplets in E., and in the same order. In Mrs. McInnis’s version, which is much the best recovered from oral tradition, the following couplets correspond to couplets in E.; 6, 8, 10, 11, 17, 19–27.

The first 2 couplets of Mrs. McInnis’s version, which are characteristic of modern versions of the song, do not occur in E. Modern versions have also versions of the refrain that is printed here. The earliest is that printed in An Gàidheal, xiii. 69 from Miss Johann MacInnes’s Mod Prize Collection, 1928. This has 9 couplets, of which 4 are found in E., and is provided with a sol-fa transcription of the tune. In 1949 a version taken down by Dr. Calum MacLean on Raasay was printed in T.G.S.I. xxxix–xl. 189. This has also 9 couplets of which 3 are in common with E., and 2 others with the Barra and Cape Breton versions printed here. Another version, recorded on Skye for the School of Scottish Studies, is printed in an Appendix to Colm Ó Baoill’s edition of the poems of Sìleas na Ceapaich. This has 7 couplets, of which 5 (all to do with the influential relations of the subject of the song) are equivalent to couplets in E. and in the Cape Breton version, and another occurs in the Barra, Cape Breton, and Raasay versions.

In passing, it may be said that the Johann MacInnes and Raasay versions provide a proper reading for an absurd misprint in the fourth couplet of the E. version, which reads

<gai>Bhigh an ruadhag sa ghlaoich,
Aig mo ghaolsa na bhreachan.<eng>

In K. this is
<gai>Bhiodh an ruaghag ’s a ghlaodhaich,
Aig mo ghaolsa fo bhreacan.<eng>

[TD 262]

which presumably would mean ‘my love would have the roe deer and its cry beneath his plaid’. The corresponding couplet in the version preserved by Dr. Calum MacLean shows that the <gai>ghlaoich<eng> of E. is a misprint for <gai>laogh aic’,<eng> and that the meaning is ‘the roe deer and her fawn’.

The story behind the song is perfectly clear. It is addressed by a girl to her lover who has deserted her and married another. It describes (1) his prowess as a hunter, (2) his handsome appearance in his fine clothes, (3) his connections with the best-born families, (4) his desertion, for which his sweetheart reproaches him bitterly, suggesting that he should leave his wife, whom she says is worthless. Much of this is cast in language which is virtually formulaic.

This leads one to question the tradition referred to by Colm O Baoill and by John MacInnes (S.G.S. xi. 14) according to which the song was supposed to have been composed by Sìleas na Ceapaich to Lachlann mac Theàrlaich Òig (MacKinnon). This looks like a folk attempt to localize the song in Skye. Sìleas na Ceapaich was too competent a poetess to need to resort to such formulaic language. It is significant that E., edited by a MacDonald, makes no such ascription, and that in K., written by another MacDonald, the poem is described as ‘anonymous’. Neither of these MacDonalds would have been backward in claiming authorship for Sìleas na Ceapaich, a fellow MacDonald, had that been part of the clan tradition.

Refrain. Mrs. Archie MacPhee sang it in this form (ab), Mrs. Neil McInnis in the form aba.

715–16. Mrs. Archie MacPhee sang this couplet also after the third couplet, but beginning <gai>’Gad éigheach.<eng> The couplet seems more appropriate here.

732. Cf. K. C. C. 30, <gai>’S brògan dubh’ a’ bhuinn eutrom,<eng> ‘black shoes with light soles’ in another song. Martin Martin says in his Description of the Western Islands of Scotland that ‘The generality now wear Shoes, having one thin Sole only, and shaped after the right and left Foot’ (instead of the hide shoes formerly worn) (2nd edn., 1716, p. 207).

735. Martin Martin says that ‘Many of the People wear Trowis: some have them very fine woven like Stockings of those made of Cloth; some are colour’d, and others striped: the latter are as well shaped as the former, lying close to the Body from the middle downwards, and tied around with a Belt above the Haunches’ (ibid. 206–7).

733. Martin Martin says that at the Michaelmas cavalcade in North Uist—and doubtless elsewhere—‘the Men have their Sweet-hearts behind them on horse-back, and give and receive mutual Presents; the Men present the Women with Knives and Purses, the Women present the Men with a pair of fine Garters of divers Colours’. Cf. vol. i, ll. 995–8.

741–50. This passage may be an interpolation. It is not in E. or K.

[TD 263]

742. Aros is in Mull, in the middle on the island, on the Sound of Mull.

751. The corresponding couplet (XVII) in E is:

<gai>Bha do chairdeas gu direach
Ris na Rioghridh bh’air sasunn.<eng>

‘Your relationship was direct with the dynasty that ruled England.’ There is a similar couplet in K. This implies that the person addressed in the poem was a MacDonald; the Lords of the Isles were connected with the royal Stuart family through the marriage of John of the Isles to Princess Margaret, daughter of King Robert II, in 1350.

The second line here seems out of place, possibly an anticipation of l. 762. See note thereon.

754. Iain Lom mentions the Lamonts and the MacLachlans together three times, ll. 343, 1276, 2185 (as royalist clans; see ed. Annie M. MacKenzie). They are both situated in Cowal, Argyllshire, and the MacLachlans are traditionally believed to have acquired their lands there by marriage with a Lamont heiress.

756. E., K., and the version printed in Bàrdachd Shìlis na Ceapaich all have <gai>Shìol Ailpein,<eng> i.e. descendants of Kenneth MacAlpine, progenitor of Scottish kings. A number of small clans claimed this status, particularly the MacGregors.

757. There was no earldom of Islay, but the Lords of the Isles having acquired the earldom of Ross by the marriage of Donald, son of Reginald, to Lady Mary Leslie at the beginning of the fifteenth century, are often called the ‘earls of Islay’ in Gaelic poetry.

759. ‘MacDhòmhnaill’ is another title of the Lords of the Isles, as chiefs of Clan Donald.

762. Martin Martin says that ‘The Natives call this Isle [i.e. Canna] by the Name of Tarsin at Sea’ (cf. l. 722). The name was probably bestowed because the island lies east and west. Nominally church land, it was occupied by the MacDonalds of Clanranald. In the present couplet E. has <gai>eillannainuibh farising<eng> (sic) ‘far-flung islands’; K. likewise; <gai>Bàrdachd Shìlis na Ceapaich, nan eileanan fada<eng> ‘of the long islands’. This would date the poem to after the acquisition of Lewis by the MacKenzies, which took place in 1610. Mr. Fred MacAulay, Senior Gaelic Producer of the B.B.C., who is from North Uist, informs me that the tradition in his family is that they originally came from <gai>Na h-Eileanan Tarsuinn,<eng> which he was told in Benbecula, referred to Coll and Tiree.

763. The MacDougalls are connected with the Lords of the Isles through their descent from Dougall, son of Somerled. See Alexander MacBain’s notes to Skene’s The Highlanders of Scotland, p. 411.

[TD 264]

CVIII

<gai>Chunnaic mi an t-òg uasal<eng>

(‘I saw the young noble’)

Recorded from Mrs. Mary Morrison (‘Bean Phluim’) at Castlebay, Barra, on 29/12/49, wire recording no. 115.

Apparently unpublished. Other version, C.J. 47, (15 couplets).

Another song on the subject of unrequited love. Not to be confused with no. CXVIII, with which it has some passages in common; as it also has with some in the second section of CV.

786. C. J., <gai>ialdag,<eng> ‘bat’.

793. C. J., <gai>sgorrlach<eng> (not in dictionaries and not rhyming).

799. C. J., <gai>Nuair a thigeadh an fhras / Gum bithinn paiste fo d’ sgeith.<eng>

801. C. J., <gai>Nuair a rachadh i seachad / Sgaoilte mach e ri grein.<eng>

805. C. J., <gai>Gun bhi creidsinn nan gillean / Air cho milis am beul,<eng> ‘Not to be believing the lads / No matter how sweet their talk’.


CIX

<gai>O, daonnan tha mi deònach<eng>

(‘O, always I am willing’)

Recorded from Miss Catherine MacMillan (‘Catriana Dhòmhnaill ’ic Nìll’ ‘Catriana Mhakaroff’), then aged 82, at Cliat, Barra, but brought up on the small island of Sandray, on 4/5/37, ediphone cylinder 34. Transcription of words later checked by Miss Annie Johnston, who recorded the air for the Linguistic Survey of Scotland in 1949. Two verses and the refrain of her version, with translation, and musical transcription, are printed in Tocher, 13: 200, in an issue devoted to the memory of herself and her brother Calum.

Art version: the air and refrain, taken down from Mrs. MacLean, Barra, were used by Mrs. Kennedy-Fraser in Songs of the Hebrides, i. 60, to ‘Gaelic verses collected by Kenneth MacLeod’.

The full text of this song does not seem to have been printed anywhere, nor have we found any version in the MS. collections we have seen. The first 4 couplets occurred only in Catriana Mhakaroff’s version, and were not known to Miss Annie Johnston; the transcription of the first couplet is doubtful.

Unfortunately, the ediphone cylinder on which the song had been recorded was scraped before the tune had been transcribed.

[TD 265]

The last 11 couplets of the song obviously relate to South Uist, but we have not found anyone there who knows it. The subject appears to be a ‘thigging’ expedition. Cf. Kirkwood, ed. J. L. Campbell, A Collection of Highland Rites and Customes, ‘To thig is to beg assistance of Friends which is very ordinary among persons of every Quality.’ See also the poem describing the exactions of some thiggers in Scottish Verse from the Book of the Dean of Lismore, ed. W. J. Watson, p. 67.

816. Various younger sons of the chiefs of Clan Mackintosh were called Allan. The most prominent was Allan Mackintosh of Torcastle who married Janet, third daughter of the eighth Lord Lovat; see Wardlaw MS., p. 108. He is said to have been the progenitor of the Sliochd Ailein (‘race of Allan’) and to have ‘dwelt chiefly at Balcherrinachy in Stratherrick, and died on 20th February 1476’ (MacFarlane, Genealogical Collections, i. 201).

817. Presumably Borve in Benbecula.

823. Kilphedir is in the south part of South Uist.

825. Mr. John MacMillan, Bornish House, informs me that there is a Port Sheumais in Loch Eynort, but the name is recent (post-1915). This kind of name could be bestowed on a tiny creek where anyone called Seumas had kept a small boat, anywhere.

831. The family most prominently associated with Arivullin of Milton was that of the MacDonalds, of which Flora MacDonald was a member; but they left early in the nineteenth century. The wife of some later tacksman may be referred to here.


CX

<gai>Dh’fhalbh mo rùn ’s dh’fhàg e ’n caladh<eng>

(‘My love has gone, he’s left the harbour’)

Recorded for us from Mrs. Fanny McIsaac, Torlum, Benbecula, by the Revd. John MacCormick on 27/11/53. Apparently unpublished. The Revd. William Matheson has very kindly allowed us to print the text of the version known to him from North Uist in addition to Miss McIsaac’s version.


CXI

<gai>O, ’s fhada bhuainn Anna<eng>

(‘O, far from us is Anna’)

Recorded from Mrs. Mary Morrison (‘Bean Phluim’) on disc no. XLI at Northbay, Barra, in March 1938, and again at Castlebay on wire, no. 105, on 29/12/49, on the second occasion with chorus.

[TD 266]

Apparently unpublished. There is another version, of 11 couplets, in the Barra MS., which contains three couplets not sung by Mrs. Morrison. As these obviously belong to the song, they are included here, being ll. 855–8 and 891–2. In the Barra MS. the order of the couplets as printed here is 10, 9, 17, 20, 21, 18, 19, 1, 2, 5, 7.

The Revd. William Matheson has given us some very interesting information regarding the identity of the lady who is the subject of the song. He writes: ‘First of all she was of Clann Néill Bhàin, which is what the MacLeans of Boreray were called in North Uist. In view of the suitors she had, I don’t think she can have been anyone less than a daughter of MacLean of Boreray. Then, again, we are told that her mother was from Harris; and the only MacLean of Boreray who has a wife from Harris was John MacLean, who died at the age of nearly 80 in 1704 (deposition by his grandson in 1766). He was factor of North Uist and Sheriff-depute of the Western Isles. Finally, the name of the subject of the song was Anne, and John MacLean had a daughter Anne, his wife from Harris being Marion (Mór ni’ Choinnich), daughter of Kenneth Campbell, tacksman of Strond. Anne married John MacDonald of Castleton, Skye, in 1690.’

804. <gai>Tobhta hamarr,<eng> so pronounced. Cf. Dwelly, p. 77, <gai>tobhta amar.<eng> The Barra MS. has <gai>tobhta tarruing.<eng>

807. <gai>Borghraidh.<eng> Gh silent, giving rise to a svarabhakti vowel between two r’s. Cf. stararaich.

872. MacLeod of Bernera. The founder of the family was Sir Norman MacLeod, third son of Sir Rory Mór MacLeod of Dunvegan, fifteenth chief of the MacLeods of Harris. Sir Norman, who was born about 1610, was a very important person in the councils of the clan, and had a distinguished career as a royalist officer in the civil wars, fighting at Inverkeithing and Worcester. He died in 1705, and is the subject of an elegy in the Book of Clan Ranald. See J. Carmichael Watson, Gaelic Songs of Mary MacLeod, p. 138.

875. <gai>Mac Fhir Locha Beòraich;<eng> not identified.

877. <gai>Mac Fhir Locha Mhùideart,<eng> the son of MacDonald of Kinlochmoidart. The family was a branch of the Clanranalds, Jacobites whose representatives fought at Killiecrankie, Sheriffmuir, and Culloden.

879. <gai>Mac Fhionghuin a’ chléirich<eng> may be a corruption of <gai>Mac Fhionghuin a’ Chréithich<eng> referred to elsewhere, cf. vol. ii, l. 141, and K. C. C. 44, <gai>‘Mac Fhionghainn o’n Chréithich’.<eng> MacKinnon of Strath in Skye.

880. <gai>Le chuilbheirean geura.<eng> Calievers, not culverins. See Éigse, ii. 44.

881. In 1750 the Baillie or Chamberlain of Kintyre was Archibald Campbell of Limecraigs. See Lindsay and Cosh, Inveraray and the Dukes of Argyll, p. 367 n. 246.

[TD 267]

883. <gai>Mac ’ic Dhùbhghaill<eng> is the patronymic of the MacDonalds of Morar, another branch of the Clanranalds.

886. Baron, in the Scots sense, a baron-bailie, ‘the deputy of the baron in a burgh of barony’ (Chambers).

887. Barra MS., <gai>tighearna na diuca.<eng>

890. Possibly this implies that her mother was of the family of MacLeod of Harris (Dunvegan).

894. Castle Kismul in Barra, home of the MacNeils of Barra, many of whose sons in the direct line were called ‘Ruairi’ (Roderick). This allusion may date the song to the extent that the MacNeils of Barra gave up living in Kismul Castle (on a little island) early in the eighteenth century.


CXII

<gai>O, mo nighean dubh, till, till<eng>

(‘Ho, my black-haired girl, come back’)

Recorded from Miss Elizabeth Sinclair (‘Ealasaid Iain Dhunnchaidh’) on Vatersay in June 1949 on the ediphone, one of the last ediphone recordings that I made.

A fragment, apparently unpublished.

903. <gai>Sgoilear Donn na Beurla.<eng> Possibly the well-known seventeenth-century reiver, Alasdair Sgoilear. See ll. 1413 and 1678 of vol. ii, and the general notes to song CVI of this volume.


CXIII

<gai>Clò Mhic ’ille Mhìcheil<eng>

(‘The cloth of Mac ’ille Mhìcheil’)

The tune of this song was recorded from the late Captain Donald Joseph MacKinnon (son of Roderick MacKinnon, ‘Ruairi Iain Bhàin’, Bruernish, Barra) at Lochboisdale on 31/10/51. The text was originally only known from a fragment of 5 couplets and the refrain on p. 134 of Gaelic MS. LXIII of the National Library of Scotland, a MS. which consists of poems by the well-known Jacobite poet, Alexander MacDonald ‘Alasdair mac Mhaighstir Alasdair’ (1700–70). The following eight pages of the MS. are missing; allowing for 11 couplets per page, the rest of the song as later recovered could have filled 3 of them.

Captain MacKinnon knew the song, but not an independent version of the text. The MS. LXIII text had been published in conventional

[TD 268]

spelling by Professor Donald MacKinnon in the Celtic Review, v. 24; the original MS. LXIII text was printed by J. L. Campbell in S.G.S. iv. 81, in 1935. Meanwhile Professor MacKinnon’s transliteration had been reprinted, with a translation, by the Revd. A. MacDonald and the Revd. A. MacDonald in their edition of the Poems of Alexander MacDonald in 1924, p. 156, with the addition of a couplet corresponding to ll. 961–2 here, which the editors say ‘is a traditional couplet not in MS. 63’, though they do not name the source.

In conventional spelling, the MS. LXIII fragment is as follows:

<gai>Òganaich ùir a’ chùil teudaich,
’S oil liom eudach a bhith dhìth ort.
Hùg air clò mhic ’ille Mhìcheil,
O hùgaibh, hùg a ri hug,
Hùg air clò mhic ’ille Mhìcheil.

Gun chuir Albainn clò am beart duit,
’S nuair thig e as, cha b’i an t-sìth ì.

Bidh e fighte, cumta, luaidhte,
Mus dig oirnn buain na h-Éill Mhìcheil.

Gheobh mise culaidh ’ga shuathadh,
Ma tà gruagaichean ’san rìoghachd.

Gum bi do chlò ruadh-sa luaidhte
Le gaoir, fuil, is fual ’ga stìopadh.<eng>

The rest of the song appeared to be hopelessly lost; but around 1949, while looking for the MacCormick MS. (see vol. i) in the papers of Alexander Carmichael, in Edinburgh University Library, I came across the version that is printed here (after first appearing in An Gàidheal of May 1951), written on a sheet of paper in pencil in the handwriting of Ella Carmichael.

Nothing is said there about the provenance of the text; if it was actually preserved by oral tradition, it is a remarkably good one. It is here translated and annotated in full for the first time.

The style is clearly that of Alexander MacDonald, and the poem, which like the rest of his authenticated Jacobite poetry was written after Culloden to inspire the Highlanders to another attempt to throw off the shackles of the Hanoverian regime (probably the rising contemplated for 1751) compares the pounding and thumping of the cloth on the waulking board to the treatment George II’s redcoats will receive when all the Highlanders rise together to avenge the treatment they have received after the ’45. But having written another waulking song on the same subject, the well-known Agus hó Mhórag, MacDonald did not include Clò Mhic ’ille Mhìcheil in his book of poems which he published in 1751 under the title of Ais-eiridh na Sean Chánoin Albannaich.

For Alexander MacDonald himself, see Bishop Forbes, The Lyon in Mourning (Scottish History Society first series); William MacKay,

[TD 269]

‘Presbyterial Notices of Mac Mhaighstir Alasdair’, T.G.S.I. xi. 171; the Revd. A. MacDonald and the Revd. A. MacDonald, The Poems of Alexander MacDonald; J. L. Campbell, ‘Alexander MacDonald, Portrait of a Traditionalist’, Scots Magazine, October 1935; ‘The First Printed Gaelic Vocabulary’, ibid., October 1938; and Highland Songs of the ’45. Details of MacDonald’s career as an S.P.C.K. schoolmaster in Ardnamurchan can be found in the early Minutes of the S.P.C.K. in Scotland, which are still unpublished.

906. The singer, who was from Barra, used the form <gai>aodach,<eng> which does not rhyme.

910. MS. LXIII, <gai>na hillmhichel,<eng> corresponding to the colloquial usage.

914. MS. LXIII <gai>da stíopadh,<eng> cf. ibid., p. 171, <gai>fág aig an tuil iad da stipidh,<eng> ‘leave them to be soaked in the flood’.

917. i.e. the MacDonalds of Sleat.

919. The MacDonalds of Clanranald.

922. The MacDonalds of Glengarry.

923. The MacDonalds of Keppoch.

926. The MacDonalds of Glencoe.

928. Alexander MacDonald, fifth earl of Antrim. His forebear had sent Alasdair Mac Colla with his army to help Montrose in 1644. His horses are also mentioned in Ais-eiridh, p. 102, <gai>Antram lu’-chleasach na’n seang-each.<eng>

931. The MacLeans. The absence of any internal rhyme from this couplet suggests that there has been a mistake in copying here at some time, and that the first line of one couplet has been combined with the second line of a following one. <gai>Spuaghail<eng> is written in Ella Carmichael’s version as an alternative to <gai>luadhadh.<eng>

933. i.e. the Camerons.

935. The MacPhersons.

937. The MacRaes.

939. The skirmish at Clifton, near Penrith, fought on 18/12/1745 (old style), in which the MacPhersons were prominent. According to Captain John Daniel, ‘The enemy’s advanced Guard, commanded by General Honywood, having got somewhat before us, planted himself in ambush near the road we were to march, and from behind the hedges expected, as the army approached to cut them off; but there the biter was bit: for though we came unknowingly upon them, we had the good fortune to receive their attack in good order, killed and drowned many of them, and put the rest to flight: General Honywood himself

[TD 270]

narrowly escaped, having lost his arm, and severely wounded’ (‘John Daniel’s Progress’, in Origins of the Forty-Five, S.H.S., 2nd ser., 2, pp. 185–6). This is the only contemporary account of the ’45 I have seen that mentions Honywood. In the Army List for 1745, Sir Philip Honywood Gen.-Col. is entered as the commanding officer of the King’s Own Regiment, Light Horse. There is also a Lt.-Col. Phil. Honywood in the King’s Own Regiment of Dragoons. Both regiments are entered as serving in Flanders.

943. The Grants.

949. The Campbells of Breadalbane. They had been Jacobites in 1715.

951. The MacNeils of Barra. See J. L. Campbell and Constance Eastwick, ‘The MacNeils of Barra in the Forty-Five’, Innes Review, xvii. 82–90.

953. The MacKinnons from Strath in Skye.

955. The Robertsons of Struan.

957. The MacNabs.

959. The Stewarts of Appin.

961. The Mackintoshes. The ‘traditional couplet’ printed by the editors of The Poems of Alexander MacDonald that corresponds to this is:

<gai>Thig bannal a nall a Cataobh,
’S bheir iad caitein air mu’n till iad,<eng>

translated,

From Sutherland a troop will come
who’ll give it a nap before they return.

This again is reminiscent of a couplet in Agus hó Mhórag:

<gai>S ioma Cló air ’n tug iad caittein,
Eadar Cattamh, agus Anuinn.<eng>

Many a cloth on which they raised a nap
Between Sutherland and Annan. (Ais-eiridh, p. 102.)

965. Angus MacDonald of Borrodale, in Arisaig. The MacDonalds of Borrodale were closely connected with the MacDonalds of Glenaladale, themselves a branch of the Clanranalds. Angus MacDonald’s was the first house Prince Charles entered on the mainland of Scotland after landing on the mainland of Scotland on 25 July 1745; in April 1746 the Prince made his way there after Culloden, and on the 25th of that month wrote his farewell letter to the Highland chiefs from there. The next day he sailed for Uist in the eight-oared boat provided by Angus MacDonald.

The Prince returned to Borrodale on 10 July, before which date Borrodale’s house had been burnt by the Hanoverians. The Prince

[TD 271]

continued on his wanderings escaping from the Hanoverian troops and militia, and it was from Borrodale that he embarked for France on 19 September 1746.

The authors of The Clan Donald were of the opinion that Angus MacDonald of Borrodale was the author of the anonymous account of the ’45 in the Lockhart Papers; but this is wrong; internal evidence makes it clear that the account was written by Alexander MacDonald, who composed this song.

982. <gai>Mac na strìopaich<eng> = the Duke of Cumberland. Cf. Ais-eiridh, p. 104: <gai>Braon do lao’d do thrócaireachd, / A dh’órdaich Dia na fírinn / Cha’n eil an uchd an F——r, Mac D—sa ris an st——ch.<eng> ‘The least drop of mercy / which God of verity ordered / Is not in the breast of the Butcher / George’s son by the harlot.’

983. Presumably the MacDonalds of Sleat and the MacLeods of Dunvegan are uppermost in mind here.


CXIV

<gai>’S muladach, ’s muladach, thà mi<eng>

(‘Sorrowful, sorrowful, am I’)

Recorded from Mrs. Neil Campbell (‘Bean Nìll’) at Frobost, South Uist, on tape on 11/10/58.

Other versions: Tune, refrain, and 1 couplet, recorded from Peigi MacRae, Glendale, South Uist, published by Margaret Fay Shaw in Studia Memoriae Bélae Bartók Sacra, 1956, p. 442.

Text alone, 19 couplets, K. C. C. 86.

A version of this song was recorded on Eriskay from Mrs. Donald MacInnes (‘Mór Dhunnchaidh’) on my ediphone by the Revd. Fr. John MacCormick in the autumn of 1947. It has 11 couplets.

In Barra a modern topical song with words composed by Dòmhnall mac ’ic Lachlainn is sung to this air. It begins:

<gai>Thog sinn oirnn gu tighinn dha’n dùthaich,
Bha ’n ceò dùmhail air na beannan.<eng>

A version of this was recorded from Mrs. Mary MacNeil (‘Mairi Ruarachain’) at Castlebay on 29/12/49. It has 9 couplets describing the return of a fishing-boat and its crew to Barra in the mist, and their hospitable reception in the house of a friend after landing. The words of eight couplets and the refrain, and a sol-fa transcription of the tune, are in A. J. (2), pp. 97–8.

993. K. C. C. <gai>Turas mo chreicheadh.<eng> So Marion MacInnes.

999. K. C. C. <gai>Cola-diag romh Oidhche Choinnleadh.<eng>

1005–10. Cf. <gai>Òran Cuartachaidh,<eng> ‘Circuiting Song’, C.G. iv. 90–3.

[TD 272]

CXV

<gai>O hao, O hao, ’s mì fo mhìngean<eng>

(‘O hao, o hao, I’m displeased’)

Three couplets and the refrain of this song were recorded from Mrs. Patterson (‘Ceat nighean Ailein ’ic Chaluim’), née MacNeil, at Benacadie, Cape Breton, on 4/10/37. In a search for a fuller version, one of 7 couplets was recorded from Mr. John MacInnes, M.B.E., by myself at Loch Carnan, South Uist, on 10/4/51, and another of 6 couplets from Miss Fanny McIsaac by the Revd. John MacCormick in the winter of 1953–4, these two versions together comprising 10 different couplets.

MS. versions. The oldest of these is probably that in the Morison MS., taken down on Mull; this has 14 couplets. A closely related version, also of 14 couplets, was taken down by D. C. MacPherson, probably in the 1860s, possibly in Lochaber or western Inverness-shire.

On 18 March 1949, the late Monsignor P. J. Nicholson, President of St. Francis Xavier University, Antigonish, Nova Scotia, sent me a MS. version communicated to him by James H. MacNeil, of Sydney, but formerly of Iona, Cape Breton, who had received it from Mr. Joseph MacKinnon, also of Sydney. This also contains 14 couplets (plus one repeated). Mr. Joseph MacKinnon wrote that ‘It [the song] has been kept up at Rear Big Pond, and at Glen Garry, Cape Breton, since ever my two great grandmothers, brought it over from Barra, some century and a quarter back’—i.e. around 1825.

Printed versions: text alone, John MacFadyen, Sgeulaiche nan Caol, 1902, pp. 15–16, 9 couplets; K. C. C. 101, 12 couplets.

The tune does not seem to have been published, nor has the song been edited or translated before, so far as I know. The versions of John MacInnes, M.B.E., Miss Fanny McIsaac, Joseph MacKinnon, and D. C. MacPherson are printed here.

Three distinct themes occur in the various versions of the song: (1) description of a reaping contest in the Lowlands; (2) description of a sea voyage from Coll to Uist, passing the Point of Sleat and other places on the south-west coast of Skye; and (3) couplets from a Jacobite drinking-song; apparently (3) only occurs in the versions of Fanny McIsaac and Màiri nighean Alasdair.

In the earliest versions of the song, in the Morison MS. and in D. C. M., the sole subject involved is the sea voyage, and the Morison MS. gives the name of the author of the song as Niall Bàn MacIonmhuinn, Croig, originally from Caolas, Coll. (Croig is beside Loch Cuan in the north-west of Mull; Caolas is a farm near the south end of Coll.) The first reference to reaping (or picking nuts) is in a single couplet in the Sgeulaiche nan Caol version, all the rest of which is devoted to the sea voyage. This is the last but one, and reads:

<gai>Gu’m bu slàn gu’n till an t-òigear
Bha ’n coill nan cnò ’ga gearradh,<eng>

[TD 273]

which can be considered a variant of the first couplet of the Uist and Benbecula versions, and of the second of the Cape Breton (originally Barra) version. It is only in the Cape Breton version that the reaping contest theme is remembered at any length. In his comments on the song, Mr. Joseph MacKinnon pictures the girl as working in the reaping contest, which she loses through accidentally cutting her hand, and thinking back to a summer when she was picking nuts in a wood with friends, and also of the young man who has left her; the young man takes up the song to describe the sea voyage on which he has gone, and ending by also thinking back to the nut-gathering in the wood. This is probably the Barra tradition regarding the song, taken to Cape Breton, but it must be a folk rationalization made to connect the two different themes in it.

Mrs. Patterson’s recollection of the song was limited to the third, fourth, and fifth couplets of the Joseph MacKinnon version.

The song can be dated, from the allusion made to the Fear Ruadh who gave a dram to the members of the crew at the end of the voyage in the Morison, MacPherson, and Sgeulaiche nan Caol versions. John MacFadyen says in Sgeulaiche nan Caol that this was the laird of Coll, who had been on the boat during the voyage. John MacLean in his poem in praise of Colonel Alasdair MacLean of Coll, in his Orain Nuadh Ghaedhlach, 1818, refers to him as Am Fear Ruadh;

<gai>’S mairg san am sin chuireadh greann air,
Thogail angair an fhir ruaidh<eng>

‘Pity him then who would irritate him, to rouse the anger of the red-headed man’ (p. 18).

Hector MacDougall refers to him as Alasdair Ruadh in his edition of John MacLean’s poems (1928, p. 2 n.), and says that he died in 1835 (p. 5 n.). He succeeded to the estate in 1790, and retired by 1823, so Niall Bàn MacKinnon’s song must have been composed between those dates. It must have reached Barra quickly, as emigration from Barra to Cape Breton ceased after 1835.

1015. <gai>Eala Bhàn<eng> is not capitalized in K. C. C., the only other context, but surely it must be the name of the boat.

1019, 1021, 1023. See notes to ll. 1081, 1083, and 1085.

1029. <gai>Nan gartan rìomhach.<eng> This implies that he had female admirers, see note to l. 733.

1035. It is just possible that this couplet is a kenning for Prince Charles Edward (considering the general context). Although Sunart was not a district he entered during his post-Culloden wanderings, he had passed by Loch Shiel to Glenfinnan not long after he landed on the mainland in 1745.

1042. In the late eighteenth and early nineteenth centuries many Highlanders went to do harvest work in the Lowlands.

[TD 274]

1053. <gai>Di-Sathuirne.<eng> Various days are assigned for the start of the voyage. The Morison MS. has ‘Saturday’; D. C. MacPherson has ‘Monday’; and Sgeulaiche nan Caol, ‘Tuesday’.

1069. <gai>Rubh’ an Àilein.<eng> The Morison MS. has <gai>Ruth-an-fhasaich;<eng> neither has been identified. On this voyage, one would expect <gai>Rubh’ Àrasaig,<eng> the Point of Arisaig.

1071. Niall Bàn MacKinnon himself? In the Morison MS. this verse is entered in the margin, without a number.

1073. Morison MS. <gai>Dol seachad a’n Carn bha sgriob aic’,<eng> cf. <gai>Sgrìob<eng> in Fr. Allan McDonald’s Gaelic words from South Uist (‘sailing on the same tack’). Sgeulaiche nan Caol has <gai>Dol seachad an Càrn ’na sìnteig,<eng> ‘at speed’. The Càrn is the rocks north of Coll known in English as the Cairns of Coll.

1079–80. The Morison MS. has, added in the margin,

<gai>Dol seachad an linne sleibhteach
Shil is sheid i le cloich mheallain<eng>

Sgeulaiche nan Caol has as third verse the same couplet as MacPherson, but as fifth couplet it also has <gai>’Nuair a nochd i ’n Linne shleiteach, / Bha muir ag éirigh na meallan,<eng> which is unnecessary duplication, and geographically out of order, as once the boat had cleared the Point of Sleat, she would be away from the Sound of Sleat. Apparently she had been sailed north from Coll as far as Arisaig and then out north-west between Skye and the Small Isles.

1081. Rubh’ an Dùnain, often mentioned in Gaelic sailing songs, is the next Skye headland west of the Point of Sleat that would have to be weathered. It is the nearest point of Skye to Canna, and the site of a prehistoric fort and a chambered cairn. An artificial channel connects a freshwater loch there with the sea; it may have been made by the Vikings.

1083, 1085. These couplets follow in the same order in both the Morison and the MacPherson MS. versions; but the order should be reversed, as sailing north-west off the coast of Skye, one passes Loch Eynort before reaching MacLeod’s Maidens.

MacLeod’s Maidens are three spectacular perpendicular rocks near Idrigill Point in Duirinish in the north-west of Skye. They are mentioned in Boswell’s Journal, and are described in A. R. Forbes’s Place Names of Skye (1923), p. 261.

1084. Morison MS., <gai>’M bial Loch-Roaig.<eng> So Sgeulaiche nan Caol. The name Croic is not in Forbes’s book referred to above. Roag in Skye is at the head of the western branch of Loch Bracadale. Following this verse, Sgeulaiche nan Caol has the couplet <gai>Gu’m bu slàn do’n t-saor a dh’fhuaigh i, / Làmh a’ chruadail dh’fhuaigh a darach,<eng> ‘A health to the carpenter who made her, / Hardy hand that seamed her oak’.

[TD 275]

1085. The Morison MS. has <gai>Thainig soills’ oirnn’ bho’n Ghealaich,<eng> ‘Light came on us from the moon’. Cf. ll. 992, 1030.

1087–8. This couplet is not in the Morison MS. version, nor in Sgeulaiche nan Caol. The boat had not passed through the Sound of Canna, which is between Canna and Rum.

1089–90. The Morison MS. has <gai>Leag sinn air corsa direach / Air tir iosal Mhic-ic-Ailein,<eng> ‘We laid our course straight for the low country of Clanranald’. This was presumably South Uist; the Clanranald land they passed was Canna.

1091. Port nan Long is on the south side of the entrance to Loch Harport, about five miles east of MacLeod’s Maidens. The boat must have turned back.

1094. Colonel Alasdair MacLean of Coll, as has been explained.

Following this verse, the Morison MS. has another couplet;

<gai>Chuir sinn acair air gach taobh dhi
’S gu’n d’thug fear mo ghaoil dh’uinn drama.<eng>

It adds nothing to the sense.

Altogether, the Morison and MacPherson MS. versions have 13 couplets in common, out of 14 in each case.


CXVI

<gai>A Dhòmhnaill Dhuinn, ma rinn thu m’eugcoir<eng>

(‘Brown haired Donald, if you have wronged me’)

Recorded from Mrs. Mary MacNeil (‘Màiri Ruarachain’), Crìochan, Barra, on ediphone at Northbay in January 1937, and again on wire at Castlebay on 29/12/49 (no. 107). Also recorded on tape from Nan MacKinnon (‘Nan Eachainn Fhionnlaigh’) on Vatersay by Mr. Donald MacPherson in July 1953. Eriskay version recorded by the Revd. Fr. John MacCormick from Mrs Marion MacInnes (‘Mór nighean Dhunnchaidh’) in the autumn of 1947.

MS. version: the only one known to us is in Fr. Allan McDonald’s sixth folklore notebook, where it is item no. 265. The provenance is not stated, but it is almost certainly Eriskay. This version has only 3 couplets, corresponding to the first, third, and sixth couplets of Mrs. MacNeil’s version.

Printed versions: K. C. C. 78 (9 couplets), text alone; text, translation, and tune, G.S.N.S. 112, 21 couplets. The words of this version are said to be from Allan A. Quinn, Coxheath, Cape Breton, and the melody from the late Frank MacNeil, Big Pond, Cape Breton, who was of Barra descent. Previously the song was printed in Gairm, iii. 144, with the tune in sol-fa (1954).

[TD 276]

No other versions are known to us, though D. C. M. contains a love song of 15 couplets made to the same refrain, p. 124, beginning <gai>Gur a mis a ta fo mhulad / ’S mi ’m shuidhe air uilinn nan àrd.<eng>

Though the version in K. C. C. contains only 9 couplets, it is obviously connected with the Cape Breton version, as the couplets occur in the same general order (1, 2, 5, 6, 8, 9, 10, 15, 16 of the Cape Breton version), and there are other similarities. In Nan MacKinnon’s version the 7 couplets correspond to 11, 3, 2, 5, 6 of Mrs. MacNeil’s, then to 13 of the Cape Breton version, ending with what is the first couplet in Mrs. MacNeil’s version.

In Barra this song was known as <gai>Cumha Bhanntrach Shanndraidh,<eng> the Lament of the Sandray Widow (Sandray is a small island south of Barra, now uninhabited). The story was that she had made the song to lament her three sons being taken by the press-gang at the time of the Napoleonic wars. There are difficulties about this, involving the possible identity of ‘Dòmhnall Donn’ and the fact that the leaders of press-gangs were not in the habit of handing out guineas (first coined in 1663) to the parents of the young men whom they took.

1095. K. C. C. and G.S.N.S. have <gai>Dhòmhnaill Ghuirm<eng> for <gai>Dhòmhnaill Dhuinn.<eng> Nan MacKinnon has <gai>Dhòmhnaill Uilleim.<eng> Fr. Allan McDonald has <gai>Dhònuill Ghuirm,<eng> preceded by the note that ‘Donull Gorm Clanranald is always mentioned with feelings of horror. The following stanza is from a song composed by a widow whose every son he [MS. she] took away from her’.

The Revd. William Matheson has pointed out to us that this suggests the possibility that the song was composed by the widow of Fearchar mac Alasdair, a son of Alexander the seventh chief of Clanranald. This Fearchar was killed in 1564 by his nephews, Allan, Angus, and Dòmhnall Gorm, the sons of Iain Mùideartach. At a later date, according to tradition, several of Fearchar’s sons were killed by Dòmhnall Gorm or his agents. This would date the song at around 1600.

Another reason for the unpopularity of Dòmhnall Gorm Clanranald in Uist was his failure to go to the help of his brother Angus who was killed by the MacLeods in the battle of Abhainn Ròdhag in South Uist around 1594.

‘Donald Gorme was also blamed for not having gone with his contingent of 20 men to aid his brother in his time of need. Men shook their heads and looked wise when they stated that Donald himself succeeded to his brother’s property and position … There is a cairn called <gai>Carn Dhomhnuill Ghuirm,<eng> which points out the scene of an irregular and bloody action, and where Donald is said to have sat watching the fight when he should have come to his brother’s aid.’ (Clan Donald, ii. 301–2; the authors give Farquhar Beaton, shepherd at Drimsdale, South Uist, as the source of the latter tradition.)

It would be interesting if the song could be dated back to 1600, but the ‘golden guinea’ and the Barra tradition are against it. It suggests

[TD 277]

a song addressed to some local recruiting agent, rather than to a press-gang leader.

1107. The seventh and tenth couplets of Mrs. MacNeil’s version are not found in the G.S.N.S. version, nor are the sixth, seventh, tenth, and eleventh of Mrs. MacInnes’s. On the other hand, the G.S.N.S. version has 6 couplets that are not found in these versions, though the equivalent of one of them is the sixth in Nan MacKinnon’s version. This, which comes after a gap in the recording, is <gai>Nach fhaic mi tighinn an t-eithear / Air nach fhaicear fear fo ràimh.<eng> The G.S.N.S. version suggests that ’S truagh at the beginning has not been recorded, if so, the translation would be ‘A pity I don’t see the boat coming, on which a man at the oar is not seen.’

1113. The words on the recording are <gai>Gura mise tha fo mhulad,<eng> but this does not rhyme. Miss Annie Johnston informed me that the correct reading should be as given here.

1129. <gai>Dosrach<eng> is uncertain, but seems to be the word that fits the sense here. <gai>Feadan<eng> (in the plural) appears to be used here and in other similar contexts to include all the hollow tubes that make up a set of bagpipes, not just the chanter.

1136. Transcription uncertain. The singer, who was old, was given to taking breath at the beginnings of the second lines of couplets.


CXVII

<gai>Gur h-e mo ghille dùbhdhonn<eng>

(‘It is my dark-haired laddie’)

Recorded from Roderick MacKinnon (‘Ruairi Iain Bhàin’) at Northbay, Barra, in March 1937, disc no. XI, and from Mrs. John Galbraith (‘Mór Iain Dhòmhnaill Phàdraig’), with chorus, at Castlebay, Barra, on 3/1/50, wire no. 156. Recorded from Mrs. Marion MacInnes (‘Mór nighean Dhunnchaidh’) on Eriskay in the autumn of 1947 with my ediphone by the Revd. Fr. John MacCormick.

The only other version known to us is that printed by Colm Ó Lochlainn in Deoch-Slàinte nan Gillean, p. 42, with tune. This consists of refrain and 8 couplets, which correspond to Roderick MacKinnon’s version in the following order: 3, 9, 4, 5, then 10 of Mrs. Galbraith’s version, then 7, 6, 8 of Roderick MacKinnon’s. The Eriskay version differs considerably.

The song appears to be associated with Barra, where it was popular.

1158. Transcription of first part of line uncertain.

[TD 278]

CXVIII

<gai>Chunnaic mise mo leannan<eng>

(‘I saw my lover’)

Recorded from Roderick MacKinnon (‘Ruairi Iain Bhàin’) at Northbay, Barra, in March 1938, on disc XI (14 couplets); from Mrs. John Galbraith (‘Mór Iain Dhòmhnaill Phàdraig’), Earsary, at Castlebay, by the Barra Folklore Committee on 2/3/50, wire no. 275 (8 couplets); from Mrs. Marion MacInnes (‘Mór nighean Dhunnchaidh’), Eriskay, by the Revd. Fr. John MacCormick on my ediphone in the autumn of 1947 (11 couplets); from Miss Flora MacRae, Smercleit, South Uist, at Pollachar on 2/4/51, wire no. 697 (4 couplets); and from Catriana Campbell, Loch Carnan, South Uist, on 10/4/51, wire no. 831 (12 couplets).

MS. versions: Morison MS. (10 couplets on ravished girl, 7 on MacDonalds, treated as separate poems); K., p. 24 (22 couplets); A. J. (2), p. 80 (3 couplets); Barra MS. (20 couplets). In the list of contents in K. the song is said to be ‘a eulogy on the Macdonalds in the Montrose campaign of 1644–5. The author is not known. Collected from different sources.’ It is doubtful if the song is directly connected with the Montrose campaign.

Printed versions: Gillies Collection, 1786, p. 245 (9 couplets); T.G.S.I. viii. 115, in paper on ‘Leaves from my Celtic Portfolio IV’ read by William MacKenzie on 7/5/1879, taken down ‘from an old woman on Skye’, 14 couplets; Òranaiche, 1879, p. 504 (17 couplets); C.G. ii 299 (1900), 5 couplets from a version ‘taken down from an old woman in Uist in 1866. She said that the song had been composed to one of the gallant Clanranalds by a lady, after the battle of Auldearn’. (The 5 couplets are quoted in the Notes to the first two volumes of C.G. to illustrate the term <gai>còta gearr.<eng>) T.G.S.I. xxvi. 241 (20 couplets), no ascription, in a paper by the Revd. A. MacLean Sinclair, read on 23/11/1905, entitled ‘A Collection of Gaelic Poems’; McD. 49, identical with K., but adding 2 more couplets in the description of the song in the list of contents; K. C. C. (1949), to couplets.

Part of text (7 couplets and refrain), recorded from the late John MacLean, M. A. (Raasay), and tune with piano accompaniment arranged by Ian Whyte: Gairm i. Àireamh 4, 47–9 (1953).

Text and translation: Colm Ó Baoill, Five MacLean Poets. (Appendix). Dr. Ó Baoill says that Mull tradition ascribes this song to the Mull bard Eachann Bacach; this seems very unlikely, as it is obviously a MacDonald song.

Text, translation and tune: F.F.S.U. (1955), 12 couplets, with two versions of the tune; G.S.N.S. (1964), 20 couplets.

Tune, one verse and refrain: Kennedy-Fraser, Songs of the Hebrides, ii. p. xxii, where it is called ‘A Jacobite Song of 1715. From North Uist’.

This is probably the most popular and widely known traditional waulking song, as well as one of the earliest printed. Being sung to a

[TD 279]

spirited but simple air, it was easily memorized. It was probably known to most traditional singers in Uist and Barra, and was also known in Skye and Mull.

Four themes are contained in the song, though two of the four occur in only a few versions. These are:

A. My lover went by on horseback without recognizing me. It was my fault—I didn’t wait for him (Gillies, T.G.S.I. xxvi.) / I don’t care, I’m indifferent about him (Òranaiche, Roderick MacKinnon, Mrs. Marion MacInnes).

B. The successful hunter is the one I prefer. Except for one couplet in Catriana Campbell’s version, this passage only occurs in the Nova Scotian versions, T.G.S.I. xxvi. and G.S.N.S., which may be connected.

C. Praise of the MacDonalds, to whom it is implied that the girl’s lover is connected, and their military appearance and capability.

D. The girl has been raped and abandoned with an illegitimate child. This passage is only represented in the Morison MS., F.F.S.U., and Catriana Campbell’s and very briefly in the Barra MS. and in Roderick MacKinnon’s versions.

D is clearly not considered connected with C by reciters. In fact, in the Morison MS. the couplets are enumerated separately. Margaret Fay Shaw was told by Mary Smith that the three couplets representing D in F.F.S.U. did not belong to the main song, and Catriana Campbell separated the 6 couplets in her version on this theme from the rest of the song (which contains nothing of C) by the triple repetition of the chorus refrain.

Although no traditional version of the song is longer than 20 couplets (the K. version, printed in McD is a composite version), no fewer than 61 different couplets occur in the printed, MS., and recorded versions under consideration here. There are 15 referable to theme A, 5 to B, 28 to C, and 13 to D. The version printed in McD is entirely devoted to the praises of the Clan MacDonald, is admittedly a composite version, and contains 12 couplets not found in any other version. The editors consider that the song refers to the Montrose wars of 1644–5. This identification, also made in C.G., depends on a couplet which does not occur in any of the recorded versions printed here, though it is found in K. C. C. and in the Morison MS., as well as in the earlier printed versions, with minor variations. In the Gillies Collection it is:

<gai>Thug sibh mionnan a Bhiobail,
Ann srath iosal uilt-eirinn<eng>

‘You took a Bible oath in the low strath of Auldearn.’

This is followed by a couplet which in various forms means ‘that your swords would not be sheathed until King Charles/King James was crowned’.

Charles is the king named in the McD., T.G.S.I. xxvi, and C.G. versions; Charles with James in brackets in K. C. C.; James in Gillies,

[TD 280]

the Morison MS., T.G.S.I. viii, Òranaiche, Barra MS., and G.S.N.S. versions. The couplet does not occur in F.F.S.U., nor in any of the recorded versions printed here.

Charles or James? The battle of Auldearn, between Inverness and Elgin, was fought on 8 May 1645, and was one of Montrose’s most brilliant victories; his Highland lieutenant, Alasdair mac Colla (see vol. ii, no. LXXI) performed prodigies of valour at it. But King Charles was already a crowned king when it was fought, and the couplet could only make sense if the word <gai>crùinte(adh)<eng> were translated ‘restored’. The allusion could be to something that had happened in the past.

Apart from this, and if James VIII is really the king referred to, the song applies equally well to the Rising of 1715 or that of 1745, especially considering the twelth couplet in Roderick MacKinnon’s version regarding the expulsion of King George, similar couplets occurring in T.G.S.I. viii, the Morison MS., and Mrs. MacInnes’s versions.

Many textual variations occur between the different versions of the song, but there is only space available to consider the most important of them here.

Opening couplets: T.G.S.I. viii, Òranaiche, F.F.S.U., and G.S.N.S. have the same opening couplet as Catriana Campbell, ll. 1190–1 here. See also the eleventh couplet in the song Fleasgach a’ Bhadain, D.C.M., p. 133. In T.G.S.I. viii and in G.S.N.S. this is followed by <gai>Mi na m’ laidhe ann an Crosal ’S trom an osn’ tha fo m’ leine<eng> (G.S.N.S. has <gai>’g a m’ léireadh for fo m’ leine<eng>). Crosal is in Skye, between Sligachan and Drynoch, in the MacLeod country; it is very unlikely that a MacLeod woman would have made a song lavishing such praise on the MacDonalds as this one does.

Couplets 1, 2, and 3 here should be compared with couplets 1, 3, and 4 of no. CVII in this volume.

1191–2. In Gillies alone, the corresponding couplet is <gai>Cha b(’)ann aige bha choire, Ach nach d’fhuirich mi fhein ris.<eng> ‘It was not his fault, but that I did not wait for him.’

1195. This line has many variants:

Gillies: <gai>Gu’n cinnich an Domhnach<eng>
Morison MS.: <gai>Sian Mhuire ’s na Domhnach<eng>
T.G.S.I. viii: <gai>Cuim nach guidhean an Dònach<eng>
T.G.S.I. xxvi: <gai>Sian nan soraidh ’s an Dòmhnaich<eng>
Òranaiche: <gai>Sian Mhuire ’s an Dòmhnaich<eng>
K. and McD.: <gai>Gun cinnich an Dòmhnach<eng>
G.S.N.S. <gai>Sian nach sòradh ’s an Dòmhnaich<eng>
F.F.S.U. <gai>Buaidh a’ bhlàir anns an Domhnaich<eng>
Barra MS.: <gai>A righ gun cuidich an Domhnach

Sian (seun)<eng> presumably refers to a charm intended to bestow invulnerability, and the meaning is probably ‘the charm of Mary and of the Lord’. Such a charm is said to have been put on Allan MacDonald of Clanranald when he was leaving South Uist to join the Rising of 1715,

[TD 281]

but it did not work, as he was shot by a conscript on his own side with a crooked sixpence cursed by the lad’s mother. See C.G. ii. 26–7.

1202. <gai>Chuireadh siubhal fo shaighdean,<eng> ‘would send arrows flying’, Gillies, T.G.S.I. viii, K., McD., G.S.N.S., is better. K. C. C. has <gai>Chuireadh fiughair fo shaighdean.<eng>

1204. Gillies, T.G.S.I. xxvi, G.S.N.S.: <gai>Chuireadh faileas ri gréin diubh<eng>

T.G.S.I. viii: <gai>Chitear lainnir là gréin annt’<eng>
K., McD.: <gai>Chit’ air bearradh an t-sléibhe<eng>
C.G.: <gai>Chit an dearrsa la greine<eng> (of short coats)
F.F.S.U.: <gai>Chìte sealladh là gréine<eng>
Barra MS.: <gai>Chìte solus le grein dhiubh<eng>

Cf. ll. 128–9, 1224, and 1244 of this volume.

1205. <gai>Rùsgadh<eng> ‘unsheathing’ cannot be right. The other versions have:

Gillies: <gai>Nach d’re’ cloidheamh an duille<eng>
Morison MS.: <gai>Nach rachadh claidheamh a dhunadh<eng>
T.G.S.I. viii: <gai>Nach rachadh claidheamh na dhùbladh<eng>
Òranaiche: <gai>Nach rachadh claidheamh an cùil<eng>
C.G.: <gai>Nach de’adh claidhe a dhubhladh<eng>
T.G.S.I. xxvi, G.S.N.S.: <gai>Nach cuirt’ claidheabh an truaill leo<eng>
K., McD.: <gai>Nach rachadh claidheamh an truaill<eng>
Barra MS.: <gai>Nach rachadh claidheamh a chùnadh

Dùbladh<eng> ‘sheathe’, a rare word in Scottish Gaelic, is probably the right reading when the rhyme is with <gai>crùinte.<eng>

Cf. ll. 1227 and 1247 here.

1207. This couplet is missing from the Gillies, Òranaiche, C.G., K. C. C., F.F.S.U., and G.S.N.S. versions. K. and McD., where one might expect to find it, have <gai>Gu ma slàn do na gillean / Dh’fhalbh le iomairt Righ Seurlas<eng> ‘Here a health to the lads / who went away on the expedition of King Charles’. Actual variants: T.G.S.I. viii, <gai>Gus an deanta Righ Seoras / A’ ròiceadh ’s a reubadh.<eng> Morison MS., <gai>Gan faicinn Righ Deorsa / Na spolan bho cheile.<eng> Barra MS., <gai>Gus a toirte Righ Deorsa / Air a spoltadh ’o cheile.<eng> T.G.S.I. xxvi, <gai>’S gus am faict’ a luchd-fògraidh / ’Dol ’s na còrdaibh na ’éirig.<eng>

1209–12. These couplets (nos. 13 and 14) in Ruairi Iain Bhàin’s version are referable to theme D of the song. No. 13 is only found in Ruairi Iain Bhàin’s and the Barra MS. versions; no. 14 only in these versions and in the Morison MS. version. In the Barra MS., no. 14 is followed by 2 couplets not found in any of the other versions discussed here: these are nos. 10 and 11 in the Barra MS.:

<gai>Cha n-ioghnadh do mhàthair
Bhith cràiteach mu d’ dhéidhinn;

Cha n-ioghnadh do phiuthar
Bhith dubhach mu d’ dhéidhinn.<eng>

[TD 282]

‘It is no wonder that a mother
is pained about you;

It is no wonder that a sister
is sorrowful about you.’

For allusions to Kirk discipline, cf. ll. 442 and 764 of vol. i.

1251. Catriana Campbell’s version of this song corresponds to that in the Morison MS. (where the couplets on the MacDonalds are numerated separately) fairly closely. As compared with her version, the Morison MS. has (allowing for variant readings) couplets corresponding to her 3, 4, 5, 7, then Roderick MacKinnon’s 14, then Catriana Campbell’s 8, 11. The Morison MS. ends with the following couplets, which in Uist and Barra are associated with different songs:

<gai>(8) Tha sac trom air mo chridhe
Cha tog fidheal na’n teud e
(9) Cha tog fidheal na clarsach
Piob no taileasg na dheigh e
(10) No piob nan dos arda
Ged robh Paruig ga gleusadh<eng>

For Morison MS. (8), cf. ll. 539–40, 757–8 here. Cf. also the ninth couplet in the song Fleasgach a’ Bhadain, Duanaire, 133. For (9), cf. vol. i l. 82, and for (10), l. 83 in the same volume. ‘Paruig’ referred to must be either Patrick Mór or Patrick Òg MacCrimmon, hereditary pipers of MacLeod of Dunvegan. The former’s dates were c. 1595–1670, the latter’s c. 1645–1730. (Francis Collinson, The Bagpipe, p. 145.)

1261–2. If the girl’s lover was going hunting otters with a gun, he would not have been on horseback. This leads us to think that the passage referring to the hunter, developed in the G.S.N.S. version, is an interpolation. Cf. also ll. 717–18 here.

1263. Morison MS. <gai>bothag na th’airidh (sic).<eng>

1265–6. This couplet is from the Morison MS. The singer, who appeared to be uncertain of the words here, apparently had <gai>Ann am bothag mi gun dh’fhiadhaich, / Far nach bu mhiann ri tòir ach éiginn,<eng> which does not make very good sense, and is unmetrical in the second line.

1267–71. Approximately the same lines occur in an entirely different song, see K. C. C. 5.


CXIX

<gai>Gura mis’ tha fo mhulad, tha lionn-dubh air mo lìonadh<eng>

(‘Sorrowful am I, full of melancholy’)

Recorded (a) from Mrs. Archie Munro (‘Màiri a’ Ghobha’), at Lochboisdale, South Uist, on 14/11/61., on tape for me with my machine by

[TD 283]

Captain D. J. MacKinnon. (b) from Mrs. Patrick MacCormick (‘Catriana ’ill’ Easbuig’), at Torlum, Benbecula, on 21/11/49; this was the first waulking song I recorded on wire (wire no. 15).

Other version: text alone, K. C. C. 56. We have not found any other version of this song.

The words of the two versions printed here and that in K. C. C. have little in common. K. C. C. has 16 couplets, of which the first and ninth correspond to Mrs. Munro’s first and third respectively, and the tenth, twelfth, and thirteenth to Mrs. MacCormick’s ninth, tenth, and eleventh.

Mrs. MacCormick began the song by singing the chorus refrain (second and third lines of the refrain), then after the first 2 couplets, sang only the solo refrain (the first line); but she sang the refrain in full after the remaining couplets.

1287–90. Compare the fifth and seventh couplets of the song beginning <gai>Chì mi am bàta a’ dol seachad<eng> in T.G.S.I. xxxix–xl. 188, which has the same refrain as no. CXX here.

1299–1302. Cf. ll. 545–9 of vol. ii.

1305–14. Cf. ll. 1195–8 of vol. i.


CXX

<gai>Gura mise tha fo mhulad an tìr a’ mhurain ’s an t-sìobain<eng>

(‘Sorrowful am I in the land of bent-grass and spindrift’)

Recorded by Mrs. Effie Monk (‘Bean Iain Aonghais ’ic Iain Bhig ’ic ’Ill’ Easbuig mhóir’), Torlum, Benbecula, for me by the Revd. John MacCormick in the winter of 1953/4.

Other versions: text alone, K 39 (18 couplets); McD. 114 (19 couplets, 18 identical with K); K. C. C. 6 (11 couplets).

Text and tune: Gesto Coll. 37 (14 couplets).

Text, translation, and tune; F. T., no. 95 (p. 258) (12 couplets); ‘Sung by Mary Ross, from Killmaluag, Skye, 1897.’ From the Farthest Hebrides, pp. 52–5 (text from McD.; three tunes, one of which is that of CXVIII here, and another identical with that in F. T.; the third originally from Alexander MacLean, son of Norman, Griminish, North Uist.)

Twenty-seven different couplets occur in these five versions altogether, although curiously enough only one, the third here, is common to all five. Seven are common to four, and four to three, allowing for variations. But all eleven couplets in the F.T. version coincide with couplets found in the K. and McD. version, though they are not in the same order. The Gesto Collection version contains one couplet that is not in the others.

This is one of the very few waulking songs where both the story behind the song, and the identity of the composer of the words, are known. We owe the preservation of these to the Revd. A. MacDonald, minister of Killearnan, who was connected with Benbecula. In K. he writes that,

[TD 284]

‘Archibald MacLean of Heisker, the hero of this song and of a number of others, was engaged to be married to Susan MacDonald, Vallay [an island off the north side of North Uist] but he left her. Her brother, Alexander, who was factor of North Uist, became very oppressive to the gay Lothaire, and leaving the country he was never heard of again. The composer of the song was Anna Macdonald (nighean Dòmhnaill Oig) who lived at Balranald, N. Uist’ (p. 39). In McD. note on this song (p. xxiv) it is stated that Anna Macdonald was Archibald MacLean’s foster-sister.

There is another song to Archibald MacLean, <gai>Tha Fear Heisgir a’ tighinn / Bu tu ceann-uidhe nan ciad<eng> (K. p. 127; McD. 199; From the Farthest Hebrides pp. 48–52). This is said to have been made by Rachel Macdonald (Raonaid nighean ’ic Néill), Grenitote, North Uist, whose dates are given as c. 1750–1820. This, together with the reference in McD. and F. T. versions to Archibald MacLean’s kelp being unmade and unavailable for export to England, would put the date of the song at around 1775.

McD. says in the notes on this song (p. xxiii) that ‘This song is in praise of Archibald MacLean of Heisker, in North Uist. His powers as a steersman and the seaworthy qualities of his galley are powerfully depicted. Archibald was a son of Iain mac Ghilleasbuig Oig of Heisker, of the family of Boreray [cf. no. CXI in this vol.]. He was at this time tacksman of Heisker and Steelbow tenant of Peinmore, now part of Balranald. He emigrated to Canada, and died at Ontario in the early part of last century.’

His prowess as a steersman is also mentioned in this song, ll. 1301–4 here.

The cause of his troubles is alluded to in a couplet found in the K., McD., and K. C. C. versions; <gai>Their gach neach air am beag e Gur e ’m beadradh a mhill e.<eng> ‘Everyone who dislikes him says that it is flirting that ruined him.’ In consequence of his breach of promise, he has had to leave North Uist (testimony to the power of factors in the eighteenth century), and his affairs are in a ruinous condition: his buildings have lost their thatch, his boat is pulled up unused on the shore, his barley is unreaped, his peat is unmade, his kelp has not been cut, and his cattle are unherded. The picture is vivid and convincing.

1316. <gai>Tìr a’ mhurain,<eng> ‘the land of bent-grass’, is a kenning for Uist, referring to the bent-grass that grows freely on the sandhills of the machair there.

1324. K., McD., and K. C. C. have <gai>do bhràithrean ’s na h-Innsibh,<eng> ‘your brothers are in the Indies’. The couplet is not in F. T.

1334. This couplet was followed by an eleventh of which the transcription is uncertain. It could be something like <gai>Làmh riachaid nan searrag Miosg nam fear air a’ phìthir (?)<eng> The other versions give no help here. The fourth and eighth couplets of Mrs. Monk’s version are also not found in any of the others.

[TD 285]

CXXI

<gai>’S ’ga innse cha déid mi<eng>

(‘No, no, I’ll not tell it’)

Recorded from Kate Monk at Hacklett, Benbecula, on 19/3/51, wire no. 627.

We have not found any other version of this song. The reference to Glen Lyon in l. 1275 suggests it may be connected with the MacGregors.


CXXII

<gai>’Ille dhuinn bhòidhich<eng>

(‘Handsome brown-haired lad’)

Recorded from Mrs. A. J. MacLellan and Mrs. Patrick MacCormick (‘Penny Aonghais ’ic Raghnaill’ agus ‘Catriana nighean ’ille Easbuig’) at Hacklett, Benbecula, on 31/10/50, wire no. 489; from Mrs. Mary Johnston (‘Màiri Iain Choinnich’) at Castlebay, Barra, on 5/4/51, wire no. 728; and from Mrs. Neil Campbell (‘Bean Nìll’) at Frobost, South Uist, on 30/4/58, on tape.

MS. version: Fr. Allan McDonald, fol. 92 (7 couplets).

Printed versions: text alone, Duanaire. (1868), p. 68, (16 couplets: headed An Gille Donn and signed Muileach); Òranaiche, (1879), p. 456 (16 couplets, identical with Duanaire, except that Òr. has <gai>deònach<eng> for D. C. M. <gai>dòineach<eng> in seventh couplet); Mac-Talla, vi. 64 (1897, 23 couplets); K. C. C. (1949), p. 49 (26 couplets, of which three in praise of the Clan Donald probably do not originally belong to the song).

Text, translation, and harmonized tune: Charles Stewart, The Killin Collection of Gaelic Songs (1884), p. 32 (11 couplets and refrain).

The ‘Boat Song’ from Lexie MacRae, Harris, printed without any words or refrain syllables on p. x of Mrs. Kennedy-Fraser’s From the Hebrides, may be a version of the air of this song.

The M.T. version is headed ‘Duanag le Saighdear Muileach’, and is signed ‘Gleann-a-Bhaird’ (? = MacLean Sinclair). The refrain here is <gai>O ho rò, a nigh’n donn, / A nigh’n donn bhoidheach, O ho rò, a nigh’n donn.<eng>

A waulking song in the Morison MS. beginning <gai>Ailein duinn a’ chùil duallaich, / Cha robh buaidh air do sheòrsa<eng> is given as sung to the refrain <gai>Hiri horo Ailein duinn / Ailein duinn bhòidhich / Hi ri ho ro Ailein duinn,<eng> which may very well indicate a version of this air, as the song here is also connected with the island of Mull.

All the couplets in the seven-verse versions taken down by Fr. Allan McDonald, and sung by Mrs. Mary Johnston, occur in the two versions printed here; Mrs. Johnston has Mrs. Campbell’s 7, 8, Mrs. MacLellan’s

[TD 286]

12, Mrs. Campbell’s, 18, 17 in that order, and Fr. Allan has Mrs. Campbell’s 7, Mrs. MacLellan’s 12, and Mrs. Campbell’s 11, 20, 22, 16, and 17.

Though no explicit reference is made, the song may very well refer to the expedition to Holland in the autumn of 1799, in which the Gordon Highlanders (the 92nd Regiment) took part. The battle in question took place on 2 October of that year, and was the subject of a long poem by Alasdair MacFhionghuin, the Morar bard, who was a corporal in that regiment. See the Turner Collection, 38; Sàr Obair, p. 344; B.Gh. 25 and 266 (where General Stewart of Garth’s description of the battle is quoted).

Mrs. Neil Campbell’s version of this song (20 couplets) is a good one. I have added to it the sixth, seventh, eighth, and nineteenth couplets from the M.T. version; they are completely in place, and are supported by some of the other versions. On the other hand, I have omitted 3 couplets at the beginning of Mrs. Campbell’s recorded version which seem quite irrelevant. These are:

<gai>’S bheir mi mollachd air gillean
… rithist mo dhòigh-sa

E bhith tric ’san taigh-seinse
Bhiodh mo lamhan ’nam phòca.

E bhith drùdhadh na glaineadh,
E bhith ’na amadan gòrach.<eng>

‘My curse on lads … He is often in the inn, my hands would be in my pockets; he would be draining the glass, being a silly fool.’

1344. Killin Collection <gai>culaidh,<eng> ‘equipment’, an obvious attempt to get rid of an English loan-word, for the Killin text, apart from couplets omitted, is the same as that of the Òranaiche.

1358. K. C. C. following the corresponding couplet, has one referring to their sailing to the Indies, from where they would not return alive: the corresponding couplet in Mrs. Johnston’s version is <gai>Ach gu stràid nan ceum dìreach / Far nach till sinn ri’r beò as,<eng> ‘but to the street of straight footsteps, whence we shall not return alive’. The Indies are not mentioned there or in any other version.

1360. Duanaire, M.T., <gai>Thug sinn ’n cath a bha dòineach.<eng> Òr., <gai>deònach.<eng> K. C. C. <gai>Thug sinn cath a bha deònach.<eng> It is likely that Duanaire and M.T. are right here.

1363. Fr. Allan McDonald, <gai>éibhlichean ruadha.<eng>

1365. K. C. C. is the only other version with this couplet.

1371. Duanaire, <gai>B’ioma té ’bha gun chéile.<eng> M.T., K. C. C., <gai>’S iomadh té bha gun chéile.<eng>

1377. M.T., <gai>Bidh a ceann air a cluasaig / ’S i ri bruadaran neònach.<eng> K. C. C., <gai>Bhiodh i tiunndadh ’s a’ gluasad / Air a cluasaig glé bhrònach.<eng> Mrs. Johnston, <gai>Agus nighean duin’ uasail / Bhios air cluasaig ’na h-ònrachd.<eng>

[TD 287]

1388. Duanaire, <gai>Théid mi dhachaigh ma’s beò dhomh.<eng> M.T., <gai>… ma’s beo mi.<eng> ‘I’ll come home if I’m alive’. This makes better sense than K. C. C. and Mrs. Neil Campbell <gai>Ged a dhealaich sinn gòrach.<eng> Fr. Allan McDonald has <gai>Bheir mo bheannachd gu m’ leannan / Bean nam meall-shuilean bòidheach,<eng> ‘take my blessing to my sweetheart, the woman of pretty smiling eyes’.

1389. <gai>Cha do dh’fhàg thu mi idir,<eng> M.T.

1390. <gai>’nam phòcaid,<eng> K. C. C.

1391–6, 1399–1400. These couplets are not found in any of the other versions known to us. This suggests that ll. 1397–8 also do not belong to the song originally.


CXXIII

<gai>Fhir bhuidh’ an deagh-nàduir<eng>

(‘Yellow haired man of good nature’)

Recorded at the genuine waulking at Gerinish, South Uist, on 29/3/51, wire no. 647, Mrs. D. J. MacLellan being the soloist.

Other versions: the only one we have been able to find is the text printed in K. C. C. 54 (23 couplets). This begins with the couplet <gai>Gura mise tha fo mhulad / ’S mi air uilinn nam fuarbheann,<eng> and the refrain reverses that recorded here (bab for aba), a matter also commented on by the late Duncan Macdonald the famous South Uist Gaelic storyteller. The impression given by the text in K. C. C. is one of confusion. Couplets 5–8 as sung by Mrs. D. J. MacLellan occur in K. C. C. as couplets 21–3; couplets 9 and 11 as couplets 12 and 16 in K. C. C. There is no further correspondence.

1417–22. Owing to technical difficulties, the words of these couplets are very difficult to make out from the recording.

1432. Presumably one of the MacDonalds who possessed Bornish for about 200 years, and were hereditary bailies of South Uist under the Clanranalds. See Clan Donald, iii. 258.

1436–44. Cf. ll. 423–8 of no. XII in vol. i, where an almost identical passage occurs in a totally different song which is not sung in couplets, but in single-line verses.


CXXIV

<gai>O, ’s toil liom an nìghneag<eng>

(‘O, I like the lassie’)

Recorded from Miss Mary Gillies, with chorus, at Castlebay, Barra, by the Barra Folklore Committee on 9/3/50, wire no. 309.

[TD 288]

The second version of the words printed here is from A. J. (2), 104. No other version of this song in MS. or print is known to us. The story behind the song is forgotten.

1445. <gai>’s toil lium<eng> is usually written <gai>’s toigh lium,<eng> but it is easy to confirm the word used in speech, by asking such a question as <gai>an toil libh ìm?<eng> ‘Do you like butter?’ to which the answer will be <gai>’s toil,<eng> not <gai>’s toigh.<eng> Alexander MacDonald wrote <gai>toil,<eng> Ais-eiridh 157, <gai>ba toil leat;<eng> 184, <gai>Go toil le d’ chairdibh.<eng>

1454, 1468. Portree, in Skye.


CXXV

<gai>Cha déid mi do dh’fhear gun bhàta<eng>

(‘I’ll not go to a man without a boat’)

Recorded from Miss Kate MacDonald (‘Ceit Iseabail’) at the genuine waulking at Gerinish, South Uist, on 29/3/51, wire no. 641.

Other versions: MS., J. L. Campbell as recorded from Mrs. Neil MacInnes (‘Anna nighean Nìll’) at Sydney, Cape Breton, on 15/10/37 (13 couplets, printed here); as recorded from Peigi MacRae, South Lochboisdale, South Uist, (7 couplets, refrain <gai>Mo ghaoil an saoil am faigh mi thu / ’S e t’fhaighinn dh’fhàgadh slàn mi<eng>).

Printed: M.T. xii. 152 (1904) (7 couplets with refrain <gai>A dh’fhear air bith cha’n àill leam, / Air bith, air bith, cha’n àill leam.<eng> K. C. C. 15 (11 couplets). Eilean Fraoich, with tune in sol-fa, p. 74 (6 couplets); another version, referring to clans, ibid. (8 couplets).
