Improvisatory clapping song on the subject of marriage. So far as the person accepted was concerned, there was no set form; the craftsman accepted might be of any profession, possibly with allusive implication.

1486, 1524. Literally ‘Lies will be growing along with him’. Shoemakers had the reputation in the Highlands of being untruthful because the shoes they were making or mending were said never to be ready on the day they were promised. The late Seonaidh Caimbeul the South Lochboisdale bard said of them <gai>Tha ch-uile h-aon cho briagach dhiubh / Gu sgialachdan do chàch<eng> ‘Every one of them is so untruthful, for telling stories to everyone else’ (Orain, p. 61).

1491. The farmer would be looking in his meal-chest, worrying that the farm servants, who got oatmeal as one of their perquisites, were using it too quickly.

1508, 1510. These lines illustrate the different meanings of the word <gai>fàsach.<eng>

[TD 289]

1514. Taking <gai>truimeir<eng> to be a borrowing of the English word ‘strummer’.

1518. The implication is that he would be fonder of his fiddle than of his wife. In Gaelic, a musical instrument is referred to as the <gai>céile<eng> ‘spouse’, of its player.

1528. If she married the miller, her sleep would be disturbed by the noise of the mill.


CXXVI

<gai>Chaidh mo lothag air chall<eng>

(‘My filly’s gone lost’)

Recorded from Annie Johnston (‘Anna Aonghais Chaluim’) in March 1938, disc no. XXXVI; and from Miss Mary Morrison (‘Màiri Eóghainn Mhóir’) at Castlebay, Barra, on 15/12/49, wire no. 120. Both versions printed here.

Clapping song, probably improvisatory. There is a version in the early nineteenth-century Dornie MS. collection made in Wester Ross by Captain Alexander Matheson, who died in 1879, see ‘Gleanings from the Dornie Manuscripts’ by Professor Angus Matheson, T.G.S.I. xli. 355. The song is written there in a peculiar way, in two quatrains of twelve-syllable lines, the first beginning with two phrases of the refrain, and one quatrain of six-syllable lines, suggesting that it was possibly sung to a different air there. Arranged in couplets, it comprises nine, of which the first corresponds to the sixth of Miss Johnston and the third of Miss Morrison’s versions, the second to the seventh and fourth, respectively, the eighth to the fifth of Miss Morrison, and the last to the first of Miss Johnston’s and the second of Miss Morrison. The other couplets are:

<gai>3.
’S tha mo lothag ’sa choille,
’s fear na foille ’na ceann.

4.
’S tha mo lothag an dràsda
’san fhàsach ud thall.

5.
’S mi gum bristeadh an gàradh
nan tàrrainn i nall.

6
’S tric, a ghaoil, a laigh mi
ri d’ shlios, ’s cha n-oil liom,

7.
’S cha b’e bh’ann ach an cailin,
beul tairis gun sgraing.<eng>

(3) my filly is in the wood, with a deceiver at her head (4) my filly is now in the pasture over there (5) I would break the wall if I would bring her over here. (6) Often, my love, I lay beside you, and I did not think it displeasing, (7) it was not but the girl, soft mouth without a frown.

[TD 290]

CXXVII

<gai>Cha n-eil mi gun mhulad orm<eng>

(‘I am not unsorrowful’)

Recorded from Mrs. MacDougall (‘Anna Raghnaill Eachainn’) at Northbay, Barra, on ediphone in January 1937, and learnt by J. L. C. Tune transcribed also from a recording made for the School of Scottish Studies by Miss Annie Johnston.

Ll. 1570–2 and 1574–80 occur also, though not in exactly the same order, in the song printed on p. 66 of Eilean Fraoich, beginning <gai>Ach gur mise tha fo fhadachd,<eng> with the refrain <gai>Seinn seinn hù ho ró, / Seinnibh hó ro ’n àil leibh, / Seinn seinn hù ho ró.<eng>


CXXVIII

<gai>Cà na dh’fhàg thu m’fhichead gini?<eng>

(‘What have you done with my twenty guineas?’)

Recorded from Mrs. Mary Morrison (‘Bean Phluim’) at Castlebay, Barra, in March 1938 on disc XXXIX, and again on 3/1/50, wire no. 169.

Printed version: text, K. C. C. 11 (9 couplets). Only five of the couplets correspond, and one of those, the fifth here, differs considerably.

Art version of air: Mrs. Kennedy-Fraser, Songs of the Hebrides, ii. 215, ‘A Druid of the Isles’. ‘Words collected and translated by Kenneth MacLeod. Air from Annie Johnson [sic], Barra, Arranged for Voice and Piano by Marjory Kennedy-Fraser.’ Needless to say, Kenneth MacLeod’s words have nothing to do with the actual song sung by Annie Johnston.

1609–10. This is the only allusion to the Clan Chisholm to be found in this collection.

[TD 291]

CXXX

<gai>Nighean donn bheadarrach, ho ró<eng>

(‘Lively brown lassie, ho ró’)

CXXXI

<gai>Hug hoireann ó, ’s e m’aighear ì<eng>

(‘Hug hoireann ó, she’s my delight’)

Recorded from Miss Kate MacDonald (‘Ceit Iseabail’) at a genuine waulking at Gerinish, South Uist, on 29/3/51. wire no. 657.

Ribald improvisatory pairing-off clapping song, unpublished. The words are often difficult to catch, with the noise of laughter and of the patting of the cloth. The first line is quite inaudible. It will be seen that the words of the refrain change at times.


CXXXII

<gai>Mhic ’ic Ailein seasgair sìobhalt<eng>

(‘Clanranald, well-to-do and gracious’)

This song has been saved from oblivion through the communication of a text from Barra (probably taken down from Mr. Roderick MacKinnon) in 1938, and by the discovery, after many traditional singers in Uist and Barra had been questioned unsuccessfully, that the air had been recorded about twenty-five years ago by Mr. John MacLean, M.A., from Mrs. Murdo MacDonald (‘Bean Mhurchaidh a’ Bhealaich; nighean Dhunnchaidh Dhualaich’) from Barra. Mrs. MacDonald was 78 at the time.

We have found no trace of the song elsewhere. The story behind it is forgotten; it wears something of the air of a Hogmanay ballad turned into a waulking song. Mrs. MacDonald’s recorded version differs in the order of the lines, and is unfortunately of poor technical quality, many of the lines being indistinct.

1628. MS. <gai>seasgair,<eng> recording <gai>stàtail.<eng>

1633. Recording has <gai>gu foinnidh,<eng> the MS. <gai>le boinne.<eng>

1637. The MS. has <gai>Thàinig a Chlann Nìl ’na sriochdadh;<eng> a correcting hand has written do above a, and <gai>shriochadh<eng> above <gai>sriochdadh.<eng> The

[TD 292]

recording unfortunately is unclear, the line appears to end with something like <gai>leachdadh.<eng> Some kind of noise must be involved if the effect was deafening. <gai>Sgriachadh<eng> ‘screeching’ seems a reasonable restoration.

1638. Cf. Irish <gai>ag baint na gcluas asam,<eng> deafening me. The recording, indistinct, might be <gai>cluain.<eng> Iochdar is at the north end of South Uist, Kilbride, at the south end; Boisdale is two or three miles north of Kilbride.

1643. <gai>Feamain chìrean<eng> is a short seaweed that grows near high-water mark; see G.W.S.U.

1652. MS. <gai>Căraibh mac tighearna an fhìona,<eng> presumably a mistake for <gai>Car’ thu mhac tighearna an fhìona,<eng> cf. ll. 756, 757, 759, etc.


CXXXIII

<gai>Òganaich dhuinn a rinn m’ fhàgail<eng>

(‘O brown haired youth who has left me’)

On 22/6/49 I took down the words of this song from Mrs. Kate Buchanan (‘Ceit Ruairi Iain Bhàin’) at Eoligarry, Barra. Unfortunately I did not have a recording machine available, so was unable to record her version of the air. In July 1975 Miss Kathy Torrey, whom I had asked to look for the song in South Uist, recorded a version from Mrs. MacLean, wife of Dr. A. MacLean, Fionn-Chrò, Lochboisdale. Subsequently it turned out that Mrs. MacLean had learnt it from a tape recording of Nan MacKinnon (‘Nan Eachainn Fhionnlaigh’), Vatersay, which Mr. Donald MacPherson had made in July 1953, and which is now apparently mislaid.

In the spring of 1977 Nan MacKinnon sent to F. Collinson a cassette recording of several traditional songs, including this one. Both versions of the air are transcribed here.

The song appears to be unpublished. As sung by Mrs. MacLean, the air sounds more like that of a spinning song; but the metrical structure of the song (8² + aba) is that of a waulking song, and in view of the song’s interest, it is included here.

1654. Nan MacKinnon (1977) has <gai>Òganaich ùir.<eng>

1663–5. Formulaic passage, cf. vol. i, ll. 893–902; vol. ii, ll. 1648–51.


CXXXIV

<gai>Bheir soiridh, soiridh bhuam<eng>

(‘Take greetings from me’)

Recorded in July 1975 by Miss Kathy Torrey from Mrs. Kate MacDonald (‘Ceit Nìll’), Daliburgh, South Uist.

[TD 293]

This spirited song seems to be little known. The only other version we have found is that in K. C. C. 12 (15 lines). But compare lines 1523–31 of no. LXXXII (vol. ii).

Although the song is fundamentally in 8² metre, the opening half-line contains six syllables, and several other first half-lines contain five. The singer did not repeat the half-lines with alternating refrain, as probably would have been done at a waulking. The long refrain was sung differently after the last half-line, from what it was previously.

1679–80. Lines about the crops growing in favoured spots in the Highlands, peas, beans, oats, barley, etc., are formulaic. Cf. vol. i, ll. 106–4; vol. ii, ll. 1025–30. It should be noted that potatoes are never mentioned in such contexts, proving that the songs in question must have been made before 1730.

1680. Peas and beans were an important crop in Scotland in old times, before the introduction of the potato. An Act of the Scots Parliament of James I ordained that ‘each man tilling with a plough of eight oxen shall sow at least each year a firlot of wheat, half a firlot [ = a quarter of a boll] of peas, and xl beans, under pain of x shillings to the baron of the land that he dwells in, as often as he be found guilty.’ The baron was bound to do the same planting under penalty of 40 shillings payable to the king. See Hume Brown, Scotland before 1700, p. 23. Cf. vol. i, l. 1063; vol. ii, l. 1028.


CXXXV

<gai>Thug an latha gu dìle<eng>

(‘The day turned to downpour’)

Recorded by J. L. C. from Nan MacKinnon (‘Nan Eachainn Fhionnlaigh’) on Vatersay on 16/5/65. Recently, she sent a tape on which she had recorded this and other songs for Mr. D. D. Campbell, Post Office, Vatersay. The air is transcribed from the earlier recording.

The song is little known; the only other versions of the text we have found is one in the Celtic Review, v. 29 (1907), taken down by Kenneth MacLeod from Ann Henderson in Morvern (30 lines but a different refrain), K. C. C. 121 (23 lines), and another in D. McI. 90, which is printed here as well as Nan MacKinnon’s. The song was overlooked, but found in time to be included in the Appendix here. It will be seen that the K. C. C. and Donald MacIntyre versions have a good deal in common. The story behind the song is unfortunately lost.

1694–5. These lines are not on the 1965 recording.

1697. In a letter to Francis Collinson, Nan MacKinnon explained <gai>tomanan meala<eng> as ‘tufts of earth where the bees have their hives in tufts of tall grass’. Bumble-bees are meant. C. R. and K. C. C. have <gai>croinn mhealadh.<eng>

[TD 294]

1702. The metre shows a line is missing here, possibly the same line as 1720.

1704–5. These lines are not in K. C. C., nor are 1724–5.

1717. K. C. C.: <gai>ris na ballaibh.<eng>

1721. K. C. C.: <gai>’s Mhic ’ic Ailein.<eng>

1722. D. McI.: <gai>Ma a can mi;<eng> K. C. C. <gai>Ach ma chanas mi.<eng>

1728. K. C. C.: <gai>triùsar o’n iarann. Air fhiaradh<eng> means that the cloth was cut so that the pattern went diagonally.

1729. K. C. C.: <gai>a’ ghallain<eng> substituted for D. McI. <gai>na h-ainnir,<eng> which does not make sense in the context.

1730. Hazel-nuts and apples were the ambrosial food of the Gael, see E. C. Quiggin, Prolegomena to the Study of the Later Irish Bards, p. 26.


BARRA VERSIONS OF XXXII (VOL. I, p. 137)

<gai>Có sheinneadh an fhìdeag airgid?<eng>

(‘Who would sound the silver whistle?’)

(Barra and Eriskay versions)

These differ fundamentally from the South Uist versions. The best Barra version was recorded from Mrs. MacDougall (‘Anna Raghnaill Eachainn’) at Northbay, Barra, on 12/3/38, disc I, the first electrical recording ever made in the field in the Outer Hebrides. This, printed here, has 36 lines. Another version, also printed here, was recorded from John MacKinnon, Craigstone, by the Barra Folklore Committee at Castlebay on 27/2/51. The tunes transcribed from these recordings are printed in vol. i of Hebridean Folksongs, pp. 325 and 326.

MS. Barra version, A.J. (2), p. 9, 24 lines, unfinished.

Printed Barra versions: Ella Carmichael, text and translation 28 lines (of which 2 are repetitions), Celtic Review, i. 147; School of Scottish Studies, W.S.B. 7, from Mrs. Buchanan (‘Ceit Ruairi Iain Bhàin’), text, translation, and tune, (27 lines). The versions sung by Mrs. Buchanan and by Mr. John MacKinnon are very closely connected.

Eriskay version: text alone, Fr. Allan MS., fol. 111 (38 lines). Tune, published by Amy Murray in the Celtic Review, ii. 201. In Fr. Allan MS. the text is headed ‘Bean Dhunnchaidh’; presumably this was Mrs. Duncan MacInnes.

Art version: Mrs. Kennedy-Fraser put the words taken down on Barra by Ella Carmichael, to the tune she said she ‘noted in Eriskay from the singing of Peggie MacInnes’ in Songs of the Hebrides, i. 134. The

[TD 295]

tune, and key, are the same as that published by Amy Murray, except that it is given in alla breve instead of 2/4 time.

South Uist versions: that in the MS. of Donald MacCormick is printed in vol. i of Hebridean Folksongs, with translation, no. XXXII, p. 136. It is closely connected with the version taken down on Eriskay by Fr. Allan MacDonald, which, however, contains 8 more lines, printed here. To a Barra singer, the Uist version appears like a series of disconnected sections. There are two versions of the text of the song in Canon MacLean’s papers. One, (vi. 9) which is in pencil and is incomplete, appears to have been taken down from Mrs. MacCuish (Màiri nighean Alasdair), cf. K. C. C. 35. The other, (i. 30) which is of 21 lines, may have been taken down in Barra.

Mr. C. P. Finlayson has drawn our attention to a short version of the song in the Carmichael papers, reference C. W. Collection, 150; the source is there stated to be ‘Donul MacPhi’, and the date of the transcription, 1889. This version contains only 9 lines, but 2 of them are not found in any other version we have seen. See the note to l. 1815. The locality is not stated, but the remark recorded by Carmichael, that ‘the Dall Mór told Donul MacPhie that this song was composed to Prince Charles’, proves that it was Barra, and that ‘Donul MacPhie’ was the Dòmhnall Gobha who gave Carmichael the ballad of the Children of Uisne (a version of doubtful authenticity, which apparently originated in the Stewarts’ 1804 Collection, see p. 562 there) in 1867. See Carmichael, Deirdire (1905), pp. 114–15.

It is a popular tradition that this song was made to Prince Charles Edward Stewart in 1745, but in fact the song cannot be taken as a unity, and all that can be said is that a part of some versions of it (e.g. ll. 1809–14, here) are on a Jacobite theme. Otherwise the fact is that the poem is expressed in seventeenth-century language and contains a number of formulaic passages on subjects which are well known in other waulking songs. Taking them first in the order in which they appear in Mrs. MacDougall’s version, the various subjects of the song are:

A. The son of the King is coming to Scotland on a fantastic ship (‘Has come’ in Canon MacLean’s fair copy version). Various chiefs will welcome him. ‘Who will sound the silver whistle’ is the test of loyalty. This passage occurs in all the versions of the song; except in the South Uist and Eriskay versions mentioned here, it is always the first section; there it is the last.

B. When the King arrives, Clan Donald will make a celebration (a rhyme). This section only in the Barra versions. This includes formulaic lines on the entertainment provided in the household of a great chief.

C. Affection is expressed for a person who is fond of music. (ö rhyme). He is usually unnamed, but the Ella Carmichael and Donald MacCormick versions make it clear that this is MacDonald of Morar (‘Mac ’ic Dhùbhghaill’). Cf. vol. i, l. 1135. The references to music and dancing in this section are also formulaic.

[TD 296]

D. The appearance and hospitality of a ‘Tormod Og’ (Young Norman, a name usually associated with the MacLeods of Dunvegan) are extolled, also in formulaic language. This passage occurs in the versions of Ella Carmichael and Mrs. MacDougall (a or ö rhyme).

E. A skilful hunter is praised, again in formulaic language (à rhyme). This also occurs in Mrs. MacDougall’s version and that taken down by Ella Carmichael.

F. Someone—according to John MacKinnon and to Ceit Ruairi Iain Bhàin’s versions a ‘famous minister’ has been killed, and the poet wishes he could have avenged him (Svarabhakti a rhyme). The reference to the ‘steel helmet’ <gai>(clogada cruadhach)<eng> seems anachronistic here if the song really has to do with the ’45 as a whole.

G. The poetess says she will leave her present place of residence, and go to Harris, where she was reared in better circumstances as a young girl. This again in formulaic language. This section is at the beginning of the Donald MacCormick version, vol. i, ll. 1128–34, and also of the Eriskay version (a rhyme).

Sections in other versions:

H. King George will be sent back home to Hanover, (a rhyme). This section occurs in the versions of John MacKinnon and Ceit Ruairi Iain Bhàin.

I. Eriskay version: formulaic passage on the entertainment in a great chief’s house (a rhyme, ll. 1819–26 here; similar lines occur in the next song in this book).

J. Eriskay version: another formulaic passage on the same subject (ua rhyme, ll. 1827–31).

K. MacCormick and Eriskay versions ‘If ye go to tended orchard’ (vol. i, ll. 1146–50).

L. MacCormick, K. C. C., and Eriskay versions: ‘But if you go to yew forest’ (vol. i, ll. 1140–5).

These two passages are closely associated with, and much more apposite to, the song Craobh an Iubhair (vol. i, no. XXXV). They seem totally out of place here.

1732. Fr. Allan McDonald has an apposite following line, not in the other versions: <gai>’S ga b’e sheinn i, sheinn e garbh i,<eng> ‘Whoever sounded it, sounded it loudly’.

1732, etc. The honorific use of the silver whistle on ships goes back at least to the early sixteenth century, see G. E. Manwaring, ‘The Whistle as a Naval Instrument’, The Mariner’s Mirror, x. 72–6. In 1532 King Henry VIII enacted that all English ships’ masters should wear silver whistles with silver chains. I am obliged to Miss R. P. Prentice, Research Assistant at the National Maritime Museum, for the above reference and information.

1735. i.e. MacLeod of Dunvegan and MacKenzie of Kintail.

1737–40. Cf. ll. 451–3 in this volume.

[TD 297]

1742. This line is an instance of the very rare use of a true passive in Scottish Gaelic. Usually the so-called passive forms are really impersonal.

1743. The version taken down by Ella Carmichael has here:

<gai>’S tu mo run, mo ghradh Mac ’ic Dhu(bh)ghail,
Fear taighe nach faighteadh bruideil,
Fidhleireachd bu rogha ciuil dhuit, etc.<eng>

Cf. l. 1135 in vol. i. This shows that the person referred to in this section is MacDonald of Morar.

1748. ‘Tormod’, anglicized as ‘Norman’, is a name associated with the MacLeods of Dunvegan and their cadet families. It is not possible to identify this Tormod Òg ‘Young Norman’ with any certainty. The best known Norman MacLeod was Sir Norman MacLeod of Bernera, 1610–1705; another Norman succeeded as chief of the MacLeods of Dunvegan in 1693.

1750. This line, from Ella Carmichael’s version in the Celtic Review, is needed to complete the sense.

1752. <gai>Treas-tarraing<eng> was three times distilled whisky, see l. 1431 in vol. ii and note thereon.

1753. The appellation <gai>o Ghallaibh,<eng> which would normally mean ‘from Caithness’, is puzzling. Brandy was not imported into the Highlands and islands via Caithness. One might suspect a corruption of <gai>o Ghaillimh,<eng> ‘from Galway’ (in Ireland). Galway is mentioned in Scottish Gaelic verse as a source of silk (l. 452, here), linen (l. 1242 in vol. i), and wax (Bàrdachd Ghàidhlig l. 6341), in these instances always in the oblique form <gai>Gailmhinn<eng> or <gai>Gailbhinn.<eng> In any case, the pronunciation is different. In the W.S.B. transcription of Ceit Ruairi Iain Bhàin’s version of the song, the word is given as <gai>Cailidh<eng> and translated ‘Calais’. This is not supported by any other version known to us, and seems unlikely. Calais was not a port from which wine and brandy were shipped to the Highlands. In the circumstances, I have translated <gai>o Ghallaibh<eng> by ‘from abroad’.

1754. In Ella Carmichael’s version this line has <gai>cadhan,<eng> ‘wild goose’, with a footnote saying that the singer had lengthened the vowel apparently to make assonance with <gai>gràdh.<eng> This is unlikely. <gai>Càrnan<eng> must be the right word.

1759. Exactly the same line occurs in the song Craobh an Iubhair, vol. i, l. 1244. Passages more appropriately associated with this song are found in the South Uist version of An Fhìdeag Airgid, see vol. i, ll. 1140–50, and ll. 8–11 of K. C. Craig’s version, K. C. C. 35.

[TD 298]

1762. <gai>Mo sgian lìth<eng> in a version corrected by Miss Annie Johnston. But chlì seems clear on the recording involved.

1772–3. Not in A. J. (2), but given by her later.

1779. This is very probably Sir Ewen Cameron of Lochiel, a very well-known Highlander of the seventeenth century. He succeeded as chief of the Camerons in 1647, fought under Dundee at Killiecrankie in 1689, and lived until 1719. His mother was a Campbell, and his grandson who succeeded him, was Donald Cameron, the ‘gentle Lochiel’ of the ’45. His being mentioned in this way in the song is further evidence that the song was not composed in connection with Prince Charles’s coming to Scotland in 1745.

1782–9. Miss Annie Johnston told me she got these lines from Mrs. Cameron (Barra) who died around 1920. Mrs. Cameron was one of the Barra singers who sang for Mrs. Kennedy-Fraser.

At a later date, Miss Johnston added ll. 1743–5 and 1747–8 in her MS. notebook.

1803–8. Formulaic passage, cf. ll. 679–83 of no. LIV in vol. ii.

1815. This line, which is also in Ceit Ruairi Iain Bhàin’s version, is surprising. The subject seems quite unrelated. The steel helmet takes one back to the sixteenth century. ‘Their defensive armour consists of an iron headpiece, and a coat of mail, formed of small iron rings, and frequently reaching to the heels’ (George Buchanan. 1582, Early Descriptions of Scotland, p. 234).

‘Alas’ seems the most appropriate translation of <gai>Mo cheist<eng> in this context; cf. Irish (Dinneen).

The short version of this song in the Carmichael papers referred to contains 2 lines following this one (1815) which are not in any other versions known to us. Light could be thrown on the passage if the persons mentioned could be identified, but this has not been possible. The lines are:

<gai>Mo ghaol am ministeir ainmeil
Cha be mac sin tu Fearachar
Cha be ogha Mairi Simason

Simason<eng> presumably = Jamieson, but there is no such name to be found in the lists of the clergy, Protestant or Catholic, of South Uist or Barra. There were Jamiesons in Canna before the Clearances: there are seven in the 1820 rent-roll.

1819–26, and 1827–31. These are formulaic passages; practically the same lines occur in vol. i, no. V (148–54 and 157–66); see also ll. 1852–64 of the present volume.

[TD 299]

BARRA VERSION OF XXIII (VOL. I, p. 112)

<gai>’S muladach mi ’s mi air m’ aineoil<eng>

(‘Sorrowful am I in a strange country’)

This is the text preserved by Miss Annie Johnston and recorded from her and her brother Calum by the Barra Folklore Committee on 7/8/50, wire no. 441; it is taken from her MS. notebook, p. 86; it differs very considerably from the version in Donald MacCormick’s collection, see vol. i, p. 112. Annie and Calum Johnston’s version of the air is printed there, p. 304. This fine song was also one of the earliest I recorded in Barra on ediphone; this was done from Mrs. MacDougall (‘Anna Raghnaill Eachainn’) in January 1937. Another Barra version (9 lines) was recorded from Mrs. Murdo MacDonald (‘Bean Mhurchaidh a’ Bhealaich’) by John MacLean, M.A., around 1953.

References to sources of Uist versions can be found in the notes to vol. i, p. 180.

1833. Mrs. Murdo MacDonald has this line as <gai>’S éisleineach mi ’s mi buain rainich<eng> on repetition, ‘I am distressed reaping bracken’.

1835. <gai>foinnidh fearail<eng> is the masculine equivalent of <gai>foinnidh fìorghlan,<eng> cf. l. 1633.

1857. Cf. l. 1121 of vol. ii. The whole passage 1855–71 is formulaic, cf. ll. 1803–8 and 1819–27, and notes thereon.

1871. Cf. l. 100 of vol. i, exactly the same line in an entirely different song.

‘Ailein’ could be Allan MacDonald, who succeeded as chief of Clanranald in 1584 and died in 1593 (1590 according to MacVurich). He was the father of Ranald MacDonald of Benbecula, ‘Raghnall mac Ailein ’ic Iain’ who is mentioned in ll. 572 and 697 in vol. i, and l. 903 in vol. ii. The next Clanranald chief called Allan was born about 1660, the son of Donald and his wife Marion MacLeod, succeeded as chief in 1710, and was killed at the battle of Sheriffmuir in 1715.

[TD 300]

ADDITIONAL NOTES TO VOLUMES I AND II

VOLUME I

P. 9, note 2, and p. 18, note 2, for 198 read 199.
P. 19, note 1, for 214 read 216.
P. 225, last line, for ‘transposition’ read ‘transcription’.


I

<gai>Ailein Duinn, o hì, shiùbhlainn leat<eng>

(‘Brown haired Allan, o ho hì, I’d go with thee’)

Two couplets of this song, and the refrain, with the tune transcribed in staff notation and a bass part added, were printed in Scottish Home Industries, p. 25, to illustrate Mrs. Mary Mackellar’s paper on the waulking day, quoted at length from T.G.S.I. xiii. The book is undated, but the copy in the writer’s possession has the year 1895 with a name on the flyleaf. This is the same year that the Gesto Collection was published, where there is another version, contributed by Miss Frances Tolmie.

Another version of the song was recorded in 1952 by Mr. Fred Macaulay from Margaret MacKay, born on the island of Scarp in 1872. This has 23 lines, and is printed, with story, translation and musical transcription, in no. 22 of Tocher, pp. 216–19.

As the story of the song was related in detail in Folklore and Folksongs of South Uist, published in 1955, and reissued in 1977, it was only briefly mentioned in vol. i of Hebridean Folksongs. In May 1976 Mrs. P. Morrison of Leverburgh, Harris, wrote me an interesting letter in which she informed me that the song was not considered a waulking song in Harris, and pointed out that Donald MacCormick’s Caolas Diadhair was a mistake for Caolas Stiadair, and that his version omitted the following line No Loch Mhiabhaig as na Hearadh. This is in the F.F.S.U. version, which runs to 30 lines.

Mrs. Morrison continued ‘I have read elsewhere that the body was never found, but that is wrong, because his body was found on one of the Shiant Isles. Another mistake is that the ship containing her remains the day of the funeral had to pass through the so-called Caolas Diadhair, which is entirely wrong, as the ship did not have to go near it, as the burial place in Rodel was on the opposite side.’

These mistakes were not made in the Revd. A. MacLean Sinclair’s account of the story, which was quoted at length in F.F.S.U., and referred to in the notes on this song in Hebridean Folksongs, vol. i.

Above music (p. 244), for ‘Mrs MacLean’ read ‘Mrs MacCormick’ (also on pp. 278 and 332).

Four lines of this song occur in the MacNicol MS., see T.G.S.I. xxvii. 396.

XXXVI

1283. <gai>Sobhal na slataich<eng> is a barn made with sides of wicker-work. Cf. the ‘Account of a voyage to the Hebrides, by a committee of the British fishery society, in the year 1787’, printed in The Bee in 1792, p. 93: ‘Their [i.e. the Highlanders’] barns are of wicker; into these they carry their corn before it be quite dry; the air finding access prevents the corn from spoiling.’

Last two lines of music, p. 335, for ‘Air’ read ‘Verse’.


XXXVIII

For Mrs. Duncan MacInnes’ (p. 193) read ‘Mrs. Donald MacInnes’ to whom this song should also be attributed on p. 197.


XL

Music, p. 341. Form: for A || BC D C read A B || C D C


VOLUME II

XLI

L. 42. <gai>Geal<eng> is better translated ‘bright’ in such contexts.


XLIV

The Revd. William Matheson has pointed out that according to the Carmichael MSS., this song was ascribed to the daughter of a MacLeod of Dunvegan who had married a MacDonald of Clanranald, but who on a visit to Skye had fallen in with a former lover from whom she much regretted parting for Uist. See T.G.S.I. xli. 20–1. Historically, the person to whom this could apply is Mór, daughter of John MacLeod of Dunvegan, who married Donald MacDonald of Clanranald, known as Dòmhnall na Cuthaige after the nickname of his favourite gun, in 1656. The marriage was unhappy and came to grief with charges of adultery against Mór and counter-charges of cruelty against Donald, whose reputation in the Highlands is traditionally not very good. Mór is said to have

[TD 303]

had a son Alexander MacLeod by Norman (b. around 1663) son of Sir Norman MacLeod of Bernera (1614–1705). (Clanranald Papers). Thomas Pennant recorded in 1770 that the tower at Corradhun in Canna had been built ‘by some jealous regulus to confine a handsome wife in’, and this tradition may have arisen from the matrimonial disputes of Mór and Donald.

See The Book of Dunvegan; the Revd. Charles MacDonald, Moidart, or among the Clanranalds; J. L. Campbell, Stories from South Uist; the Clanranald Papers, etc. In the Book of Dunvegan, Mór’s defence against her husband’s charges is wrongly dated 1689; the correct date is 1680, and the charge then was one of adultery with one Alexander Mac Ronald, which she denied.


XLVI

This song is discussed at length by Professor Derick Thomson in his An Introduction to Gaelic Poetry (1974), and a full translation of the version printed by Alexander Carmichael in vol. v of Carmina Gadelica, edited by Professor Angus Matheson, is given (pp. 75–81).


XLVII

Notes, p. 201. The Revd. William Matheson points out that John Cameron and the Balluchulish man from whom Malcolm MacFarlane took down a version of the air of Chailin òig as stiuramaiche, were one and the same person, and that the writer in the Deò Gréine referred to was MacFarlane.


XLVIII

For ‘Mrs. Geoghegan’ read ‘Miss Mary MacKiggan’ (name given as ‘MacGeoghan’ in Songs of the Hebrides).

451. Read <gai>Cha b’e dhùisgeadh as mo shuain mi<eng> (as translated).


XLIX

Music, p. 290, text under second bar, read <gai>seòlt-a.<eng>


LIII

660. <gai>Mac Eora,<eng> etc. The Revd. W. Matheson points out that the name must be Eòiridh, anglicized in Lewis as ‘Dorothy’.


LIV

678. For <gai>Ciorcabost<eng> read <gai>Circeabost;<eng> but Dr. A. MacLean tells me that he once heard Calum Johnston sing this line as <gai>Eadar Circeadal is Ruadal.<eng> This makes better sense, as these places exist in South Uist, on the south side of Loch Eynort and the south side of Loch Skipport respectively.

[TD 304]

LV

Compare tune no. 12 in Patrick McDonald’s Collection, headed <gai>Ugi nan gu ’smo thriall dhachaidh;<eng> but musically the resemblance is very slight.


LVI

Compare tune no. 5 in Patrick McDonald’s Collection, headed <gai>Si ’nàill so dhuisg mi.<eng> Again, the musical resemblance is very slight. Simon Fraser (1815) referred to the tunes in Patrick McDonald’s Collection as ‘ill selected, and worse communicated’.


LIX

758. <gai>Rubh’ an Dùine<eng> may be for <gai>Rubh’ an Dùnain<eng> in Skye, but <gai>Dùine<eng> is clear on the recording. But the Canna place-name is <gai>Sgor an Duine.<eng>


LX

The massacre in the Eigg cave was also mentioned by Dr. Johnson in A Journey to the Western Islands of Scotland, 1775, p. 154, and by John Leyden in his Journal of a Tour in the Highlands and Western Islands of Scotland in 1800 in a letter written to Sir Walter Scott on 27 August of that year. Leyden said that ‘Having procured candles, we began with the cave of the massacre, the most easterly of those on the south side of the island. The entrance is low, narrow, and irregular, and we were obliged to enter on all-fours. Before we could stand erect we found we were creeping over dead men’s bones. The cave then becomes more spacious and lofty … It extends in length about a hundred paces, and through its whole extent is strewed with bones’ (pp. 155–6).


LXII

See C.G. v. 10–15, with which the version printed by the Revd. A. J. MacVicar appears to be connected.

812. The sound referred to is that between North Uist and the small island of Rona which lies to the south-east of North Uist. The Revd. W. Matheson tells me that in the days of sail it was an important roadstead, known as <gai>Seòlaid Rubha Eubhadh.<eng>


LXIII

Music, p. 314. In note above Variants, for ‘Introduction’ read ‘Introductory’.


LXIV

867. For ‘you you’ read ‘for you’.


LXV

964. for ‘strath’ read ‘stream’. <gai>Struth<eng> is clear in the MS.

[TD 305]

LXVII

Title: for mór read Mór.

Foreword: last line of second paragraph, for ‘they two’ read ‘the two’.

1068. Translation, read ‘Hanging from the Tower by a rope’.

Epilogue, last paragraph but one: it is uncertain whether the reciter said <gai>asad<eng> or <gai>orst;<eng> it sounds more like asad. This implies that by throwing the cogie or wooden bowl with some food in it and hitting her stepdaughter with it, the stepmother had removed the spells the fairy lover had put on her stepdaughter.


LXVIII

1122. <gai>Gachdan air òl.<eng> In an undated letter to me written a considerable number of years ago, in Gaelic, Miss Annie Johnston wrote that it was more than twenty years since she had taken down the words of this song from an old lady long since dead. She had not known the word gachdan at the time, but understood that it meant <gai>slugadh sìos deoch làidir ann an cabhaig,<eng> ‘drinking down strong drink in a hurry’. Perhaps it could have been <gai>glocan air òl.<eng>


LXXIII

The Revd. W. Matheson draws my attention to the fact that there is another line after 1260 on the Linguaphone record of this song, <gai>Bealach a’ Sgail ’s cha n-e fantail,<eng> ‘to put behind us yonder pass, Bealach a’ Sgail, and not to wait’. ‘Bealach a’ Sgail is on the way from Uig in Lewis to North Harris, and is still sometimes used by people doing the journey on foot. It was the regular route in the days before modern means of communication’.


LXXIV

1315, 1344, <gai>luaidh.<eng> Rather ‘If I heard of another girl being mentioned in connection with you’ ‘being matched with you’.

1345. Better <gai>stuaighe,<eng> and translated literally.

1354. For <gai>na<eng> read <gai>nach.<eng>


LXXV

The Revd. W. Matheson tells me that the date of the publication of Finlay Dun’s Orain na’h Albain was 1848.

1362. For <gai>thùs ri<eng> read <gai>thùs thu.<eng>


LXXVII

1430. For <gai>deòir<eng> read <gai>beòir.<eng>

1439. <gai>Hiort nan eun móra.<eng> The expression <gai>Na h-eòin-mhóra<eng> was translated ‘the Great Auks’ by Alexander Carmichael in a St. Kilda song quoted in his ‘Grazing and Agrestic Customs of the Outer Hebrides’ printed in Appendix A of the Report of the Crofters’ Commission, p. 467 (1883).

[TD 306]

When the late Professor James Carmichael Watson edited this poem for Carmina Gadelica, iv. 108, he changed the translation to ‘great birds’. But the ‘great birds’ must have been Great Auks, for which St. Kilda was distinguished by being the only British breeding locality; Martin Martin describes the Great Auk or Gairfowl as ‘the stateliest, as well as the Largest of all the Fowls’ there (Voyage to St. Kilda, 1698, p. 48). By 1758 the Great Auk was only an irregular visitor to St. Kilda, and the last specimens were seen there in the 1820s. None have been seen anywhere since Icelandic fishermen killed the last survivors on the little island of Eldey in 1844.

INDEX OF PERSONS AND PLACES

<gai>‘Ailein’<eng> (g. for nom. in Uist), 238, 1871. used for Clanranald, 516.
